HEADLINE: Big Freeze Blights a Citrus Crop, and the Lives of Workers

BYLINE: By EVELYN NIEVES 

DATELINE: PORTERVILLE, Calif.

 BODY:
When winter threw a fit last Christmas, slamming through the Central Valley and scaring the life out of the citrus groves, people here were sure 1999 was over.
It was. By the time the ice storms backed off four days later, the orange and lemon crops were nearly gone, and so were the picking and packing jobs. 
 The freeze destroyed more than $700 million worth of produce in the Central Valley, the nation's largest food producer, including more than 75 percent of the valley's navel oranges, about 83 percent of its Valencia oranges and virtually all the lemons. The storms left from 14,000 to 28,000 farmworkers out of work.
More than half a year later, with the sunlight pressing on the valley like a hot compress, the damage from the freeze is getting worse. Growers and packing-house owners are up to their chins in debt; workers are up to their eyes in it.
To see the dusty towns in freeze-struck counties like Tulare, which grows many of the nation's navel oranges, one would never know that this nation is richer than ever, a gushing geyser of money and jobs. People stand in long lines for boxes of free Government-issue food -- white cans with big black letters that say pork, peaches and peas. Some people are moving from houses and apartments into decrepit trailers to save on rent. Others have pulled up stakes, packing their children and setting out in pickup trucks to hunt down jobs: two weeks picking cherries in Stockton; two weeks working the strawberry fields in Oregon. Then, maybe, on to Seattle.
Even in good times, the Central Valley's core, from Stockton to Bakersfield, is a vivid reminder that there are swaths of this country where families must literally count their pennies. Unemployment in Tulare County, where the freeze hit hardest, usually hovers around 15 percent, more than 40 percent in some towns. It takes a long time to recover from one fierce display of nature. Unemployment is now more than 20 percent countywide -- 50 percent in some rural areas. Most people do not expect life to return to normal again until the navel orange harvest begins in late fall, or to fully recover for at least another year.
The last freeze struck in 1990, and people say they are still recovering from that one.
"I wanted to leave this time," said Victor Espinoza, who said he had spent 25 years working the lemon and orange orchards of Tulare County. Sitting in his front yard in Porterville, a town the freeze laid flat on its back, he said he was stir-crazy after nearly seven months without work. He has a wife and teen-age daughter to support, and he has bills to pay, same as everybody. "I wanted to go to Stockton or Washington," he said. "But friends said, 'Don't bother. There are too many workers, working too little.' "
Here in Porterville, which has 37,000 residents, thousands and thousands of ruined oranges, their insides like little straw balls, lie strewn all over the groves. Nearly everyone depends on the crops one way or another. If the businesses do not grow, ship, pick or pack, their customers do. So downtown, J. C. Penney, the mutliplex movie theater and the Cookie Jar Cafe are all empty.
Agencies, government and otherwise, have helped. The Federal Emergency Management Agency has handed out $4.6 million in mortgage and rental assistance to more than 2,700 workers. The state Legislature, after lobbying by nonprofit community organizations and the United Farm Workers, temporarily increased the maximum a worker can earn and still receive unemployment benefits from $25 a week to $200 so that workers called for a few hours will not lose more by working than by collecting $150 unemployment checks. The United States Department of Agriculture is providing food to affected workers, as are churches and private food pantries. The state has given some aid, and small numbers of workers have obtained temporary community-service jobs.
But the aid has not reached everyone, or even most people. Most of the out-of-work laborers are immigrants, some legal, some not. "There are some enormous cracks" in the system, said James Gordon, the chief executive officer of Proteus, a nonprofit community organization in Visalia that has a contract from Federal and local governments to provide freeze relief.
For example, he said, men who have not registered for selective service are not eligible for emergency aid, though many are immigrants who arrived in the United States after age 26 or who never knew about the requirement. During the last freeze, changes in welfare laws meant that undocumented workers, who make up an estimated 50 percent of the pickers, could no longer receive emergency aid. Even some people with legal work permits have been too intimidated to apply for help.
Guadalupe Arceo, a 30-year-old mother of three, is one of those. Ms. Arceo, who is separated from her husband, has neither worked nor received any aid this year. When she applied for rental assistance, she said, she was told -- wrongly, relief workers say -- that she might hurt her application for permanent resident status because the Immigration and Naturalization Service would not look kindly on applicants who might be a public burden.
Although eligible, Ms. Arceo has never applied for food stamps or aid for her children, ages 3 to 11. Since December, she has paid the $280 rent for her apartment in a public housing project with loans from relatives. Last month they told her their money had run out. They suggested that she move to New York to get a job to pay her bills.
For years, Ms. Arceo has eked out a life here so that her children could remain in their hometown, Now, she said, leaving is her only choice.
"I'll live with my brother's family in Manhattan through the summer, make enough to pay the rent here and more, and come back when the season starts again in November," she said, plane tickets in hand. "I don't see any other remedy."
Grape workers are also in trouble because they are not eligible for any freeze relief, even though they, too, were affected. Many of their jobs were taken when labor contractors replaced them with laid-off citrus workers willing to work for minimum wage.
Antonio and Melania Gomez, who have spent more than 20 years picking grapes in Tulare County, and who scraped together enough money to buy a little house in Porterville nine years ago, have barely worked three weeks this year. Their bills are stuffed into a letter holder in their living room, months of utility bills, not including the high-interest loans they have taken from acquaintances.
Their bank, they said, told them that it would turn them over to a collection agency if they missed just one mortgage payment, and they were turned down by every agency they asked for help. They cannot receive welfare ("Not that we've ever wanted that!" Mrs. Gomez said) because they own a house. It is a very humble house, in a poor neighborhood, but a house nonetheless. They are raising a 3-year-old grandson, but they receive no aid for him because they are not his legal guardians.
"I beg," Mrs. Gomez said, blushing deep red, as her husband left the room to get Cokes for his guests. "I drive to the nearby towns, stand on the corners and put out my hand. I stand on line for food and I stand on corners for money, because otherwise, we would starve, and we would sleep on the streets."
The grape harvest, which was supposed to begin again the first week of July, has been delayed a week or more by a mid-April cold snap. The Gomezes worry that all the extra laborers will finish the season's work early and they will never catch up on their debts.
"We are so disillusioned that we're putting the house up for sale," Mrs. Gomez said.
Some workers are relatively lucky. Mirna Gonzalez, who earned a reasonable living working in a large packing house, is one of 16 citrus workers now employed as temporary community organizers for the National Farmworkers Service Center. The group offers advice to workers and, with the state Department of Social Services and Foodlink, a nonprofit organization, distributes food boxes in 32 communities. Ms. Gonzalez's husband, also a citrus worker, is one of 8,000 laid-off workers collecting unemployment. "When I visit other families," Mrs. Gonzalez said, "especially those who are not documented and are not getting any help, I think we're very lucky."
The workers who live in the worst conditions are in shed-like cement buildings, 12 men to one house, and in trailer parks where chickens and pigeons roam on the pitted dirt roads.
Ramona Martinez, an undocumented citrus worker, has spent eight years picking oranges while living in a one-bedroom trailer with her husband, three sons and her 68-year-old mother. She said the family was starting to earn enough to move up. But that hope was wiped out by the freeze. She and her husband, who are both trying to become legal workers, pay their $200 rent with money they receive in aid for their American-born children, and they buy food with $150 a month in food stamps.
Mr. Martinez, also an orange picker, has just taken a two-week job as a mechanic's assistant, while her eldest son, who is 11, does odd jobs for tips -- packing groceries or pumping gas or stocking shelves. Then he gives his mother the money.
The boy "keeps saying, 'Let's go back to Mexico, where we could live better,' " Mrs. Martinez said with a laugh. Like many immigrants in Tulare County, the family comes from a dirt-poor town in Michoacan, in central Mexico. She said she tells her son that they are still better off here than there, or just the same when there is a freeze.

HEADLINE: Essay;
Free Pills for Geezers?

BYLINE: By WILLIAM SAFIRE 

DATELINE: WASHINGTON

 BODY:
"Free milk for babies" was the battle cry of social reformers a century ago. Today the bumper sticker of those pressing for inclusion of prescription drugs in Medicare might read "Free Pills for Geezers."
(I'll get mail from members of my age cohort whining about geezer as a demographic slur, but it's no more pejorative than "young whippersnapper.") 
 Just as free milk was good for youngsters, free pills will be good for indigent oldsters. Medication, especially in geriatric and psychiatric treatment, is often better and always cheaper than hospitalization. Insurance coverage of most prescriptions for the aging is an idea whose time has come; the only question is how best to pay for it without more speedily bankrupting Medicare.
Only a few days ago, the White House had its answer: sock the middle class and wealthy in Medicare with a bigger premium to cover the costs of prescriptions for poor people. Through a "means test," Mr. Clinton would be Dr. Robin Hood.
Democrats in Congress popped that trial balloon in a hurry. Raise premiums on the old folks? Political suicide. Republicans agreed, asking why retirees who saved and invested to build a nest egg all their lives should be penalized for their prudence by becoming targets of a tax increase.
After some frantic scrambling, Clinton came up with a fallback plan for announcement tomorrow. How, without a new premium increase, do you add a prescription benefit to Medicare, which already faces insolvency in eight years?
His answer: out of the pocket of the taxpayer. According to Robert Pear, who consistently comes to work earlier than any other reporter in the New York Times Washington bureau, "Medicare would get a huge infusion of cash from the Federal Treasury: $700 billion, representing about 15 percent of Federal budget surpluses anticipated over the next 15 years."
Stipulate that a new benefit will never be taken back. Remember that all official projections of Federal deficits and surpluses for the past 15 years have been wildly mistaken. Then ask: Can we be sure that the business cycle will become an arrow straight upward, that no tax reduction will ever be needed to stimulate a faltering economy -- in essence, that Miss America is Rosy Scenario?
Optimism is in, but to base the solvency of Medicare on the willingness of the next generation of taxpayers to subsidize a planned, ever-increasing tide of red ink is more than a little risky.
Beware reliance on a permanent surplus. Twenty years from now, the children of today's boomers might not want to carry the heavy tax burden to support a generation that bet on the come, irresponsibly refusing to plan ahead to insure itself.
Beware, too, of killing the goose that lays the golden pills. The most troubling two words in the Clinton 80-minute news ramble touched on the prescription drugs to be dispensed under Medicare: they had to be "properly priced."
Is Washington to decide on what price is "proper" for a new drug? It's fine for government to help negotiate volume discounts for purchases, but destructive to snatch the pricing function from the free market.
If Medicare listens to demagogic complaints about greedy profits for the latest pharmaceuticals and dictates the price it will pay, private investment risking its capital in discovering new treatments will dry up. Take it from one who wrote Nixon's speech imposing price controls; they don't work.
How, then, do we make it possible now for a poor Pop to pop a pill? It's not rocket science and requires no universe-wide government takeover of the health care system.
Months ago, the bipartisan Breaux-Thomas Medicare commission came up with a sensible plan of premium support that would help Medicare recipients pay for a health plan of their choosing. It's not "free pills for all," but it's similar to the generous Federal-employee plan, and the better-off can buy more benefits. What's needed now is to add a prescription-drug benefit to the basic benefits package.
Clinton stiffed the commission's proposals: not expensive enough, provided no political issue, and the Clinton name was not on them.
But it's practical and doable this year. If you want prescriptions covered through Medicare, raise your voice, Gramps.

HEADLINE: Where Prison Plans Cast a Pall Unto the Heavens

BYLINE: By The New York Times 

DATELINE: SPRINGFIELD, Vt., June 26

 BODY:
When residents in this economically hard-pressed town vote on Tuesday on whether to allow construction of what will be Vermont's largest prison, the fate of the annual stargazer convention may be decided as well.
The Stellafane Convention for telescope builders is considered to be one of the biggest "star parties" in the nation. Last year's the Stellafane conclave drew over 2,500 enthusiasts to boulder-capped Breezy Hill, just three miles west of the proposed site of a $26 million prison. 
 Fearing that bright lights at the 350-bed correctional institution will cloud their view of the heavens, the Stellafane devotees took action, and by Friday, had given Gov. Howard Dean of Vermont a petition with more than 700 signatures protesting the proposed prison's proximity to the Stellafane observatory, one of only three national historic landmarks in the state.
"This is a beautiful place where the night sky is incredibly transparent," said Maryann Arrien, president of the Springfield Telescope Makers, the group that maintains the two observatories at Stellafane and oversees the convention each year in southeastern Vermont. "You can see stars of the faintest magnitudes plus the dust lanes of the Milky Way. Why ruin it for the sake of 350 criminals?"
A Stellafane stalwart, Brad Vietje, said the annual convention brings more than $1.5 million in revenue to town businesses.
The first observatory named Stellafane, Latin for "shrine to the stars," was built in 1923 when Russell W. Porter, a Springfield native and later one of the designers of the 200-inch-diameter Hale Telescope at Mount Palomar, Calif., constructed a two-story clubhouse for the Springfield Telescope Makers, an informal yet earnest group, on 30 acres his parents had given him.
Years earlier, Mr. Porter and several others working at Jones & Lamson, a local machine-tool company, had been inspired by James Hartness, inventor of the turret lathe and his own tracking telescope, and later president of Jones & Lamson.
In 1926, the Springfield Telescope Makers invited other groups of stargazers to Stellafane to exchange ideas and compare instruments, beginning a tradition. Each summer, telescope builders and astronomers make the pilgrimage to the Stellafane clubhouse and observatory, still painted the light pink that Mr. Porter first used.
At a town meeting last week to discuss the prison plan, state engineer James Richardson contended that light pollution from the prison would brighten the night sky only 1 percent. Several scientists, in letters of protest to Governor Dean and other state officials, noted that even a fraction of the light generated by a prison would severely limit stargazing at Stellafane.
But Vermont needs a new prison because its older ones are full; many of the state's prisoners are sent to cells in New Jersey and Virginia. The prison in Woodstock, in the southeastern part of the state, will soon close its doors, and Governor Dean has strongly supported the construction of a new prison in the same part of the state.
So far, it has been a hard sell.
Windsor residents overwhelmingly voted not to expand the small, 100-bed correctional institution there. Brattleboro voters, followed by their neighbors in Rockingham, both voted against considering a prison.
However, a year and a half ago, voters in Springfield, heartened by the prospect of a much-needed economic lift, gave the town's Select Board the approval to negotiate a deal to bring the prison to town.
Once regarded the center of this country's machine-tool industry, Springfield has suffered years of economic decline. Thousands of skilled machinist jobs were lost over the last 25 years as the big companies in town -- Jones & Lamson, Bryant Grinder and Fellows Gear Shaping -- lost orders to growing competition from abroad.
Patricia Moulton Powden, executive director of the Springfield Regional Development Corporation, said, "This community needs and wants more decent paying jobs." But water and sewage lines need upgrading, at an estimated cost of more than $10 million, before the town can attract new manufacturers.
The state has promised Springfield about $7 million in incentives to accept the prison, including $1 million for water and sewage improvements and $3 million for a new community center. Mrs. Powden, together with Springfield's two state representatives, say they hope the voters accept the proposal.
As for the Stellafane's convention's contribution to the town's economy, which could be lost if light from the prison lights up the heavens, Mrs. Powden said, "The tourism dollars are great, but I'd like to add to that what the state gives us. I have to believe there's a compromise possible with Stellafane.""
A new prison comes with the promise of 160 new jobs, most with a starting pay just under ten dollars an hour, but there is no way of saying how many Springfield residents would be employed there.
"They can't project how much of the money spent on building and servicing the prison will trickle down into the local economy," said Stephen Ellis, a 41-year-old lawyer who moved here from Philadelphia five years ago.
Standing next to Stellafane's 13-inch Schupmann Telescope, Ms. Arrien, 46, a filmmaker who lives in Putnam Valley, N.Y., said, "If the prison gets built, many people will stop coming here."
Mr. Ellis said he and his wife would put their house on the market "two days after the vote" if the prison proposal passed.

HEADLINE: Looking Extremely Moderate

BYLINE: By MIKE ALLEN 

DATELINE: HARTFORD, June 26

 BODY:
While other states debate gun-control laws, Connecticut is about to get a gun-seizure law, intended to disarm dangerous people before they fire. By promising to sign the measure, Gov. John G. Rowland, a Republican, is providing one of the starkest illustrations yet of how far some G.O.P. officials are going in an effort to change their party's face.
Governor Rowland is part of a class of Republicans, personified most prominently by Gov. George W. Bush of Texas, who are challenging old images of the party by playing down ideological bugle calls and pursuing policies that will help win the votes of women and suburbanites. 
 Connecticut's gun bill, the first of its sort in the nation, would allow the police to get a warrant to confiscate the firearms of anyone who "poses a risk of imminent personal injury to himself or herself or to other individuals." The factors to be considered by a judge before issuing the warrant include threats, alcohol abuse and cruelty to animals.
Legal scholars say the bill may violate three or more amendments to the Constitution. Hunters in the state call it the "turn-in-your-neighbor law," arguing that it will leave them ripe for false accusations in custody fights or neighborhood disputes.
Mr. Rowland's signature on the bill will cement his status as one of the most moderate of the nation's prominent Republicans. This is a very different John Rowland from the one who was elected to Congress in 1984 on Ronald Reagan's coattails. Back then, Mr. Rowland goaded Democratic leaders into starting Congressional sessions with the Pledge of Allegiance and bragged in his official biography about his plan to "save $1 billion over three years by rooting out welfare fraud." Campaigning successfully for his first term as governor in 1994, he railed about the cable television and other "Club-Med-like amenities" at a new state prison.
Now, he says his priorities are "the three C's -- cities, children and civility," and his Web site heralds his record on issues like urban revitalization, children's health and environmental protection.
Mr. Rowland, who at 42 is the nation's youngest governor, said that supporting the gun bill was "a no-brainer" after April's shootings in Littleton, Colo., which killed a dozen high-school students. He acknowledged that "civil libertarians will be able to make some good arguments" against the bill, but said he trusted the police and courts to use discretion in applying it.
"I may not understand it or accept it, but the world is changing," Mr. Rowland said in an interview on Friday. "As society changes, laws have to change to protect citizens along the way. Sometimes you have to try new and different and creative ways to solve problems. You have to take some risks."
Other politicians may resist labels. Mr. Rowland, an early endorser of Governor Bush for the Presidency, proudly declares himself "a moderate, centrist, pragmatic, common-sense, compassionate conservative."
He plans to veto only one of the 307 bills sent to him by the Connecticut Legislature during the session that ended this month, even though Democrats control both chambers. Among the bills he has signed is a requirement that health insurers pay for contraception for women, which abortion opponents in Connecticut said was their biggest loss in a decade. When he was in Congress, Mr. Rowland opposed abortion, but he became a supporter of abortion rights before running for governor.
Mr. Rowland's malleability, which has led critics to brand him "the whatever governor," makes him look like a pioneer in a party where many of the up-and-comers are eager to have voters forget the bad old days of 1994, when Republicans were talking about cleaning out the government stables. Mr. Bush is soaring in opinion polls with his sunny talk on the presidential nomination trail of a Republicanism that is "generous of heart" and a nation where "the weak are valued."
Gov. Frank Keating of Oklahoma, the chairman of the Republican Governors Association, said in an interview on Friday that all but 5 or 6 of the nation's 31 G.O.P. governors were moderate conservatives. "We have to bring everyone together -- black, white, red and yellow -- to reach consensus and advance the ball," he said.
Those moderates include Mr. Rowland's next-door neighbors -- George E. Pataki of New York and Christine Todd Whitman of New Jersey. Like Mr. Rowland, Mr. Pataki has proved politically multilingual, avoiding a revolt by party regulars even as he has reached out to independents and Democrats. Mrs. Whitman, nationally prominent for her moderate views on social issues, has had more trouble with conservatives. But she has been increasingly attentive to their concerns since her uncomfortably narrow re-election in 1997, most recently by supporting notification of a parent before a teen-age girl can get an abortion.
Brad Coker, the managing director of Mason-Dixon Polling and Research Inc., in Washington, pointed out that by "moving to the center, getting off ideology and talking about issues that people care about," the nation's Republican governors were copying the strategy that won the White House for President Clinton in 1992.
In contrast to the governors' pragmatism, however, the alpha Republicans in Washington still seem to be playing from old cue cards featuring rigid rhetoric and philosophical purity at the expense of public opinion. Mark D. Pfeifle, a spokesman for the Republican National Committee, contends, "When the dust settles, they all have the same goals and in many cases the same outcomes."
As part of Mr. Rowland's new-look Republicanism, he decided long ago simply to ignore issues that can be red meat in his party, including school vouchers and single-sex marriage. He said he considered debates on those "no-win issues" to be a waste of time that would change nobody's mind. During this year's Gay Pride Month, for example, he refused to sign the proclamation that local organizers wanted, but did not prevent them from flying their rainbow-striped flag over the State Capitol.
Sometimes his attention span seems to be as short as that of the voters. This spring, he squeezed legislators to insure passage of financing for a stadium and convention center for downtown Hartford, as salve for the loss suffered by the city when the New England Patriots football team decided not to move here. But legislators never took up a plan he offered in a budget address in February for tax credits to help families pay for higher education, and he has not pushed it further.
Although moderation is very much of the moment, Governor Rowland's critics argue that his most recent stances amount to proof of what they had long suspected -- that his views have no fixed address.
"Ideology is not important to him, nor particularly are ideas," said State Senator Kevin B. Sullivan, the Senate's highest-ranking Democrat. "His role and his power and his position are, so he takes the path of least resistance."
The gun-seizure measure, which is part of a broader firearm-safety bill, is likely to win Mr. Rowland favorable publicity when he signs it in the coming week, but may prove to be a headache in the long run. Steven B. Duke, a Yale Law School professor who specializes in criminal law, said, "An anonymous caller could tell the police, 'Joe Blotz has a gun and is making weird noises,' and that would be enough for them to act."
Laurence H. Tribe of Harvard Law School also sees serious constitutional problems with the bill. "Encouraging a society of whispers and rumors," he said, "hardly seems conducive to a society where people feel safe and secure."
None of the criticism fazes Mr. Rowland, who is basking in Connecticut Republicans' speculation that his longtime support of Mr. Bush will win him a shot at the vice presidential nomination, or perhaps an eventual Cabinet post. (The Connecticut cognoscenti have settled, somehow, on Secretary of Housing and Urban Development.)
Governor Rowland was referring to the gun bill, but he could have been talking about his own stands, when he said serenely, "This is a product that can be refined along the way."

HEADLINE: Shalala Plays Down Cost Of Clinton's Medicare Plan

BYLINE: By ROBERT PEAR 

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, June 27

 BODY:
Donna E. Shalala, the Secretary of Health and Human Services, moved today to head off criticism of the cost of President Clinton's plan to help buy prescription drugs for older Americans, saying that the new Medicare benefits "will not be as generous as people think."
"This isn't about just adding some new lush benefit," Dr. Shalala said on the CBS News program "Face the Nation." "This is critical to health care." 
 More than a third of the 39 million Medicare beneficiaries have no insurance to cover prescription drugs. On Tuesday, Mr. Clinton will unveil a plan for the Government to offer subsidized drug coverage to all beneficiaries. The plan depends in its entirety on approval by Congress, but it is impossible to predict with any certainty what Congress will do.
Administration officials said the premium for drug coverage would start in the range of $20 to $25 a month, then climb gradually in subsequent years toward $45 a month. Under the plan, the Government would pay about half the cost of prescription drugs, up to a certain level to be specified by law, and beneficiaries would pay the rest.
As of noon today, an Administration official said, the maximum annual benefit -- the amount payable by the Government -- had not been decided. But like the premium, it could rise over time, the official said.
Half of Medicare beneficiaries without any drug coverage now spend more than $40 a month of their own money for prescriptions.
Comments by Mr. Clinton and his aides suggest that the Administration is open to a deal with Congress: Democrats would win Medicare coverage of prescription drugs, and Republicans would get some of the tax cuts they want.
John D. Podesta, the White House chief of staff, met last week with Representative Bill Archer, the Texas Republican who is chairman of the Ways and Means Committee, where tax bills usually originate.
Aides to Mr. Archer said he tried to keep the meeting secret because he feared it would feed the suspicions of liberal House Democrats who worry that Mr. Clinton will cut a deal with Republicans in Congress.
Dr. Shalala said the cost of Medicare drug coverage would not "spin out of control," but she refused to say how it would be financed.
Gene Sperling, the White House economic adviser, said the cost to taxpayers would be manageable. "The reason why it is going to cost very little is because the plan overall is going to include significant savings in the Medicare plan," he said on CNN's "Late Edition."
Administration officials said Mr. Clinton's proposal would save large amounts of money by extending provisions of the Balanced Budget Act of 1997, which cut Medicare payments to most health care providers.
That idea is sure to meet fierce opposition from doctors, hospitals, nursing homes and home health care agencies, which are lobbying Congress to restore money cut in 1997.
In a letter last week to Republican lawmakers, Representative Bill Thomas of California, one of the party's leading experts on health policy, suggested questions that should be raised about Mr. Clinton's plan. For example, Mr. Thomas asked: "Is it targeted to individuals that need coverage? Does it give a benefit to those who already have coverage, possibly jeopardizing the munificent benefits they already receive? Would the benefit be available to retired millionaires who do not need assistance?"
Under Mr. Clinton's plan, insurance for drug expenses would be offered to all Medicare beneficiaries, including those who now have such coverage from former employers. A lobbyist for a large labor union said: "A lot of employers may decide they're better off putting their people into the Medicare drug program. Employers could pick up drug costs that Medicare does not cover, and they might pay the premium for Medicare drug benefits too."
Edward A. Kaplan, vice president of the Segal Company, an employee benefits consulting concern in New York City, said, "Anything the Government does to pick up prescription drug expenses under Medicare could provide a windfall for private employers, unions and state and local governments that offer retiree health coverage."
If Medicare covers prescription drugs, Mr. Kaplan said, employers might curtail retiree health benefits or might try to control rapidly rising costs by giving retirees a lump sum of cash, which the retirees could use to buy private health insurance.

HEADLINE: CLINTON TO UNVEIL PLAN TO ALLOCATE BUDGET WINDFALL

BYLINE: By RICHARD W. STEVENSON with ADAM CLYMER 

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, June 27

 BODY:
Seeking to capitalize on new projections showing that the Federal budget surplus will grow far bigger than anticipated, President Clinton plans to unveil sweeping new proposals on Monday to allocate the windfall among Social Security, Medicare and other spending and tax cut proposals, Democrats said today.
Mr. Clinton's plan is sure to intensify his long-running battle with Republicans in Congress over the nation's priorities as it enters a new era in which the two parties must divide up pots of money far larger than anything they could have imagined even a year or two ago. 
 In putting out a proposal on Monday, Mr. Clinton is trying to get a step ahead of the Republican leadership, which will have a new set of surplus estimates available from the Congressional Budget Office in the middle of the week.
Democrats said new projections by the White House's Office of Management and Budget showed that the total budget surplus would be $179 billion higher over the next 5 years, and $517 billion higher over 10 years, than its last estimates, in January. Both the portion of the surplus that comes from excess Social Security payroll taxes and the portion from general tax revenues and lower-than-expected spending are considerably higher than earlier projections, they said.
The White House wants to use much of the money on revised approaches to shoring up the two big entitlement programs, Social Security and Medicare, both of which will face serious financial problems in coming decades.
Under his new plan, Mr. Clinton will say that Social Security can be put on sound footing until 2053, nearly two decades beyond the point it will run into trouble if nothing is done. And he will call for Medicare to get enough new financing to keep it solvent until 2027, an improvement of about a dozen years beyond current predictions.
Democrats said that with so much extra money now on the table -- the new projections show about $1 trillion available over the next decade even without including excess Social Security revenues that have swelled surplus figures in recent years -- Mr. Clinton will open the door to a sizable tax cut.
Until now Mr. Clinton had insisted that he would agree to a tax cut only after Social Security's finances had been made stable for at least 75 years, the actuarial benchmark for the retirement system's solvency. His new approach implies that he would be willing to consider a tax cut even if Social Security's solvency is not guaranteed for that long.
Still, Democrats said Mr. Clinton intended to demand that Republicans accede to his approach to Social Security and Medicare before considering tax cuts or allowing any substantial increase in spending.
Republicans are already calling for a tax cut of $775 billion over the next decade, and the new surplus figures are certain to make them more determined to return as much money as possible to taxpayers.
But there is considerable pent-up demand for additional spending within both parties, for purposes as varied as the military, transportation, education, child care and health.
Mr. Clinton, however, will not call for a removal of the tight spending limits imposed on Congress by the bipartisan 1997 balanced budget agreement, Democrats said.
Mr. Clinton also wants his main tax-cut proposal, to provide incentives for retirement savings to low- and middle-income families, to get priority over Republican calls for tax-rate reductions, reduced capital gains taxes, cuts in the estate tax and other tax cuts at the top of the Republican wish list.
White House officials declined to provide details of the budget plan, which Mr. Clinton is scheduled to unveil early on Monday before leaving on a fund-raising trip. Mr. Clinton is scheduled to unveil a separate proposal on Tuesday for overhauling Medicare, the health insurance system for the elderly.
But people who had been briefed on the White House plan said Mr. Clinton would propose that starting in 2011, general tax revenues be diverted to Social Security to help shore up the system as the baby boom generation begins retiring in earnest.
So robust is the expected flow of revenue into the Treasury under the new White House proposals that by 2015 the Government will have paid off the entire $3 trillion in national debt held by the public, they said.
Mr. Clinton's new plan includes a revised version of a proposal that the two parties have been jockeying over in Congress for several months: a "lock box" to insure that the excess Social Security revenues are not spent, but used only to reduce the national debt or to insure the retirement system's long-term viability.
In his original budget proposal in January, Mr. Clinton proposed reserving 62 percent of the budget surplus over 15 years for Social Security, but in a way that would allow him to spend a portion of the Social Security surplus in the next few years. Both parties have rejected that approach, and although they differ on details, have basically endorsed setting aside every dollar of each year's Social Security surplus.
White House officials said their new plan would respond to the Congressional criticism, and would also include a provision that would link reducing the national debt to improving Social Security's long-term financial condition.
The Administration's original effort to do that in its January budget was built around a complex proposal under which the benefits of reducing the national debt would have been funneled to Social Security in the form of new Government bonds, an approach that Republicans rejected.
Administration officials said the new plan would try to reach the same end by different means.
"We are very hopeful that the new Presidential proposal, that new Social Security lock box, will be a way of bringing together both Democrats and Republicans," Gene Sperling, the director of the White House's National Economic Council, told CNN's "Late Edition" today.
In part because of Mr. Clinton's "Social Security first" strategy, the degree to which the two parties can agree on tax and spending priorities seems largely to depend on whether they can make enough progress on shoring up the retirement system to give them political cover for using the surplus for other purposes.
There is also some question whether either party -- especially the Democratic -- wants a comprehensive deal this year encompassing Social Security and Medicare, tax cuts and additional spending. Members of both parties see all of those as issues that can play well for them with their core constituencies on the campaign trail, so the grease of a fiscal windfall will still have to overcome the political incentive for gridlock.
Still, with a legacy-minded President and a Congress seeking to avoid the do-nothing label, anything is possible.
With Republican efforts to pass spending bills in danger because of the tight spending caps -- and with Mr. Clinton likely to veto the $775 billion, 10-year tax cut they have in mind -- party leaders have increasingly been banking on a budget windfall to help avoid gridlock. Speaker J. Dennis Hastert said last week that he expected to promptly use any non-Social Security surpluses that becomes available.
But there are several different sets of ideas on what to do with any windfall. Congressional Republicans will say it underlines the good sense of tax cuts. Mr. Clinton indicated on Friday that he would use the money as a way to help deal with both Medicare and Social Security.
But House Democrats openly, and many Republicans quietly, see new good news on the budget as the only way out of a spending box they built in 1997. Then, they set tight caps on the amount of money Congress could spend on the military and on domestic programs other than Social Security, Medicare, welfare and other so-called entitlement programs.
And unlike agreeing on tax cuts or Medicare and Social Security legislation, passing annual spending bills is something Congress really has to do; it is Mr. Hastert's first priority. Right now the process is in a shambles, with only the smallest bills coming to the House floor and the Senate considering none of them at all.
But the underlying problem is that the limits agreed to in 1997, as some critics said at the time, are too low to maintain Federal programs at their current levels, making no additions except to adjust for inflation.

HEADLINE: Dinner for a Presidential Library, Contributions Welcome

BYLINE: By DON VAN NATTA Jr. 

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, June 27

 BODY:
Quietly, at a French restaurant on the East Side of midtown Manhattan on Monday evening, President Clinton, the most effective fund-raiser ever to occupy the White House, plans to embark on the most ambitious fund-raising campaign of his long political career.
After having dinner with 40 executives, mostly from the fashion industry, the President is expected to talk about his vision for the William Jefferson Clinton Presidential Library, a museum and public policy center on the banks of the Arkansas River in Little Rock that will be dedicated in part to the preservation of the 42d President's legacy. 
 But Mr. Clinton will not be likely to dwell on the library's price tag: $125 million.
Indeed, by law the President cannot solicit contributions for the cause. None of the guests at Monday night's dinner at La Grenouille have paid any money to attend, and the event was not listed as a library fund-raiser on Mr. Clinton's official schedule. Instead, the entry for the dinner simply said: "private event."
Though $125 million exceeds the total combined amount given to the Clinton-Gore campaigns in 1992 and 1996, the President's supporters say the task of raising that amount for the library is hardly as daunting as it seems.
Unburdened by the limits on individual contributions to Presidential candidates and legal defense funds, the William J. Clinton Presidential Foundation can accept unlimited contributions for the library from individuals, corporations, foundations and even foreign governments. Each donation is tax-deductible.
Thus, for the first time, the President's most supportive, wealthiest friends will have no restrictions placed on their generosity, and supporters believe some will give millions of dollars to the foundation.
Supporters of Mr. Clinton said the foundation had already received generous pledges from several of Mr. Clinton's Hollywood friends, most notably the Dreamworks moguls Steven Spielberg and David Geffen, and Ronald W. Burkle, the California supermarket magnate. This fall, supporters said, a library dinner will be held in Hollywood, possibly at Mr. Geffen's house.
Neither Mr. Spielberg nor Mr. Geffen agreed to comment for this article. A spokesman for Mr. Burkle also declined to comment.
Not surprisingly, Mr. Clinton is taking an intense interest in the library. He ruminated for many months over the proper location. And, after many more months of reviewing dozens of blueprints, the President has still not chosen an architect, despite widespread speculation in the field. Mr. Clinton is taking his time because he "wants the design to be absolutely perfect," one friend said.
"I want it to be great, and I want it to be a great gift to my state," Mr. Clinton said at a news conference on Friday. "I've worked quite hard on it and thought a lot about it, and I think that -- I think the people at home will be quite excited about it when they see what we're going to do, and will be thrilled by it."
Despite the President's enthusiasism, the library project is sensitive enough at the White House that most of those involved in the planning and fund-raising for the library refused to talk about it.
Although these are not expressly political contributions, some critics of the system of campaign financing say it is improper to raise such large amounts of money while still in office.
Mr. Clinton would not be the first sitting President to raise money for a library, but the Administration is sensitive because it has been criticized for its fund-raising tactics in political campaigns, like allowing favored contributors to stay in the White House's Lincoln Bedroom.
Terence McAuliffe, a close friend of Mr. Clinton who raised $27 million for the Clinton-Gore campaign in just seven months in 1995, is leading the library foundation's fund-raising effort.
Besides the library fund, Mr. McAuliffe is helping Mr. Clinton and his wife, Hillary Rodham Clinton, raise money to retire their nearly $10 million debt to their lawyers.
Mr. McAuliffe has also agreed to help Mrs. Clinton if she chooses to seek the New York Senate seat being vacated by Senator Daniel Patrick Moynihan next year.
Supporters of the library project said fund-raisers had spoken about the library foundation's needs with friends and admirers of the President, some of whom have already made general pledges to help.
But Skip Rutherford, a friend of the President who is the local coordinator for the library project in Little Rock, said there had not yet been any financial commitments from anyone.
Still, Mr. Rutherford and other supporters said they were confident of raising the $125 million needed for the library and its endowment. They brushed aside any suggestion that the President's impeachment last December would slow the flow of money to the project.
Mr. Clinton's library will sit on 17 acres on a site now strewn with weeds and aging warehouses near Little Rock's River Market. The site is within walking distance from the Old State House, where Mr. Clinton served as Governor and announced his intention to run for President.
There are 11 Presidential libraries in the United States. The first was for Theodore Roosevelt, the 26th President. The earliest President to have his own library is Rutherford B. Hayes, the 19th President, whose library was built in 1916 in Fremont, Ohio.
Mr. Clinton's planned library would be the most expensive ever built, followed by the George Bush Presidential Library and Museum in College Station, Tex., which cost $83 million and was dedicated in 1997.
Each Presidential library was built entirely with private donations, but nonpartisan groups have criticized Presidents who chose to raise money while still in office, as President Reagan did in the last two years of his second term for his library in Simi Valley, Calif.
"If a donor is giving millions of dollars to the President to enhance his immortality, and doing it while the chief executive is still in the Oval Office, the amount of power that donor has just acquired is off the charts," said Charles Lewis, the founder and executive director of the Center for Public Integrity, a nonprofit watchdog group.
Mr. Lewis said one of Mr. Clinton's most enduring legacies would be that he was a politician who was "not only adept at fund-raising, but had a cast-iron stomach for doing it."
"He's a little bit like the battery bunny," Mr. Lewis said. "He just keeps on raising money."
Presidents Nixon, Ford, Carter and Bush did not begin raising money for their libraries until after they left office, but all four left the White House earlier than they had intended. Mr. Nixon struggled to raise money for his library, partly because his solicitors began seeking donations a decade after he resigned. One of his supporters said in 1990 that it was hard to get money from contributors who were "not basking in the light of a sitting President."
Ronald Reagan, the most recent two-term President, used the allure of his Presidency to raise millions for his library before he left office in January 1989. Mr. Reagan also began earlier than Mr. Clinton has begun. Mr. Clinton's supporters said they hoped to imitate the success that Mr. Reagan had in generating contributions while in office, though they worry privately that they should have started even earlier.
"As two-term Presidents, both President Clinton and President Reagan have that luxury to get a good start on their libraries," Mr. Rutherford said. "President Reagan had a series of events -- dinners, receptions -- where people discussed the library."
Mr. Rutherford said Mr. Clinton had spent a lot of time speaking with people about his hopes for the library. "Quite frankly, lots of people have just gone to Washington on a family vacation and see him or go by the White House to visit, and the library usually comes up," Mr. Rutherford said. "It's just on his mind."
The host of the private dinner in Manhattan on Monday night is Arnold Simon, chief executive of Aris Industries. Forty fashion industry leaders are expected to attend, including the designers Vera Wang and Nicole Miller, Mr. Simon said.
"I love the President," Mr. Simon said. "You very rarely run across politicians with a real heart. I just think he has done wonderful things for the country, and I've benefited from them personally. And this is my way to try to help."
Mr. Simon, who was the chairman of Designer Holdings, which made Calvin Klein jeans, said he hoped that the dinner would encourage his colleagues to contribute to the library.
Mr. Simon said he intended to call the dinner guests in the weeks to come and ask each of them to write a check payable to the Clinton library foundation.
"The President," Mr. Simon said, "has done enormous amounts for the country in respect to unemployment, the stock market and interest rates."

Allies Revising Assessment Of Damage to Serb Forces
In the two weeks since NATO forces arrived in Kosovo, alliance officials have had to scale back their initial estimates of the damage inflicted by the 78-day air campaign on the Yugoslav Army, which they concede remains a force capable of maintaining President Slobodan Milosevic's hold on power. David Trimble, the First Minister of the Northern Ireland Assembly, said the stalled peace effort in Ulster could be saved by a simple statement from Sinn Fein, the political wing of the Irish Republican Army, that the I.R.A. is committed to disarming by May 2000. He did not mention the position he has repeatedly expressed that the I.R.A. must start actual disarmament before Sinn Fein will be allowed to take ministerial posts in the Assembly. Some politicians and analysts said this was a sign that Mr. Trimble was getting ready to compromise. A9
 
Perils for Russia's Farms
A blisteringly hot summer and the reverberations from Russia's economic crisis have taken a devastating toll on the nation's crops, making it likely that Moscow will appeal for American food aid, according to Russian and American officials. A10
 
New Movement in Indonesia
Hundreds of nongovernmental organizations have begun to flex their muscles now that President Suharto is gone, pushing agendas that include human rights, environmental protection, workers' protection, legal aid and political freedoms, as well as rights for the poor. All these groups are part of a transformation of Indonesia that is not quite a revolution, but a realignment of the patterns of power, in which the people above now have to listen to what the people below are saying. HEADLINE: CRISIS IN THE BALKANS: THE TOLL;
Damage to Serb Military Less Than Expected

BYLINE: By STEVEN LEE MYERS 

DATELINE: DJAKOVICA, Kosovo, June 27

 BODY:
NATO warplanes demolished the Yugoslav Army's World War II-era base here. In what was left of the motor pool, 18 military vehicles, including one armored personnel carrier, were scattered today in charred, twisted heaps.
Up close, however, it was clear that most of the destroyed vehicles were old wrecks, many already up on blocks and gathered there by the Serbs for repairs or junking. NATO's warplanes had not destroyed Yugoslavia's front-line fighting vehicles, but rather a junkyard. 
 In Djakovica and all across Kosovo, the devastating results of NATO's 78 days of bombing are obvious, from blown-out military barracks to exploded fuel tanks. What is less clear is the damage done to the Yugoslav Army itself, particularly the tanks, other armored fighting vehicles and artillery batteries.
In the two weeks since NATO forces arrived in Kosovo, alliance officials have scaled back their initial estimates of the damage inflicted on the Yugoslav Army and the special police, which they concede remain capable of maintaining President Slobodan Milosevic's hold on power.
While NATO and Pentagon officials stand by their claims to have significantly damaged those forces, they acknowledge that the units that withdrew from Kosovo a week ago were clearly not as hobbled as they had believed.
And those forces, they say, could be used by Mr. Milosevic again, at least to squelch domestic disturbances in Serbia, and at most to either foment or face down a separatist threat from Yugoslavia's lesser republic, Montenegro.
"It's exaggerated," a former senior allied official, who spoke to top European leaders in recent days, said of NATO's damage estimates. "NATO hit a lot of dummy and deception targets. It's an old Soviet ploy. Officials in Europe are very subdued. No one's pounding their chest over this."
Even in Junik and the villages around Mount Pastrik in southern Kosovo, where allied bombers staged intense attacks on Yugoslav forces that had massed against the Kosovo Liberation Army in the last weeks of the air war, there are few signs of the scorched carcasses of tanks or other military equipment NATO officials had expected to find.
"Nothing is here," Lieut. Col. Dietmar Jeserick, a spokesman for the German peacekeeping troops based in Prizren, said today.
"We found positions," the colonel said. "We found bomb damage in those positions, but we didn't find any vehicles or tanks."
While some vehicles were removed from Kosovo by Serbian forces to break down for spare parts, NATO officials themselves acknowledge that fewer pieces of military hardware were destroyed than the alliance had first estimated.
For instance, NATO's Supreme Commander, Gen. Wesley K. Clark, said last week that the alliance had destroyed 110 of the roughly 300 tanks the Yugoslav Army had poured into Kosovo. But that number was smaller than the 150 tanks NATO believed that it had destroyed in the waning days of the war, a senior NATO official said.
General Clark and other NATO and Pentagon officials emphasize, however, that NATO's bombing ultimately accomplished its mission, which was to force Mr. Milosevic to end his brutal campaign against Kosovo's ethnic Albanians and turn the province over to a NATO-led peacekeeping force.
"From our perspective, we're satisfied we destroyed enough stuff to get him to say uncle," Capt. Stephan R. Pietropaoli of the Navy, the spokesman for the Pentagon's Joint Chiefs of Staff, said in a telephone interview.
But whether it was indeed the pain inflicted on his military that formed Mr. Milosevic's prime motivation for ending the standoff over Kosovo is an open question.
A senior NATO official said many leaders at NATO believed that it was not the damage to the Yugoslav forces that finally forced Mr. Milosevic to relent, though the alliance intercepted communications from Serbian commanders saying their troops were suffering heavy losses around Mount Pastrik.
Instead, the official said, Mr. Milosevic relented when Russia, long a supporter, came to accept NATO's conditions and when the consensus for considering a ground campaign to oust his forces from Kosovo appeared to grow. Other factors were at work, too, including the damage the NATO attacks were causing to Yugoslavia's civilian infrastructure and economy as a whole.
Such an assessment may gain greater currency as NATO takes full stock of the size of the Yugoslav Army that completed its withdrawal from Kosovo a week ago -- tired and battered, to be sure, but defiant, orderly and clearly not as weak as NATO officials believed.
As the Serbs pulled out over 11 days, NATO commanders counted 220 tanks, 300 armored personnel carriers and 308 artillery batteries, along with hundreds of other vehicles and all manner of military equipment loaded on trucks, according to NATO officials here and in Brussels.
There were also 47,000 soldiers and policemen who left the province, several thousand more than intelligence reports showed during the height of Mr. Milosevic's campaign. The officials attributed the discrepancy to paramilitary units, whose movements were harder to track. But the large numbers appeared to undercut NATO's claims to have killed 5,000 to 10,000 troops.
Mr. Milosevic's Government, eager to obscure the fact that it lost a province considered to be Serbia's cultural, religious and historical birthplace, has used state-run television to show troops returning triumphantly from Kosovo. The Government also claimed that only 576 soldiers and policemen had been killed.
In remarks to reporters while visiting Kosovo last week, General Clark dismissed questions about the extent of the bomb damage and ridiculed Mr. Milosevic's claims as absurd. "Just because the air campaign stopped doesn't mean the Serb propaganda campaign stopped," he said.
But he added that a complete picture would not be known until NATO sends analysts here in the coming weeks to assess the air strikes.
The Pentagon has announced that the Deputy Secretary of Defense, John J. Hamre, and the Vice Chairman of the Joint Chiefs of Staff, Gen. Joseph W. Ralston, would lead a thorough review of the war, including the damage done to the Yugoslav Army.
In war, initial assessments of damage are often overblown; the oratory almost always is. After the Persian Gulf war ended in 1991, the Pentagon learned that the American-led coalition had not hurt the Iraqi Army nearly as much as first thought. The bombing and ground invasion left it badly beaten, but still strong enough to put down rebellions in northern and southern Iraq and threaten Kuwait again only three years later.
In Kosovo, there are several explanations for the scaling back the damage assessments now, officials said. Careful reviews of cockpit video footage showed that pilots in some cases mistakenly believed that they had destroyed a target. They also showed that some of the targets hit were not tanks or artillery batteries, but rather clever decoys made by the Serbs to fool pilots flying three or four miles up in the sky.
Outside its armed forces, however, Yugoslavia as a whole clearly suffered enormous damage, particularly to its roads, bridges and industry after 11 weeks of increasingly intense bombing.
According to a Pentagon assessment compiled after Mr. Milosevic agreed to NATO's demands, allied warplanes destroyed Yugoslavia's oil-refining ability, nearly half of its military fuel storage installations and many of its factories producing military equipment and ammunition. The alliance also reported eviscerating Yugoslavia's air force of its most sohpisticated jets and inflicting some heavy damage on Yugoslav ground forces in Kosovo.
Maj. Gen. Charles Wald, a senior analyst on the Joint Chiefs of Staff, said 60 percent of the barracks and other installations of Yugoslavia's Third Army in Kosovo had been either damaged or destroyed. Not a single building at the army base here in Djakovica was habitable, and many were leveled entirely.
But it is also clear crisscrossing the scenes of the heaviest attacks that the Serbs took advantage of the restraints that kept allied pilots at high altitudes and under orders to avoid civilian casualties. Up and down western Kosovo, there are revetments where Serbian tanks and artillery pieces hunkered down, well hidden, particularly at the foot of Mount Pastrik.
There are also scores of decoys. Some of them involve older vehicles fashioned to look like tanks. Others are comical. A few miles west of Pristina, there is still a "bridge" of logs and black tarp 100 yards from the main road. On either end were two "tanks" made of wood and plastic sheeting, with long tubes protruding toward the sky.
On the ground, the decoys would fool no one, and NATO officials said pilots had quickly learned to distinguish them, but a fake bridge near Djakovica took a missile right through its center span. At least one tank rests in a charred heap on a hill at a military base near Urosevac, and local Albanians said several remained in the hills above Junik, an area inaccessible because of land mines.
A bigger mystery is what happened to the tanks and other heavy equipment NATO did destroy. The senior NATO official said the retreating Serbs had taken a lot of damaged tanks with them, ferrying them back on flatbed trucks to use for spare parts.
"They took out every last scrap they could," he said, "so when they get back to Yugoslavia, they can cannibalize bits and pieces to put things back together."

HEADLINE: Point, Counterpoint in the Microsoft Trial

BYLINE: By STEVE LOHR 

 BODY:
The last of 26 witnesses stepped from the stand last week in the Microsoft antitrust trial. They were the human voices in a case brimming with other evidence -- videotapes and transcripts from hundreds of people, and thousands of E-mail and other documents running to more than two million pages.
The Justice Department and 19 states suing Microsoft say that their witnesses have shown the big software maker to be an overreaching monopolist, wielding its market power to thwart competition. Microsoft, the Government says, prodded industry partners and rivals to favor its products over competing software. The Government adds that the company bundled its Internet browser to its industry-standard Windows operating system and gave the browser away as part of a predatory scheme that unfairly hurt rivals, posing a long-term threat to consumer welfare. 
 Microsoft says its witnesses have shown that the company is a tough but legal competitor. Its business activities, Microsoft says, have fostered innovation and spurred growth in the computer industry, to the benefit of the economy and consumers. Where the Government alleges illegal arm-twisting, Microsoft sees hard bargaining and commonplace business deals. Microsoft says it put browser technology into Windows to improve its flagship product, a user convenience that should not be deemed an antitrust violation.
 The two sides have held occasional settlement talks dating back to May 1998, just before the suit was filed. They may yet settle. But if not, Judge Thomas Penfield Jackson will rule, possibly by the end of the year, based largely on his reading of the witness testimony. Below are summaries of what each said. STEVE LOHR
 
http://www.nytimes.com

GRAPHIC: Photos: Government Witnesses -- James L. Barksdale; William H. Harris; Garry Norris; David J. Farber. Microsoft Witnesses -- Paul Maritz; James Allchin; Richard L. Schmalensee; Gordon E. Eubanks
 
Chart:
 
ON USE OF MARKET POWER: Government Witnesses
 
James L. Barksdale; President, Netscape
Said Microsoft offered to divide the browser market; when Netscape refused, Microsoft withheld technology and struck exclusionary deals with PC makers and Internet service providers to hobble Netscape.
 
David M. Colburn; Senior Vice President, America Online
Said Microsoft made his company an offer it could not refuse: a featured place on the Windows desktop -- the most valuable real estate in cyberspace -- in return for making Microsoft's browser the default choice for its millions of subscribers.
 
Avadis Tevanian; Senior Vice President, Apple Computer
Said Microsoft tried to get Apple to stop distributing its Quicktime multimedia software. He also said Microsoft threatened to stop developing productivity software for the Macintosh unless Apple favored Microsoft's browser.
 
Steven McGeady; Vice President, Intel
Said Microsoft threatened to strongly support Intel's rivals unless the chip maker's software arm stopped developing multimedia and Internet software.
 
James A. Gosling; Vice President, Sun Microsystems
Said Microsoft tried to subvert the technology threat posed by Java, an Internet programming language, by co-opting it. Sun, he said, felt that "when Microsoft was holding out its hand, there was a knife in that hand."
 
William H. Harris; President, Intuit
Warned that Microsoft's market power could enable it to gain control over "major areas" of Internet commerce. He said that to obtain prime placement on the Windows desktop for its on-line personal finance software, Intuit agreed to bundle Microsoft's browser and not Netscape's.
 
John Soyring; Software Executive, I.B.M.
Explained how difficult it was for his company's OS/2 operating system to make headway against Windows because of the "applications barrier to entry." To make a word processing program for OS/2, one would have to "re-create much of the application from scratch."
 
Garry Norris; Executive, I.B.M.
Said Microsoft repeatedly tried to get I.B.M.'s PC business to favor Microsoft software over rival products. After I.B.M. refused, it had to pay a higher price for Windows than its leading peers in the PC industry.
 
ON USE OF MARKET POWER: Microsoft Witnesses
 
Daniel Rosen; General Manager, Microsoft
Called the Government charge that Microsoft tried to persuade Netscape to divide up the browser market "either fabrications or the products of a fundamental misunderstanding."
 
Brad Chase; Vice President, Microsoft
Said his company's deal with America Online did not require its subscribers to choose Microsoft's browser. He also testified that America Online's recent purchase of Netscape created a new and potent competitor to Microsoft.
 
Eric Engstrom; General Manager, Microsoft
Said his company did try to persuade Apple to use Microsoft's technology. But he said this was an effort at technological cooperation and not an attempt to hinder competition or divide markets.
 
Paul Maritz; Group Vice President, Microsoft
Said Intel pulled back from its multimedia software efforts because the software was designed for a previous version of Windows.
 
Robert Muglia; Senior Vice President, Microsoft
Said his company developed its own implementation of Java not to "pollute" Sun Microsystems' Internet programming language but to enable programs written in Java to run faster and better on Windows.
 
Cameron Myhrvold; Vice President, Microsoft
Denied that on-line services had to favor Microsoft's browser to be included in a featured place on Windows.
 
William Poole; Senior Director, Microsoft
Said its contracts with companies doing business on the Internet, requiring them to promote Microsoft's browser, were "routine cross-licensing agreements."
 
Joachim Kempin; Vice President, Microsoft
Said the Government claim that his company could dictate terms to the manufacturers "totally ignores the business reality" that they are "our valued partners."
 
John T. Rose; Senior Vice President, Compaq Computer
Characterized Compaq's disputes with Microsoft more as sparring between comparative equals than as his company bending submissively to Microsoft's will.
 
ON INTEGRATING THE BROWSER: Government Witnesses
 
Glenn E. Weadock; Consultant
Surveyed 13 large corporations that said they did not regard the browser as part of the operating system -- as Microsoft contended -- and many found adapting their software to Windows with a bundled browser to be a costly inconvenience.
 
David J. Farber; Computer Scientist, University of Pennsylvania
Said software was "infinitely malleable" and that there was no technical efficiency from Microsoft's decision to fold its Internet browser into Windows.
 
Edward W. Felten; Computer Scientist, Princeton University
Wrote a program that he said stripped the browser out of Windows. The technical experiment was intended to support the allegation that Windows and the browser are separate programs and not one, as Microsoft asserts.
 
ON INTEGRATING THE BROWSER: Microsoft Witnesses
 
Michael Devlin; Independent Software Developer
Said Microsoft's decision to include browser technology in Windows made it far easier to write programs that take advantage of Internet functions.
 
James Allchin; Senior Vice President, Microsoft
Said his company's decision to fold its browser into the Windows operating system and give the browser away free was a straightforward product design decision that benefited consumers.
 
ON COMPETITION: Government Witnesses
 
Franklin M. Fisher; Economist, Massachusetts Institute of Technology
Described Microsoft's entire pattern of behavior in the Internet software business as "predatory" -- its pricing, product bundling and contracts. He said Microsoft's behavior stifled competition and innovation. And while he conceded there was little evidence of harm to consumers to date, he said Microsoft's tactics posed a substantial threat of harm.
 
Frederick R. Warren-Boulton; Economist
Said the price Microsoft charges for Windows was "significantly" higher than it would be if the company faced competition, and that its Windows price had been rising in recent years as other components of PC's had generally declined.
 
ON COMPETITION: Microsoft Witnesses
 
Richard L. Schmalensee; Economist, Massachusetts Institute of Technology
Said that Microsoft faced many competitive threats, ranging from the Linux operating system to Web software. He said the price of Windows was low because of the ever-present challenge to Microsoft's leadership in the software industry. Microsoft, he added, is not a monopoly and its actions in the Internet software market were pro-competitive.
 
Gordon E. Eubanks; President, Oblix
Described the software business as an arena of fast and fluid change where today's market leader is tomorrow's also-ran. The industry does tend toward "natural monopolies" based on technology standards like Windows, he said.

SECTION: Section C;Page 2;Column 5;Business/Financial Desk

HEADLINE: Brazilians Take to the Web With Uncommon Speed

BYLINE: By SIMON ROMERO

DATELINE: SAO PAULO, Brazil

 BODY:
Pierre Schurmann, one of Brazil's Internet barons, did not know things would develop so fast.
Three years ago, Mr. Schurmann, 30, founded Zeek, a popular search engine. Earlier this year, he sold Zeek to the Star Media Network, a company that was formed to seek out Internet opportunities in Latin America, and became director of new business development in Brazil. Then Star Media went public with a stock offering on Nasdaq. 
 "The speed of it all is amazing," Mr. Schurmann said in an interview. "I'm not sure when the 18-hour days are going to let up."
They will probably continue for some time because Star Media is facing competition from companies including Yahoo Inc., Microsoft, America Online, Psinet and Telefonica S.A. -- that are jumping into Brazil's portal war.
The growing competition in Brazil among portals, the Web pages people often use for information and links to other sites, may be a preview of how large Internet companies will spread in other parts of the non-English-speaking world.
"This is perhaps the most competitive portal market in the world at this time," said Osvaldo Barbosa de Oliveira, the head of Microsoft's on-line operations in Brazil. Mr. Oliveira cited the entry this year of the three largest portals, America Online, Yahoo and Microsoft, plus competition from companies that provide local-language content. In addition, Telefonica of Spain is entering the battle, as well as the Globo organization, Brazil's largest media group.
Microsoft's MSN was the first large foreign portal to establish operations in Brazil, reaching deals with 19 Brazilian content providers. Yahoo quickly followed with its own Portuguese-language portal, opening an office in Sao Paulo this month, while America Online, whose partner in Latin America is the Cisneros Group of Venezuela, is expected to start operations this week.
Making Brazil a priority is easy to understand. With a single currency, set of borders and regulatory framework for nearly 165 million people, this country is an easier target for expansion than the many Spanish-speaking countries stretching from Mexico to Argentina. "Brazil is another animal altogether because you get a huge market with a common culture in one swoop," said Laura d'Oliveira, the president of the Sky3 in Hollywood, Fla., which specializes in making corporate Web sites more profitable through on-line marketing in Latin America.
The number of people using the Internet in Brazil grew 130 percent last year, making it Latin America's most wired nation, accounting for half of the region's six million people on the Web, according to the International Data Corporation. And despite a weak economy and big gaps between rich and poor, industry experts expect the number of Internet users in Brazil to grow 60 to 100 percent over the next three years.
Those users pay rates for Internet access that are comparable to those in the United States. The top two providers charge about $20 and $12 a month, respectively, for unlimited access. Others charge a fee for a certain amount of time and then more for each extra hour. For instance, IBM Brazil charges $23 a month for 30 hours of access and $1.50 for each additional hour.
Much of the growth in Internet use has taken place through the efforts and innovation of local companies like Universo Online, Brazil's largest service provider and portal, with 400,000 subscribers. Universo Online, which is owned by two large media concerns, Grupo Folha and Grupo Abril, is known for imaginative strategies like using live on-line ads to sell cars two years ago, when such practices were rare anywhere.
Although Caio Tulio Costa, the managing director of Universo Online, declined to comment on speculation that the company had been in takeover talks with a large portal company starting operations in Brazil, he acknowledged that Universo Online was close to selling a 10 percent stake to a new partner. And the company has begun its own buying spree, with the purchase last week of Miner Technology, a company in central Brazil that creates Web sites.
While newcomers learn to compete with entrenched locals, the portal war is taking place amid two bigger transformations. The first is the invigoration of Brazil's telecommunications system, a change that occurred after the nation's telephone monopoly was privatized and opened to competition in 1998. That has increased telephone and Internet access nationwide, something is expected to continue.
The second is the consolidation among Internet service providers, or I.S.P.'s, a trend underlined by the recent purchase of two leading providers by Psinet of Herndon, Va., catapulting Psinet into the ranks of the country's five largest.
"By acquiring I.S.P.'s in Brazil, we're tapping into a market for E-commerce that should grow by 75 to 100 percent over the next couple of years," said Philippe Kuperman, head of Psinet's Latin American activities. Though companies like Yahoo are largely in search of advertising revenue, Psinet hopes to make money by providing specific services to the corporate market.
In a similar, yet wider-reaching transaction, Telefonica bought a controlling stake last week in Nutec, Brazil's second-largest service provider and portal. Telefonica, already the biggest telephone company in Brazil after gobbling up many parts of the former state monopoly, bought Nutec to pursue its goal of being the largest portal in the Portuguese- and Spanish-speaking world. Local news organizations speculated that Telefonica paid $500 million for Nutec, although people close to the transaction said its value was considerably less.
Nobody is quite sure when, or how, the dust will settle as the competition among portals gears up and Internet use continues to climb.
"In the U.S. it's hard to find a friend who's not on the Internet, while in Brazil I know many people who aren't connected," said Gabriel Bitran, a Brazilian who is deputy dean at the Massachusetts Institute of Technology's Sloan School of Management and an expert on electronic commerce. "I don't think poverty should be that much of an obstacle as the price of PC's falls."
