HEADLINE: Dinner for a Presidential Library, Contributions Welcome

BYLINE: By DON VAN NATTA Jr. 

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, June 27

 BODY:
Quietly, at a French restaurant on the East Side of midtown Manhattan on Monday evening, President Clinton, the most effective fund-raiser ever to occupy the White House, plans to embark on the most ambitious fund-raising campaign of his long political career.
After having dinner with 40 executives, mostly from the fashion industry, the President is expected to talk about his vision for the William Jefferson Clinton Presidential Library, a museum and public policy center on the banks of the Arkansas River in Little Rock that will be dedicated in part to the preservation of the 42d President's legacy. 
 But Mr. Clinton will not be likely to dwell on the library's price tag: $125 million.
Indeed, by law the President cannot solicit contributions for the cause. None of the guests at Monday night's dinner at La Grenouille have paid any money to attend, and the event was not listed as a library fund-raiser on Mr. Clinton's official schedule. Instead, the entry for the dinner simply said: "private event."
Though $125 million exceeds the total combined amount given to the Clinton-Gore campaigns in 1992 and 1996, the President's supporters say the task of raising that amount for the library is hardly as daunting as it seems.
Unburdened by the limits on individual contributions to Presidential candidates and legal defense funds, the William J. Clinton Presidential Foundation can accept unlimited contributions for the library from individuals, corporations, foundations and even foreign governments. Each donation is tax-deductible.
Thus, for the first time, the President's most supportive, wealthiest friends will have no restrictions placed on their generosity, and supporters believe some will give millions of dollars to the foundation.
Supporters of Mr. Clinton said the foundation had already received generous pledges from several of Mr. Clinton's Hollywood friends, most notably the Dreamworks moguls Steven Spielberg and David Geffen, and Ronald W. Burkle, the California supermarket magnate. This fall, supporters said, a library dinner will be held in Hollywood, possibly at Mr. Geffen's house.
Neither Mr. Spielberg nor Mr. Geffen agreed to comment for this article. A spokesman for Mr. Burkle also declined to comment.
Not surprisingly, Mr. Clinton is taking an intense interest in the library. He ruminated for many months over the proper location. And, after many more months of reviewing dozens of blueprints, the President has still not chosen an architect, despite widespread speculation in the field. Mr. Clinton is taking his time because he "wants the design to be absolutely perfect," one friend said.
"I want it to be great, and I want it to be a great gift to my state," Mr. Clinton said at a news conference on Friday. "I've worked quite hard on it and thought a lot about it, and I think that -- I think the people at home will be quite excited about it when they see what we're going to do, and will be thrilled by it."
Despite the President's enthusiasism, the library project is sensitive enough at the White House that most of those involved in the planning and fund-raising for the library refused to talk about it.
Although these are not expressly political contributions, some critics of the system of campaign financing say it is improper to raise such large amounts of money while still in office.
Mr. Clinton would not be the first sitting President to raise money for a library, but the Administration is sensitive because it has been criticized for its fund-raising tactics in political campaigns, like allowing favored contributors to stay in the White House's Lincoln Bedroom.
Terence McAuliffe, a close friend of Mr. Clinton who raised $27 million for the Clinton-Gore campaign in just seven months in 1995, is leading the library foundation's fund-raising effort.
Besides the library fund, Mr. McAuliffe is helping Mr. Clinton and his wife, Hillary Rodham Clinton, raise money to retire their nearly $10 million debt to their lawyers.
Mr. McAuliffe has also agreed to help Mrs. Clinton if she chooses to seek the New York Senate seat being vacated by Senator Daniel Patrick Moynihan next year.
Supporters of the library project said fund-raisers had spoken about the library foundation's needs with friends and admirers of the President, some of whom have already made general pledges to help.
But Skip Rutherford, a friend of the President who is the local coordinator for the library project in Little Rock, said there had not yet been any financial commitments from anyone.
Still, Mr. Rutherford and other supporters said they were confident of raising the $125 million needed for the library and its endowment. They brushed aside any suggestion that the President's impeachment last December would slow the flow of money to the project.
Mr. Clinton's library will sit on 17 acres on a site now strewn with weeds and aging warehouses near Little Rock's River Market. The site is within walking distance from the Old State House, where Mr. Clinton served as Governor and announced his intention to run for President.
There are 11 Presidential libraries in the United States. The first was for Theodore Roosevelt, the 26th President. The earliest President to have his own library is Rutherford B. Hayes, the 19th President, whose library was built in 1916 in Fremont, Ohio.
Mr. Clinton's planned library would be the most expensive ever built, followed by the George Bush Presidential Library and Museum in College Station, Tex., which cost $83 million and was dedicated in 1997.
Each Presidential library was built entirely with private donations, but nonpartisan groups have criticized Presidents who chose to raise money while still in office, as President Reagan did in the last two years of his second term for his library in Simi Valley, Calif.
"If a donor is giving millions of dollars to the President to enhance his immortality, and doing it while the chief executive is still in the Oval Office, the amount of power that donor has just acquired is off the charts," said Charles Lewis, the founder and executive director of the Center for Public Integrity, a nonprofit watchdog group.
Mr. Lewis said one of Mr. Clinton's most enduring legacies would be that he was a politician who was "not only adept at fund-raising, but had a cast-iron stomach for doing it."
"He's a little bit like the battery bunny," Mr. Lewis said. "He just keeps on raising money."
Presidents Nixon, Ford, Carter and Bush did not begin raising money for their libraries until after they left office, but all four left the White House earlier than they had intended. Mr. Nixon struggled to raise money for his library, partly because his solicitors began seeking donations a decade after he resigned. One of his supporters said in 1990 that it was hard to get money from contributors who were "not basking in the light of a sitting President."
Ronald Reagan, the most recent two-term President, used the allure of his Presidency to raise millions for his library before he left office in January 1989. Mr. Reagan also began earlier than Mr. Clinton has begun. Mr. Clinton's supporters said they hoped to imitate the success that Mr. Reagan had in generating contributions while in office, though they worry privately that they should have started even earlier.
"As two-term Presidents, both President Clinton and President Reagan have that luxury to get a good start on their libraries," Mr. Rutherford said. "President Reagan had a series of events -- dinners, receptions -- where people discussed the library."
Mr. Rutherford said Mr. Clinton had spent a lot of time speaking with people about his hopes for the library. "Quite frankly, lots of people have just gone to Washington on a family vacation and see him or go by the White House to visit, and the library usually comes up," Mr. Rutherford said. "It's just on his mind."
The host of the private dinner in Manhattan on Monday night is Arnold Simon, chief executive of Aris Industries. Forty fashion industry leaders are expected to attend, including the designers Vera Wang and Nicole Miller, Mr. Simon said.
"I love the President," Mr. Simon said. "You very rarely run across politicians with a real heart. I just think he has done wonderful things for the country, and I've benefited from them personally. And this is my way to try to help."
Mr. Simon, who was the chairman of Designer Holdings, which made Calvin Klein jeans, said he hoped that the dinner would encourage his colleagues to contribute to the library.
Mr. Simon said he intended to call the dinner guests in the weeks to come and ask each of them to write a check payable to the Clinton library foundation.
"The President," Mr. Simon said, "has done enormous amounts for the country in respect to unemployment, the stock market and interest rates."

HEADLINE: Young People Open to H.I.V. Testing

BYLINE: By ALISA TANG 

 BODY:
Jennifer Jako once had a "not me" attitude about AIDS.
"I really was an unlikely candidate for this disease," Ms. Jako, an AIDS educator and documentary film producer, said in a panel discussion in New York last week. "I didn't think this would happen to me." 
 Ms. Jako, who is from Lake Oswego, a suburb of Portland, Ore., tested positive for H.I.V. eight years ago, when she was 18.
Her youth was typical of many people testing positive: 51 percent of new H.I.V. infections in the United States are among people under 25, the Centers for Disease Control and Prevention says. Yet, the agency says, only a quarter of sexually active teen-agers have been tested.
The panel discussion was held to announce a study by the Henry J. Kaiser Family Foundation, which explored the attitudes toward H.I.V. testing among 73 higher-risk teen-agers, defined as sexually active and economically or socially marginalized youth in urban areas that have a high incidence of H.I.V.
With few exceptions, the study found, young people believed that H.I.V. could not affect them. But most of the teen-agers said that, given the opportunity, they would be tested for the virus.
Maureen Michaels, president of Michaels Opinion Research, which conducted the study for the foundation, suggested that doctors ask sexually active adolescents whether they would like to be tested for H.I.V.
"It's a simple question," Ms. Michaels said. "It doesn't get asked all the time, but when it does, young people will say yes."
Authorities say that acting quickly is important. "The key to successful treatment is getting treatment early and consistently," said Victor Barnes, associate director for international H.I.V. prevention at the Centers for Disease Control. "For individuals who do not seek H.I.V. testing until they are experiencing symptoms of illness associated with H.I.V.-related diseases or AIDS itself, today's treatments cannot offer as much hope."
The teen-agers favored H.I.V. testing in offices that provided confidential and inexpensive tests, the study reported. Many did not want their parents to know.
Some of the teen-agers had called around for cost estimates for the H.I.V. test and given up because they thought they could not afford it and did not realize their other options, such as free testing in some cities.
The teen-agers also were more likely to be tested by doctors and nurses who respected the fact that they were sexually active. "I wouldn't have gotten tested if the nurse hadn't given me the opportunity," said Ms. Jako, who has received a grant from the Kaiser Foundation to travel and speak to young people about AIDS awareness. "As I was leaving the office, the nurse said, 'You've gotten tested for S.T.D.'s, why not get tested for H.I.V. as well?' My nurse and doctor were convinced that I was going to be healthy."
"I'm so grateful for their having provided me with the opportunity," Ms. Jako said. "It is their responsibility to give us the opportunity to be tested. If they hadn't tested me then, I could have attributed symptoms I had to other things, and I would never know to this day that I'm H.I.V. positive."

HEADLINE: Panel Criticizes Extent of Arafat's Power

BYLINE: By DEBORAH SONTAG 

DATELINE: RAMALLAH, West Bank, June 28

 BODY:
Yasir Arafat, president of the Palestinian Authority, has concentrated executive power so completely in his own hands that he personally approves all senior officials' vacations and their per diem expenses, an international study released here today found.
Mr. Arafat has used government jobs to ease unemployment and to reward political loyalty to such an extent, the report says, that the public sector is bloated, inefficient and risks incurring staggering debts to pay pensions. 
 The Palestinian Legislative Council is relatively impotent, the judiciary is in disarray, unregulated monopolies rule many industries, and the police force abuses its powers. Yet, compared with many infant countries' governments, the Palestinian Authority, whose West Bank and Gaza territories are not even a state, is managing openly and spending well its billions of dollars in aid from international donors.
Those are some of the conclusions presented here today by an American-European panel sponsored by the Council on Foreign Relations in New York and paid for by the European Commission and the Government of Norway. The panel, led by former Prime Minister Michel Rocard of France, has spent a year evaluating Palestinian institutions with the Palestinians' cooperation.
On Tuesday, the group, a high-powered assembly of former European Prime Ministers and American legislators, is to present its recommendations formally to Mr. Arafat.
The committee leavened its critical findings with compassion for the Palestinian Authority. It is not easy to evolve overnight from a secretive authoritarian military organization in exile like the Palestine Liberation Organization into a pluralistic government whose financial records are accurate and open, Mr. Rocard said.
In its five years of life, the authority has gone further down the path toward building democratic institutions, securing public order and delivering services to its rapidly growing population than many comparable "postconflict" governments, the group said.
"Have Africa in mind, please," Mr. Rocard said.
Palestinian journalists at a news conference here said the dynamic -- wrapping harsh assessments in praise -- reminded them of this Arabic saying: After hitting someone over the head, you must readjust his cap.
But the panel, which will continue to function as a consultant to the Palestinians, seemed determined to develop a critical-minded partnership with the authority and to goad it toward secular democracy.
That reflects, in part, the international community's determination to make sure that its $3.3 billion in assistance, pledged for five years, is not spent on a government in training that is corrupt, inefficient and abusive toward its citizens.
The report also underscores the fact that the Palestinians now enjoy significant political good will around the world, particularly from the Europeans, who consider the emerging Palestinian society a pet project.
"The five-year-old story of the Palestinian Authority is a story of success," said Terje Roed-Larsen, a special adviser to the Norwegian Foreign Minister. "Why? Because the Palestinian Authority has built its institutions under extremely difficult circumstances."
Mr. Roed-Larsen referred to the many impasses in the peace negotiations with Israel, which demanded that the Palestinians build "their new house" while traveling on a bumpy road to statehood. He noted that the Palestinians had been more closely scrutinized than any other "entity recipient of donor money on this globe."
That said, the committee's report suggested that it was a matter of great urgency for the Palestinian Authority to "acknowledge its own shortcomings" and change.
"Not to reform is not an option," said Henry Siegman, the project director and a senior fellow at the Council on Foreign Relations. "The World Bank and the I.M.F. will participate in the next phase, and they will be looking closely at the trend lines, at whether there is movement toward reform."
The report recommended that the Palestinians carry out these activities, among others:
*Establish a constitution, a move that Mr. Arafat has delayed.
*Pare the president's office by delegating added authority to the ministries and local governments.
*Grant greater power to the Legislative Council, which, the report says, the president stifles.
*Abolish the secretive state security courts and provide more support to an independent judicial system.
*Establish more financial openness and accountability by, at the least, presenting a detailed and timely budget to the Legislature and disclosing public revenues.
*Bring the police force under clear civilian authority, and, as the Israelis demand, reduce its size substantially.
When Mr. Arafat learned of the criticisms in an informal meeting, Mr. Siegman and others recounted, he suggested that he was being judged too harshly. For instance, what the Americans and Europeans see as a bloated payroll, he explained, he sees as a political management tool.
"He told us that if these people were not on the public payroll, they'd be in the streets causing problems for him, and then we'd be asking him to keep better control of the streets," Mr. Siegman said. "He argued that we overstated everything, that he's not running Monaco, and that he has people inside and outside who want him to fail."
The report, at least in the executive summary released today, does not deeply examine two problems that the authority's critics have considered paramount -- financial corruption, which involves the revenues that the authority collects, and not international money -- and human rights violations.
One Palestinian official noted that the entire leadership of the European Commission, which financed the panel, had stepped down after accusations of cronyism and corruption had been made."So what we are supposedly doing is perhaps not that remarkable," the official said.
At the news conference, the Palestinians' Planning and International Cooperation Minister, Nabil Shaath, said, "Questions about human rights have already been dealt with."

SECTION: Section A; Page 18; Column 1; Editorial Desk

HEADLINE: A Windfall's Promise

 BODY:
The new era of government surpluses forecast last January has suddenly grown bigger and brighter. Over the next 15 years, President Clinton announced yesterday, the Federal Government will produce surpluses of $5.5 trillion, a cool $1 trillion higher than January's number. The President also offered some ideas about how to spend this windfall. In terms of broad intent, his proposals are not much different from those he unveiled five months ago. But in important details they are much improved. Mr. Clinton has also left the door ajar for discussion of broader tax cuts down the road. That is a political necessity, given Republican control of Congress, and positions the President to argue for tax relief for low- and middle-income families. But Mr. Clinton has no intention of cutting a deal on taxes until Congress addresses his main priorities.
His top priority, one that he hopes will become an important part of his legacy, is to reduce the national debt and shore up Social Security and Medicare. Fully 80 percent of the surplus would be devoted to these purposes, with the rest divided equally between discretionary-spending initiatives and a program to encourage individual retirement accounts. The emphasis on Social Security and Medicare is sensible and farsighted. If nothing is done, Social Security will be threatened with insolvency as the baby boomers retire. Medicare also requires substantial investments. 
 One addition to the President's plans is a program to provide improved prescription drug benefits for the elderly. The potential of this program for cost escalation has to be studied closely. But the most important change involves Social Security. In his earlier proposal, Mr. Clinton wanted the flexibility, in the early years, to spend Social Security surpluses on other programs -- much as those funds are spent now. Responding to criticism from both Republicans and Democrats, Mr. Clinton has now adopted the idea of a "lock box" to insure that excess revenues are used only to reduce the debt and to insure Social Security's long-term health. In addition, the interest saved by reducing the debt in future years would be earmarked exclusively for Social Security.
In sum, Mr. Clinton's fire wall around the country's two biggest entitlement programs is even stronger than before, which should please his Republican critics. Of course nothing will fully satisfy these Republicans unless he also gives some ground on their proposals for high-end tax cuts. On this score, Mr. Clinton reiterated his preference for targeted tax cuts for low- and moderate-income families, while not ruling out discussions about something bigger.
But he was tactically smart to insist on first things first. Strengthen the entitlement programs, he said, then we can talk about taxes and spending. That is the correct order. These rosy predictions, after all, are only predictions. No one can be sure what the economy will look like three years from now, let alone fifteen. The reasonable approach is to capitalize on today's boom to fix tomorrow's problems in Social Security and Medicare. 
http://www.nytimes.com

HEADLINE: Another Time Warner Unit Wins Tax Break From City

BYLINE: By CHARLES V. BAGLI 

 BODY:
Three months after New York City granted a generous tax break to one division of Time Warner Inc., the Giuliani administration has agreed to provide the media giant with an additional $28 million subsidy for its publishing division, which includes Time, Fortune, Money and In Style magazines.
And Time Warner's bargaining for tax breaks may not be over. Warner Music, the company's music division, is scouring Manhattan for a new home, and, real estate executives said, another government incentive. 
 The city's latest deal with Time Inc. coincided with the company's decision to extend its lease in the Time and Life Building, at 1271 Avenue of the Americas, where it occupies 80 percent of the 1.6 million-square-foot skyscraper. The company, which has 4,200 employees in the city, has also signed a deal to rent 220,000 square feet of space in an adjacent office tower at 135 West 50th Street, which will be known as the Sports Illustrated Building.
Time plans to spend $190 million renovating the two buildings, which will be connected on the second floor.
Last March, the city granted Home Box Office, another division of Time Warner, an $11 million tax break after it decided to renew its lease at the northeast corner of 42d Street and the Avenue of the Americas, opposite Bryant Park. As with Time Inc., few people in the real estate industry expected HBO to leave the city.
"How many times are they going to give a tax break to Time Warner," said Jonathan Bowles, research director of the Center for an Urban Future, a nonprofit policy institute that is critical of such deals. "At one time, these deals might have been necessary to retain large employers. But today, they are nothing more than giveaways to influential companies that just want to grab their share of the city's tax revenue pie."
The Giuliani administration has granted more than $2 billion in tax breaks and electrical subsidies to 50 large corporations, a policy that critics say shifts the tax burden to small and medium-size companies while taking badly needed funds from the municipal coffers.
Randy L. Levine, Deputy Mayor for economic development, defended the $28 million deal, saying that Time Inc. had considered moving some administrative jobs to Atlanta, Boston and New Jersey. Mr. Levine also said that $12.3 million in benefits was tied to job growth.
Under the agreement, the company would get $1 million if it brought back 250 jobs that were transferred elsewhere in recent years. But if either Time Inc. or HBO reneges on the deal, both companies will lose all their tax breaks, he said.
"It's a good example of how we are creating jobs in New York City and bringing back jobs from other places," Mr. Levine said.
Ironically, such deals were the subject of an award-winning series in November in Time magazine about "corporate welfare," which, the magazine said, costs every working American the equivalent of two weeks' pay a year.
Given that Time Inc. still had 10 years to go on its lease at the Time and Life Building, some real estate executives were skeptical about the company's threat to move some jobs.
Peter Costiglio, a spokesman for Time Inc., declined to discuss the package of tax breaks, referring questions to city officials.
A year ago, the Giuliani administration rebuffed Time Warner when the company asked for tax breaks for its proposed new headquarters at Columbus Circle. Richard D. Parsons, president of Time Warner, had argued that he only wanted the same kind of subsidies the city had granted to his competitors: Bertelsmann A.G., Viacom Inc. and NBC. The New York Times Company and the News Corporation have also received tax breaks.
But city officials said it made no sense to provide tax breaks for a luxury project at one of Manhattan's most sought-after development parcels. Since then, Time Warner has talked to state officials about getting an incentive package for the Cable News Network, which is supposed to move into new digital broadcast studios in the 2.1 million-square-foot project at Columbus Circle.
"We made it clear during the bidding for Columbus Circle that there would be no tax breaks," Mr. Levine said. "We told Time Warner that we would deal with the individual operating companies. Therefore, the parent company will not be eligible for any incentive packages."
Although the city balked at providing tax breaks at Columbus Circle, it had no such compunctions about midtown Manhattan, where office rents are among the highest in the country. HBO briefly considered moving to a new tower in Times Square before it began renegotiating its lease at the Avenue of the Americas and opened negotiations with city and state officials.
Time Inc. was cramped at its headquarters in the Time and Life Building, its home since 1959. Company executives considered building a new tower, as well as moving some employees to different locations. Instead, the company extended its lease through 2017 and found additional space in an adjacent building. It will occupy 1.7 million square feet, with the ability to expand to 2.6 million square feet.
"We effectively expanded the corporate headquarters, created swing space for retrofitting and put Sports Illustrated's brand on the new building, all while satisfying Time's economic, location and image considerations," said Mitchell S. Stier, executive vice president of Julien J. Studley, the broker who represented Time Inc., along with Matthew Barlow and Michael Colacino.

HEADLINE: Foreign Affairs;
This Is the Big One

BYLINE: By THOMAS L. FRIEDMAN 

 BODY:

Memo to: President Mohammad Khatami of Iran & Prime Minister Zhu Rongji of China
From: President William J. Clinton
Dear Gentlemen, I am writing to you both because though you may not know it, you share the same problem, a serious problem, which if not addressed effectively will become my problem. Quite simply, you are both facing the biggest challenge ever from your domestic opponents. This is the Big One for both of you. You run from this challenge and your aspirations for your respective countries will be squashed. It's that simple. 
 President Khatami, let's start with you. Your hard-line opponents have ginned up this bogus allegation that 13 Iranian Jews have been spying for Israel. We both know Israel would never be so crazy as to use the tiny Jewish minority in Iran to do its spying. But we also know why your opponents seized on this canard -- because it puts you in an impossible position. If you defend these Iranian Jews, your opponents will denounce you for protecting "Zionist agents"; and if you don't release them, and, God forbid, they are executed, your whole strategy of improving Iran's relations with the West -- beginning first with Western Europe and then with the U.S. -- will be aborted.
And let me assure you, if you kill these 13 Jews, Iran will be treated as a pariah state like you've never seen. You and Slobodan Milosevic and Saddam Hussein will be vacationing together for the rest of your lives.
We both know why this came up now. Iran will be holding its parliamentary elections in March. President Khatami, you trounced your opponents in the presidential election last year, winning 70 percent of the vote. But the hard-liners still control the Parliament, the judiciary, the Revolutionary Guards and the Intelligence Directorate. If you can win the Parliament in March, your opponents will really be on the run, so they are using this case to undermine you before the March runoff. But you still have the mass base with you, and you need to rally them, in the streets if need be, against those who want to use this spying case to cut off Iran from the world. Tell the prosecutors that if there is evidence of spying they must show it to the world. If not, Islamic justice requires that the accused be set free.
Prime Minister Zhu, the same goes for you. You know we bombed your embassy in Belgrade by accident. And you know your domestic opponents are keeping this thing alive to undermine all of those in China who want to integrate with the world and with the U.S. I know you need to let off steam, but the steam can also blow you away. When I read your recent Peoples Daily editorial comparing the U.S. with "Nazi Germany," it said to me that your opponents are trying to stir up a level of anti-Americanism not seen since the Cultural Revolution. And they are doing it to make it impossible for you to make the concessions needed for China to join the World Trade Organization -- which would impose a set of global rules on China's trading practices that your corrupt opponents have no interest in. I know you also have people who sincerely want to restore the walls around China, to free it from American domination and influence.
But Zhu, there is no future in Chinese isolationism, and if you don't take a stand against your opponents, if you are unable to mobilize President Jiang and China's elites to complete this W.T.O. deal (you've stopped even returning our negotiators' phone calls), you will never be able to accelerate economic reform as you aspire, let alone attract sufficient foreign investment or participate in the setting of global trading rules.
I know what I am asking you means I've got to confront my own domestic opponents, too. Zhu, you have my word that once you join the W.T.O. I will bring China into the G-8 and make it the G-9, and this time I won't shrink from getting Congress to ratify your W.T.O. membership. Mohammad, let me remind you again: the door is open for normal relations between America and Iran. Yes, I am ready to lift the sanctions on Iran and to take the measures that you have long called for, such as settling outstanding financial claims. But I'm not going to lift a finger for a rogue state that won't renounce terrorism and kills innocent Jews. Like I said, guys, this is the Big One for both of you. You run away from these challenges, you turn the other cheek, you balk, you blink, you're finished.

HEADLINE: ON BASEBALL;
Interleague Games Too Lucrative to Lose

BYLINE: By Murray Chass 

 BODY:
The upstart Cincinnati Reds sweep a four-game series from the Houston Astros and supplant them in first place. The second-place Seattle Mariners take two of three from Texas and move a game closer to the first-place Rangers. The second-place Mets win the first game of a three-game series with the first-place Atlanta Braves but then lose the next two games, winding up with a net loss of a game in the standing.
These series, played this past weekend, are the kind baseball should schedule more of, not interleague games, a growing number of players believe. Games like these bear most directly on division races, and they should take precedence over the still newfangled idea of games between National and American League teams, the players feel. 
Other complaints are growing, too. The interleague phase of the schedule, players say, creates unfair circumstances for teams competing for the wild-card spots in the playoffs. The Mets, for example, play the Yankees six times, giving them a more difficult burden than the San Francisco Giants, who have six games against the Oakland Athletics.
And if they have to play inter league games, some players say, rotate the division matchups, as was intended originally, rather than having the same teams play every year.
All of the criticisms are well founded, but the only one that has caught the immediate attention of the officials who make such decisions is the last. Rotating divisions has been put back into their planning, possibly for next season, certainly for seasons after that. In adjusting the interleague schedule, however, officials don't want to lose the attractive series between geographical rivals. Attendance figures demonstrate why.
The three-game series between the Mets and the Yankees at Yankee Stadium drew 168,404; the Yankees previously averaged 110,877 for three games. These are figures for some other series, with previous three-game average home attendance in parenthesis:
*Angels at Dodgers, 147,770 (120,012)
*Indians at Reds, 141,325 (61,599)
*White Sox at Cubs, 115,206 (101,325)
Two series in that category did not draw well: Florida at Tampa Bay (64,174) and Montreal at Toronto (76,651). What those figures suggest is that no one in Canada, not just Montreal, wants to see the Expos play, and that Major League Baseball made a major mistake in placing a team in St. Petersburg, Fla.
Actually, attendance figures from the first segment of this season's interleague schedule -- the second begins next Friday -- generally reflect serious fan interest in the games. Only three teams, Colorado, San Diego and Minnesota, averaged fewer fans for interleague games than for intraleague games to that point.
The Tigers and the Cardinals, geographical rivals not, drew remarkably well for their home-and-home series, 125,371 in Detroit, 135,912 in St. Louis. The Phillies and the Orioles attracted 143,906 in Baltimore, which was 7,000 a game more than the Orioles had drawn in the first two months of the season. And so on.
Critics of interleague play never wanted to see it started. Some now accept it for the economic windfall it has brought. Others ignore the economic benefits. They are the same people who criticize teams for selling naming rights to stadiums and putting advertising on the outfield walls.
But teams have to generate revenue where they can to pay enormous salaries.
Like interleague play or not -- there still is much to be said for having a World Series where the teams have not played during the season -- no one can deny fan interest.
"It's the drama, the excitement," Commissioner Bud Selig said. "We have to listen to our fans, and our fans for the most part in most places are loving this."
Selig was quick to shoot down the argument that not all interleague matchups are good. "There are," he said, "some intraleague rivalries that aren't too good."
By no means, however, is Selig satisfied with the existing schedule. He has long been a proponent of an unbalanced schedule, in which teams play significantly more games against each team in their division than against teams in other divisions.
Creating an unbalanced schedule with rotating interleague games and a continuing slate of interleague games between natural rivals is a challenge for the schedule makers.
Katy Feeney, the National League's senior vice president, cautioned that the combination of rotating opponents and maintaining natural rivalries would very likely create a greater imbalance of the sort that players object to, the one that has the Mets playing the Yankees while the Giants play the Athletics.
Although union officials are involved in the ongoing schedule discussions and would presumably restrain baseball officials from going astray with their ideas, the players could conceivably decide they no longer like the interleague plan and reject it. Interleague play requires player approval.
In the meantime, both sides will watch with avid interest to see if any fans show up July 9, 10 and 11 for the series between the Devil Rays and the Marlins north of Miami and the Blue Jays and the Expos north of the United States in Montreal.

HEADLINE: THE MARKETS: STOCKS & BONDS;
SharesRise in Slow Trading In Advance of Fed Meeting

BYLINE: By KENNETH N. GILPIN 

 BODY:
Stock prices staged a broad advance yesterday, helped by a rally in the bond market. But with the Federal Reserve beginning two days of policy-making meetings today, the pace of trading was subdued.
"There is a great deal of uncertainty about the wisdom of chasing stocks right now," said Ned Collins, head equity trader at Daiwa Securities America. 
 Led by financial stocks like J. P. Morgan, American Express and Citigroup, the Dow Jones industrial average rose 102.59 points, or nearly 1 percent, to 10,655.15.
Broader market measures did even better.
The Standard & Poor's 500-stock index gained 16.04, or 1.2 percent, to 1,331.35. Retailers like Wal-Mart Stores and technology stocks like Microsoft, Lucent Technologies and Worldcom MCI were among the biggest gainers in the index.
Internet stocks had a volatile day, but a late surge pushed the Nasdaq composite index up 49.79, or nearly 2 percent, to 2,602.44. And the Russell 2000 index of small-company stocks gained 5.5, or 1.2 percent, to 448.61.
With the Fed meeting looming in the immediate future, however, trading was light.
On the Big Board, 652.9 million shares were traded, the third-lowest total of 1999. On Nasdaq, 761.9 million shares changed hands.
Given recent statements by Alan Greenspan, the Fed chairman, and other central bank officials, there is a broad consensus in financial markets that the Fed will raise short-term interest rates a quarter of a percentage point when the meeting concludes tomorrow.
But it is far less clear whether a move by the Fed would be a one-time event or the first in a series of moves. That uncertainty is likely to be removed when -- and if -- the Fed announces it has raised rates. Still, the Fed's message could come as an unwelcome surprise.
"If the language of the Fed announcement suggests that this is the first of a series of moves, that would be a problem for the market," said Philip S. Dow, a managing director in equity research at Dain Rauscher Wessels in Minneapolis.
"But the market is looking for negatives at a time when second-quarter earnings, which will start coming out next week, are going to be very good" he said. "For people who look at investing as a marathon, not a footrace, this is a pretty good time to be positioned."
Financial stocks, which have been jostled by rising interest rates, soared on yesterday's rally in the bond market.
J. P. Morgan was the top gainer in the Dow, rising 5 1/2, to 134 1/16. American Express gained 2 13/16, to 125 1/4. Citigroup rose 2 3/16, to 46. And Merrill Lynch added 2 13/16 to 74 13/16.
Retailers and software makers were also strong performers.
Wal-Mart gained 2 1/8, to 45 9/16. Dayton Hudson rose 2 5/8, to 66 7/8. And Microsoft gained 1 13/16, to 86 3/4.
Oil stocks struggled.
Exxon fell 1 1/8, to 75 1/16. Royal Dutch/Shell fell 1/2, to 58 7/16. And Mobil declined 1 1/8, to 95 5/8.
An announcement from Philip Morris that it does not plan to separate its tobacco and food businesses met with little reaction. Philip Morris closed at 41, down 3/16.

Treasury Prices Rise
 By Bloomberg News
 Bond prices enjoyed their biggest gains in more than a week after a report showed inflation was not accelerating, and President Clinton said that bigger-than-expected surpluses could be used to retire the national debt.
The Government said prices paid for goods and services were unchanged in May after climbing in April, easing concern that the Federal Reserve would raise interest rates several times, though the central bank is still expected to increase rates this week.
The price of the benchmark 30-year bond rose 23/32, to 88 17/32. Its yield, which moves in the opposite direction, fell to 6.09 percent from 6.15 percent.
Bonds also rose after Mr. Clinton said the United States could use its budget surplus to pay off the national debt by 2015. The move would lower interest rates, the President said, as investors compete for a dwindling pool of bills, notes and bonds.

HEADLINE: Whitman Quietly Signs Bill Limiting Teen-Age Abortions

BYLINE: By DAVID KOCIENIEWSKI 

DATELINE: TRENTON, June 28

 BODY:
Avoiding the kind of public fanfare that she often uses when she signs major legislation, Gov. Christine Todd Whitman signed into law today the first significant restriction on abortion rights in New Jersey in more than 20 years, prohibiting a girl younger than 18 from obtaining an abortion unless her parents are notified.
Governor Whitman, who has built a national reputation as a moderate Republican by supporting abortion rights, signed the measure in the privacy of her office, just hours after conducting a highly publicized bill-signing ceremony to enact the state's $19.5 billion budget for fiscal year 2000. 
 New Jersey conservatives stunned many lawmakers in Trenton early this month by quickly ushering the bill through the Legislature during the last two weeks before summer recess. Mrs. Whitman, who had long opposed parental notification, acknowledged today that she began negotiating with legislators after they presented her with a bill that had been approved by a veto-proof majority.
And she said she agreed to sign the bill only after persuading its sponsors to include exceptions for pregnant teen-agers who can prove to a judge that they are mature enough to make the decision on their own and for cases in which there is evidence of physical, sexual or emotional abuse by a parent or guardian.
"This legislation strikes a common-sense balance that recognizes the rights of parents to know when a medical procedure will be performed on their minor child, while preserving a young woman's legal right to choose whether or not to have an abortion," the Governor said in a statement. Earlier in the day she rejected suggestions that she was trying to play down the abortion bill-signing, saying that she signed a dozen measures today without public ceremony.
The new law, which will take effect in 90 days, will make New Jersey the 39th state to require either parental notification or parental consent for minors seeking abortions.
Abortion-rights supporters have have pledged to challenge the constitutionality of the bill, and said they plan to file court papers in the next 90 days. Lenora Lapidus, legal coordinator for the American Civil Liberties Union, said early this month that she believed the law violated the State Constitution's guarantee of equal protection because it would deny minors access to the same health care options available to women over 18.
Ms. Lapidus also argued that the law was unconstitutional because it treated pregnant minors who choose to have their children differently than those who choose to have an abortion.
"New Jersey's State Supreme Court, in its past decisions, has ruled that the State Constitution gives broader protection to reproductive rights than the U.S. Constitution," said Ms. Lapidus, noting that courts in four states have struck down parental involvement laws. "And we're confident that they will rule that way in this case as well."
But abortion opponents say that similar bills have been upheld in many other states, and that New Jersey's law was carefully crafted to withstand a court challenge.
Marie Tasy, director of New Jersey Right to Life, praised Governor Whitman for signing the bill and said that many public opinion polls indicated that more than 65 percent of state residents favored parental notification. John Tomicki, the conservative lobbyist who helped negotiate the bill, said he suspected that any court challenge of the parental notification law would be financed largely by abortion providers, like Planned Parenthood, whose employees' livelihoods are at stake.
"It's unfortunate that these people are putting profits in front of parents' rights," said Mr. Tomicki, director of the League of American Families.
Although the abortion measure is likely to capture the most headlines, Mrs. Whitman and her aides tried to focus the most attention on the state's budget, which was delivered on time and with a surprising absence of rancor. Mrs. Whitman used her line-item veto to cut $5.5 million in spending proposals from the budget passed by legislators last week, far less than the $42 million in expenditures she vetoed last year.
The Governor said she was gratified that the budget will increase school spending and set aside $98 million to preserve open space while providing $8.6 billion in property tax relief.
But many Democrats expressed disappointment that the spending plan, which comes as the state is enjoying a strong economy, did not include more significant tax reductions. The tax cuts will save homeowners an average of $120 a year, but New Jersey's property taxes will remain the highest in the nation.
"We have to do a better job of leveraging our fiscal resources so that more citizens can benefit from New Jersey's prosperity," said Richard J. Codey, the Senate minority leader. "This budget provides some cosmetic property tax relief at a time when we should shoot for substantial property tax relief."
But Governor Whitman said the budget contained a variety of tax cuts to help the elderly, veterans and families earning $20,000 a year or less.

HEADLINE: U.S. Consumers Earning More, Spending More and Saving Less

BYLINE: Bloomberg News 

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, June 28

 BODY:
Spending rose in May at a faster pace than incomes, helping prolong the economic expansion as consumers go deeper into debt, Government figures showed today.
Spending rose six-tenths of a percent in May, and incomes increased four-tenths of a percent, the Commerce Department said. Those followed similar increases in April. The savings rate, meanwhile, fell to a record low in May. For every dollar saved, $1.012 was spent. 
 Consumers continue to spend on cars, appliances, electronics and clothing, making the United States economy the world's strongest. Economists say consumers are more willing to take on new debt and home-equity loans to pay for their purchases because high stock prices are inflating household assets.
Also, investors are selling stocks and using gains to pay for a range of big-ticket items, said Joel Naroff, president of Naroff Economic Advisors in Holland, Pa. "A steep stock market decline could reduce consumption quite sharply," he said.
At the same time, the report today included an inflation gauge known as the personal consumption price deflator, which showed that prices were unchanged in May after rising in April. The tame result helped ease investor concerns that the Federal Reserve Board might raise short-term interest rates by more than the expected quarter-point when it meets on Tuesday and Wednesday.
Alan Greenspan, the Fed chairman, prefers the price deflator over the more widely followed Consumer Price Index, calling it "a far superior measure of true underlying inflation from a technician's point of view."
The savings rate is measured by subtracting taxes and spending from incomes. But some economists say they think Americans are saving more than the statistics indicate. The savings rate does not take into account capital gains from investments, and it does not account for improvement in home values. It also assumes that purchases of big-ticket items, like cars and trucks, are made in one day with cash rather than over time with credit.

HEADLINE: PLANNING FOR A SURPLUS: THE RED INK;
Clinton Sees the Possibility Of Zero U.S. Debt by 2015

BYLINE: By DAVID E. SANGER 

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, June 28

 BODY:
President Clinton today raised the mind-bending possibility that the giant national debt clock in midtown Manhattan would soon start running in reverse -- and that by 2015, the Government would owe no money to investors around the world.
There is little question that Mr. Clinton described the general direction of the clock correctly. Barring a stock market disaster or a Japan-like recession, the Federal Government's $5.6 trillion debt, the figure on the clock, will begin diminishing in the next few months. That number includes debt that the Government owes to itself, mostly to the Social Security system. 
 The more important figure -- debt that the Government owes individual investors, companies and governments around the world -- has actually been in decline for two years. How much it can be reduced in 15 years is far more problematic, dependent on a huge range of economic assumptions -- chiefly the growth rate of the national economy -- that, given the inexact nature of such things, are most likely subject to major revision.
But even if the United States could pay off all its debt in the next 15 years, many economists and some Government officials say that might not be as great as it sounds. Although huge debts in the 1980's and early 1990's, when the Government ran up huge annual deficits, were a tremendous drag on the economy, a bit of national debt may be a good thing.
"It's almost hard to imagine what this country would be like debt-free," said Alan Sinai, the chief economist of Primark Decision Systems, an economic consulting group. "But while no politician would want to admit it, the optimal debt for the United States is probably not zero. What that optimal level should be, though -- now that's a subject for a real national debate."
Without question, reducing the debt creates a host of advantages for the United States. As the Treasury tames its appetite for borrowed money, it no longer competes with homeowners looking for mortgages, for example, or companies seeking to raise money. As a result, interest rates have more room to fall.
And as the debt declines -- Mr. Clinton's projections show that it will fall below $3 trillion in 2005, and below $2 trillion in 2009 -- the amount of interest the Government pays each year goes down substantially, freeing up even more cash, while raising the national savings rate. That, in turn, helps to compensate for the free-spending ways of American consumers, who in these boom times are barely saving.
"That may be the biggest single advantage," one of Mr. Clinton's senior economic advisers said today.
But a debt-free United States might create a more complex, and some say riskier, financial landscape worldwide.
For international investors, there is no safer place to put money than United States Treasury bonds. When the Asian economic crisis hit in 1997, and accelerated last year after the collapse of the Russian economy, investors around the world put their assets into United States Treasuries. These investments help make the dollar the world's most popular "reserve currency," the money other governments hold for economic security in their central banks. And they give the United States subtle but significant economic clout around the world.
If the Government stops long-term borrowing, the money that becomes available may stay in the United States, invested, say, in mortgages or corporate debt. But if investors do not have the security of investing in United States Treasuries, they may be less interested in holding their cash in dollars, and that could affect the dollar's value on world markets.
Investors could put their money in another country's treasury bonds -- say those issued by the new European Central Bank or the Bank of Japan. But that requires taking a bet on the future of European and Japanese currencies, adding a significant risk to the investment.
Whether any of this happens depends on a series of assumptions. The chief one is the future of the American economy. Mr. Clinton's projections, released today, assume that the American economy will grow between 2.1 percent and 2.6 percent a year for the next 15 years. The Administration made similar bets for the past seven years, and it was wrong every time. But the surprise was pleasant: the economy expanded far faster, and for far longer, than even the most optimistic Government projections.
The risk is that future errors could be in the opposite direction. That is what happened to Japan, which assumed that the successes of the 1980's would extend into the 1990's. It was the blunder of the decade, and Japan is mounting a huge debt as it tries to spend its way out of seven-year recession.
"These are difficult projections to make for even the next year or two," Mr. Sinai said today, "and even more difficult for beyond that." And the risk is accentuated because most of the paydown of the debt is to occur between 2010 and 2015, allowing plenty of time for economic and political miscalculation or happenstance.
On the other hand, the Government is closer to paying off the debts that really matter than even Mr. Clinton indicated today. While the debt clock reads $5.6 trillion, the figure that kicks around the United States Treasury is less than half that: $2.77 trillion, when the amount of debt held by the Federal and state governments and the Federal Reserve is subtracted. Under the President's projections, that debt will be paid off around 2011.

HEADLINE: BUSINESS DIGEST

 BODY:

Kroll Business Consultant Provided Access to Famous
Martin R. Frankel, the man the authorities suspect of siphoning more than $300 million from a string of insurance companies, gained access to powerful people with the help of a business consultant, Thomas J. Corbally.
Mr. Corbally was an insider at Kroll Associates, one of America's best-known private investigative agencies, having worked closely with Jules B. Kroll for almost 20 years and having an office at the firm's Manhattan headquarters. Page A1. 

Some Kosovo Aid Seen as Useless
About a third to half of the emergency medical shipments sent to Kosovo refugees, many given by American companies that got generous tax breaks for the donations, were inappropriate and likely to gather dust in warehouses or be destroyed at Government expense, according to the World Health Organization. A1.
 
Nalco to Be Sold for $4.1 Billion
Suez Lyonnaise des Eaux of France will acquire Nalco Chemical for $4.1 billion in cash and $400 million in debt. Suez is powerful in municipal water treatment and energy systems, but it has been a laggard in the industrial market, particularly in the United States. Gerard Mestrallet, Suez's chief executive, said that by adding Nalco, Suez could sell water treatment, energy and other services that corporations contract out. C2.
 
Clinton Details His Budget Plan
President Clinton laid out his proposal to use the growing Federal budget surplus to eliminate the national debt while shoring up Social Security and Medicare, and left open the possibility of a deal with the Republican Congress over its demand for big tax cuts. A1.
 
Agriculture Unit Proves a Hard Sell
American Home Products is having a hard time selling its troubled agricultural division, analysts said, because two of the most likely buyers -- Novartis and the Astra Zeneca Group -- are also trying to sell theirs. Sales worldwide of agricultural products are projected to dip about 3 percent this year, and the slip has prodded many chief executives to jettison their divisions. Market Place. C12.
 
Stocks Rally in Subdued Trading
Stock prices staged a broad advance, helped by a rally in the bond market. The Dow Jones industrial average rose 102.59 points, or nearly 1 percent, to 10,655.15. The Standard & Poor's 500-stock index gained 16.04 points, or 1.2 percent, to 1,331.35, while the Nasdaq composite index rose 49.79 points, or nearly 2 percent, to 2,602.44. The yield on the 30-year bond fell to 6.09 percent from 6.15 percent. C16.
 
Fidelity and Lehman Form Alliance
Fidelity Investments and Lehman Brothers have entered an alliance under which Fidelity customers will get access to a wide range of Lehman research and underwritings of stocks and bonds. Lehman will get a big retail outlet for its stock and bond underwritings and greater marketing capability. Shares of Lehman rose $3.6875, to $57.125. C2.
 
Colombia Devalues Its Currency
Hurt by a steep economic decline and speculative selling pressure on the peso, Colombia devalued its national currency by 10 percent in an emergency decree. C4.
 
A $100 Million Settlement for Fraud
Robert E. Brennan, the former New Jersey financier whose prominent philanthropy and love of horse racing turned out to have been financed by stock fraud, has agreed to a $100 million settlement of state charges. C6.
 
Steelmaker in Merger Talks
Mexico's largest steelmaker, which defaulted on $1.9 billion of debt two months ago, announced that it was discussing a proposed merger with an industrial conglomerate. C4.

HEADLINE: Projects for Kennedy and Newark Approved

BYLINE: By ANTHONY RAMIREZ 

 BODY:
The Port Authority of New York and New Jersey approved yesterday nearly $2.5 billion in projects at Newark and Kennedy International Airports that are expected to ease airline congestion, shorten waits for passengers and create thousands of jobs.
The Port Authority board, which consists of six members from each of the two states, voted unanimously for the projects. 
 The Kennedy project, due to be finished in 2006, will create about 1,000 construction jobs and will more than double the number of American Airlines gates. The Newark project, to be finished in 2003, will create 5,200 jobs and increase by a quarter the number of Continental Air Lines gates as well as provide improvements to the rest of the airport.
Lewis M. Eisenberg, chairman of the board, said in an interview: "This vote moves forward two of the largest economic development projects in the metropolitan area in the last decade. And it comes at a time when these two facilities are in urgent need of accommodating new international passengers from all over the world."
New York and New Jersey are hotly competitive for airlines because passengers tend to live or visit here rather than simply pass through, as they do at the locations of other busy airports like O'Hare in Chicago. If airlines can win passenger loyalty through improvements, airline executives reason, they can snare more high-paying first-class and business-class passengers. Last year, a consortium of international carriers, including Japan Air Lines and Air France, built Terminal 1 at Kennedy, the first new terminal in 25 years.
David S. Stempler, president of the Air Travelers Association in Washington, a consumer advocacy group, said the projects were "going to be great for passengers" because they would shorten waits for tickets and passport checks and walking time between connecting flights. "A lot of the foment among passengers comes from the lack of airline choice, limited services and congestion, and much of that has to do with the limited infrastructure of the airports themselves."
The larger of the two projects approved yesterday, costing $1.37 billion, is at fast-growing Newark Airport, where Continental Air Lines plans to spend at least $590 million to add 12 gates to its present number of 44 gates. Continental carries the majority of passengers at Newark and accounts for a quarter of the traffic in the metropolitan region, said David Messing, a spokesman for Continental, which is based in Houston.
The remainder of the spending, more than $780 million, will finance airportwide improvements, like new roads and parking garages.
Unlike the Newark project, the Kennedy project, which will cost $1.1 billion, will focus mainly on a new terminal for American Airlines. American will pay about $1 billion, and the Port Authority plans to spend $102 million for ramps and other infrastructure improvements leading to the new terminal.
Unlike the annual traffic at Newark, which last year hit a record 32.5 million passengers, up from 22.5 million in 1988, traffic at Kennedy has been flat, at roughly 31 million passengers, for most of the last 10 years..
The Kennedy project will more than double American Airlines' gates, to 56 from 23. American, one of the largest airlines at Kennedy, has 23 jet gates and 18 open-air parking spots for small aircraft of American Eagle, a subsidiary. After the expansion, American will have 41 jet gates and 18 new enclosed gates for American Eagle aircraft, said Al Becker, a spokesman for American Airlines, which is based in Fort Worth.
The new American terminal will replace the present Terminals 8 and 9, which handled 10 million passengers last year. The new terminal will have enough space to handle up to 14 million passengers and have 220 check-in counters and 20 curb-side check-in positions.

SECTION: Section A; Page 16; Column 1; National Desk 

LENGTH: 963 words

HEADLINE: PLANNING FOR A SURPLUS: THE POLITICS;
Surplus a Salve for Clinton and Congress

BYLINE: By DAVID E. ROSENBAUM 

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, June 28

 BODY:
In the aftermath of the battle over impeachment last winter, the friction between President Clinton and the Republicans who control Congress was so intense that the gears of Government seemed likely to be paralyzed for the next two years.
But an extra half-trillion dollars can buy an awful lot of lubrication. 
 No one in either party was willing to predict confidently today that the budget windfall -- $25 billion next year, $179 billion over the next 5 years and $517 billion over the next 10, a 20 percent larger surplus than was forecast in January -- would enable a peaceful resolution of long-standing disputes over Social Security, Medicare, taxes, education and managed health care.
In their public statements, both sides said they would try.
Mr. Clinton concluded his remarks this morning by saying he hoped "very much to work with Congress in the weeks ahead."
Representative Dick Armey of Texas, the majority leader, declared, "I hope the President's comments today indicate a summer of cooperation."
Behind the scenes, though, members of both parties were extremely cautious.
An influential Republican Senator said he and his colleagues simply did not trust the President to do more than jockey for political advantage. Even before the impeachment episode, Republicans periodically questioned the President's word on budget matters. Now they reflexively distrust it on nearly everything.
An Administration official said he could not imagine anything more than "a token tax cut" that Republicans would vote for and that the President could swallow.
The newfound money did allow the President, however, to open the door a little wider to tax reductions, the central element in Republican dogma.
And it permitted Republicans to agree with the President's point that hefty new spending may be needed to put Medicare on sound footing.
That means Mr. Clinton can no longer hold, as he did in his 1996 campaign for re-election, that Republicans were determined to cut Medicare to give tax breaks to the wealthy.
And Republicans can hardly accuse the President, as they did in their successful campaign in 1994 to win control of Congress, of favoring the biggest tax increase in history.
"He's moving toward us, and we're moving toward him," a political strategist on a Republican staff in the House said.
But as for the outcome, the staff member was uncertain. "It's either going to result in a hug, or it's going to result in a train wreck."
Since the beginning of this Presidency, the relationship between Mr. Clinton and Republicans in Congress has been dominated by fights over the budget.
In 1993, Mr. Clinton's first budget, which raised taxes and also was the first step toward reducing what appeared at the time to be indelible red ink, was approved without a single Republican vote in Congress.
In 1995 and early 1996, the budget dispute resulted in Government shutdowns.
In 1997, an eleventh-hour discovery that the annual deficit would be $50 billion less than anticipated -- a windfall not unlike the one announced today by the President -- led to passage of the Balanced Budget Act of 1997, a cooperative arrangement for which Mr. Clinton and Congress took credit.
But last year, for the first time since modern budget procedures were adopted in the 1970's, Congress was unable to agree on a budget.
Although these fights involved revenues and expenditures, they were not primarily over money. They were mainly over competing views of the role of Government in society.
President Clinton sees the Government as a powerful engine for improving people's lives. Taxes, in his view, are not too high, and the revenues are needed to support the elderly and improve education.
The dominant Republican view is that the Government is an impediment to social and economic advancement. The Government should be shrunk, Republicans maintain, and taxes should be lowered so people can decide for themselves how to spend their earnings.
In that vein, the new budget boon does not help resolve some of the main disagreements between the President and Congress.
For instance, they both want to invest some Social Security money in the stock market. But the President wants the Government to control the investment. Republicans want individuals to have their own private investment accounts. Neither seems willing to compromise.
On the matter of taxes, Republicans favor measures like a lower capital gains tax and a reduction or even abolition of taxes on inheritances. The beneficiaries would largely be the most affluent taxpayers.
The tax cuts the President favors would seek to further specific social goals like making child care more affordable and helping communities raise money to build and modernize schools.
As a political matter, President Clinton's popularity has tended to rise in periods of confrontation like those of the Government shutdowns and the impeachment hearings. And Republicans have gained when there has been accommodation, as in the 1997 budget agreement.
So it might seem that Congress would be looking for a deal and the President would prefer an issue. But that may not be the case.
The word at the White House today was that the lame-duck President is thinking more nowadays about his legacy than politics. And in Congress, the bad feelings toward the President among Republicans and the distaste for cooperating with him can hardly be overestimated.
At a news conference this afternoon, Senator Trent Lott of Mississippi, the Republican leader, began by talking of the potential for accommodation. But by the end, he could not resist sticking the needle.
"He says he wants to work with us and then he flies off to fund-raisers," Mr. Lott declared. "I don't know if he's serious or not."

HEADLINE: Restraint on Rebuilding Serbia

 BODY:
As NATO peacekeepers struggle to quell ethnic violence in Kosovo, the West is considering the larger job of reconstruction in the Balkans. The initial impulse to limit aid to Serbia while Slobodan Milosevic remains in power is right. Some humanitarian assistance is justified, but no money should go to Mr. Milosevic or his Government.
The West has agreed to raise money for the rebuilding of Kosovo and Montenegro and economic development aid for Macedonia, Albania and other neighboring countries that suffered because of the war. The idea is to help them recover and tie the region more closely to Europe. 
 While few Serbians showed interest in the atrocities their countrymen were committing in Kosovo, they do not deserve to freeze this winter because of it. Democracy will be furthered in Serbia if people get over their fury with the West and realize that it is their leader, not ordinary Serbs, who is the target of sanctions.
Whenever possible, humanitarian aid should go to Serbia through independent groups, such as nongovernment organizations, opposition political leaders who run cities like Nis and Cacak and the Orthodox Church, which recently called for Mr. Milosevic to step down. Serbia has a temporary shortage of commodities that can most easily bypass the Government -- food and medicine. But the long-term threat is a winter of rolling electricity blackouts, which will cut heat and water for many. The country's electric power system should be rebuilt, but through the work of foreign companies or those owned by the opposition in Serbia. The West should support democracy in Serbia by helping groups like the independent media and opposition mayors, perhaps even helping them rebuild factories or infrastructure in their cities.
One of the most difficult reconstruction issues is what to about roads and bridges needed by Serbia's neighbors. Bulgaria, for example, depends on Serbia's portion of the Danube River -- currently blocked by bombed-out bridges -- for shipping. The river should be cleared of debris so commerce can resume, but the West ought not to rebuild the bridges.
If the reconstruction of Bosnia is prologue, then money will surely fall short of what Kosovo, Montenegro, their Balkan neighbors and democratic groups in Serbia need. Serbia's most basic need is not economic help, but democracy, which is best furthered if Mr. Milosevic is denied Western aid.

HEADLINE: Big Visions for the West Side;
Architects Turn Imaginations Loose on the Rail Yards

BYLINE: By THOMAS J. LUECK 

 BODY:
For developers and architects, it has long been the juiciest prize in Manhattan, a squalid corridor of rail yards covering 12 square blocks and stretching from the General Post Office on Eighth Avenue to the Hudson River. But what should be built on it?
Now the architects, or at least a small, internationally diverse group of the biggest names in their field, have spoken. And anyone expecting the predictable may be in for a jolt. 
 A stadium? Perhaps. And if it were built over the shoreline of the Hudson River, there might also be room for a new city park, an addition to the Jacob K. Javits Convention Center and a new Madison Square Garden.
Something simpler? What about a vast, glass-covered winglike structure extending from Ninth Avenue to the river that would provide the roof for New York City's biggest public commons? In a truly minimalist approach, one architect would leave the site almost entirely untouched except for erecting 70-foot-high "wind-blinkers" (that's British for windmills), thereby creating what he calls "the Lungs of Midtown Manhattan."
Whimsical or not, these are three of the five entries in what is regarded as one of the world's most prestigious architectural competitions for one of the city's most sought-after pieces of real estate.
The competition, which was sponsored by the Canadian Center for Architecture and judged by a panel that included such architectural luminaries as Frank O. Gehry and Philip Johnson, was intended to provoke fresh, cutting-edge and even practical ideas for the rail yards west of Pennsylvania Station, the site bordered by 34th Street and 30th Street between Eighth Avenue and the Hudson River.
The winner, announced last night, was Eisenman Architects, a New York firm headed by Peter Eisenman. It was the proposal by Mr. Eisenman, who is known for his experimental, sometimes controversial approach, that would incorporate a sports stadium, an addition to the Javits Center, a new Madison Square Garden and a public park atop the site's major new buildings.
In addition to the prestige of winning, Mr. Eisenman will receive a $100,000 prize but no assurance of future work on the site. All the finalists have already received $50,000 stipends to cover the four months' work needed to prepare their entries.
"This is the most important competition in America since the Chicago Tribune competition of 1922," said Mr. Johnson, referring to the architectural event that set parameters of style and design that were followed for decades. "It was hard to pick a winner," added Mr. Johnson, who at 92 is widely regarded as the dean of American architecture.
The competition has taken on a special importance not only because of the high-profile architects, but also because two high-ranking government officials were among the jurors. They were Charles A. Gargano, Gov. George E. Pataki's economic development adviser, and Joseph B. Rose, director of the New York City Department of City Planning.
The impressive lineup of architects was a reflection of the intense interest in the future of this site. Even though there is no official government mandate or financing to develop the land at the moment, the prospects for large-scale construction may be stronger than ever.
This is due to the city's strong economy, combined with the intense pressure to expand or rebuild public buildings on the periphery of the site. One is the Javits Center, just north of the rail yards, which the city and state hope to enlarge. Another is the General Post Office, which is to be renovated as a new Pennsylvania Station.
Added pressure has come from City Hall, where Mayor Rudolph W. Giuliani wants part of the rail yards used for construction of a new sports stadium. The Mayor also supports extending the No. 7 subway line, which now runs along 42d Street as far west as Times Square, to serve as a conduit for both the Javits Center and the new stadium.
"This is not just an intellectual exercise," Mr. Rose said. "There is real interest and demand in this site, and the competition serves as a catalyst to get first-rate designers thinking on an ambitious scale."
All five firms will be invited to attend further discussions with city officials, he said.
Just how significant the results of this particular competition prove to be remains to be seen. Architectural competitions are far from new, particularly in a city as densely developed as New York and as preoccupied with urban design.
And, as was the case with the site of the New York Coliseum, the most ambitious and provocative proposals often have little relationship to what is eventually built.
In the case of the rail yards, the hurdles to development are large and complex, and the interests of builders, residents and government regulators are bound to conflict.
"We definitely are not interested in a stadium, and we don't want this corridor developed in a way that creates a barrier across the West Side of Manhattan," said Leni Schwendinger, president of the Hell's Kitchen Neighborhood Association, one of several groups that have promised to fight any development plan that seems too immense.
Debate over the future of the rail yards site is bound to be intense, because much of the land is publicly owned, having been subdivided over more than 50 years among the city, the state and the Metropolitan Transportation Authority.
The competition underscores the intense interest surrounding the rail yard site among architects, who consider it one of the world's most important big-city tracts of underdeveloped land.
According to the organizers of the event, the finalists were chosen from 110 firms in Europe, Asia, Africa and the United States.
All five designs will be on display for a month starting in October in Grand Central Terminal.
Among the other proposals was one by the New York City firm of Jesse Reiser and Nanako Umemoto of Reiser & Umemoto RUR Architecture P.C., calling for a huge glass roof atop a public commons, which could accommodate up to 90,000 people for cultural events. That plan would also include three new hotels.
Ben van Berkel and Caroline Bos, of Van Berkel & Bos Studio, based in Amsterdam, proposed a vast structure that would house a larger and more technologically advanced Javits Center.
That plan would also provide a new pier in the Hudson River and gardens along the riverfront.
A California firm, Morphosis, headed by Thom Mayne of Santa Monica, Calif., proposed a large new structure extending along the spine of the rail yards site, which like Mr. Eisenman's plan would support a landscaped public area on its roof.
Mr. Mayne would use the part of the site just west of the post office and the new Pennsylvania Station for a new Madison Square Garden.
Perhaps least expected among the proposals was that of Cedric Price, of the London firm of Cedric Price Associates. He would take advantage of the breezes off the river to power his "wind-blinkers" and preserve the largest remaining open tract of industrial land in Manhattan.
"There are many situations in which to be systematically late is to be systematically wrong," Mr. Price said in the somewhat oblique text accompanying his entry. In other words, he seemed to be saying, enough damage has been done to the site already. Leave it alone.
