HEADLINE: PLANNING FOR A SURPLUS: THE OVERVIEW;
CLINTON OUTLINES PLAN FOR SURPLUS; G.O.P. SUSPICIOUS

BYLINE: By RICHARD W. STEVENSON 

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, June 28

 BODY:
President Clinton laid out his proposal today to use the fast-growing Federal budget surplus to eliminate the national debt while shoring up Social Security and Medicare, and left open the possibility of a deal with the Republican-led Congress over its demand for big tax cuts.
The plan could set the stage for a grand compromise with Republicans encompassing new spending on the military and social programs as well as tax cuts and the fate of the two giant retirement-security programs. Or it could just as easily lead to a hardening of positions and a bitter struggle extending into the 2000 elections. 
 Republicans, who plan to begin a push in the next few weeks to pass a $775 billion, 10-year tax cut in the face of a veto threat, welcomed the news of a growing surplus and said they were heartened by some aspects of Mr. Clinton's proposal. But they remained suspicious of Mr. Clinton, who has repeatedly bested them in budget politics and like them is torn between a desire for a bipartisan deal and his party's need for potent campaign issues.
"We're interested in deeds, not words," said Senator Trent Lott of Mississippi, the Republican leader.
Predictions of budget trends have as often as not proved wildly inaccurate, and the current rosy estimates, essential to the debt-reduction plan, rest partly on assumptions that the economy will avoid any prolonged downturns. Page A14.
The Congressional Budget Office plans to unveil its own surplus estimates this week, and they are expected to be even larger than the White House numbers disclosed by Mr. Clinton today.
Citing calculations by the White House's Office of Management and Budget, Mr. Clinton said the surplus would be more than 20 percent higher over the next 10 years than projected just five months ago.
From 2000 through 2009, the total surplus is now anticipated to be a cumulative $2.93 trillion, up from an estimate of $2.4 trillion in February, putting more than $500 billion more on the table. Over 15 years, the surplus will be more than $1 trillion larger than previously estimated.
"The surplus is the hard-earned product of our fiscal discipline," Mr. Clinton said this morning on the White House South Lawn before leaving on a fund-raising trip to Connecticut and New York. "We should use it to prepare for the great challenges facing our country -- caring for our parents, caring for our children, freeing our nation from the shackles of debt so that we can have long-term sustained economic prosperity."
In the short run, the new Administration projections show that the Government will reach a milestone next year, when it will not only run a surplus but for the first time since 1960 will spend less than it takes in even without including revenues generated by Social Security taxes.
The $70 billion surplus the Government posted last year and the estimated $99 billion it is running this year are completely accounted for by revenues that are technically earmarked for the retirement system but that in practice go into a pot with all other tax receipts.
The non-Social Security surplus next year will be tiny in the context of a $1.9 trillion budget -- about $5 billion -- but it will grow rapidly, and will total more than $1 trillion over the next 10 years, according to the White House projections.
In the long run, the projections foresee a considerably more far-reaching accomplishment: the elimination by 2015 of the Federal debt held by the public, which now stands at $3.6 trillion and has long hurt the economy by forcing up interest rates and requiring a big chunk of tax revenues to go for interest payments.
"We have now cut up Washington's credit card," Mr. Clinton said. "Now we can pay off the debt."
Mr. Clinton's basic approach was to dangle the possibility of agreeing to tax cuts and new spending, but only if Congress goes along with his new ideas for Social Security and Medicare, both of which face severe financial problems in coming decades as the baby boom generation retires.
The President did not formally propose lifting the tight spending caps that have left Congress struggling to pass appropriations bills this year. But in a nod to his own party's priorities, Mr. Clinton said he wanted to spend $156 billion of the surplus over the next decade on domestic programs including education, child care and health, a sum that would require the caps to be revised or eliminated in the next few years.
Administration officials said they would concentrate on passing Mr. Clinton's proposal for tax incentives intended to stimulate more retirement savings among low- and middle-income families. But when asked if he could support additional tax cuts, Mr. Clinton left the door open to compromise.
"Within the framework of achieving these objectives, obviously I'll be working with the Congress to achieve them," he said.
The President is to release a detailed plan on Tuesday for overhauling Medicare. He said today that it would include using nearly $800 billion from the surplus over the next 15 years to keep the system of health insurance for the elderly afloat at least until 2025, an improvement of a decade over previous estimates.
But today Mr. Clinton effectively backed off of his previous demand that Social Security's financial health be assured for 75 years, the traditional benchmark for long-term solvency. Instead, Administration officials said that while they would still like to complete an overhaul of Social Security for the long run, Mr. Clinton would be willing to unlock the surplus for tax cuts and new spending as long as Congress and the Administration could make progress on bolstering Social Security's finances.
To achieve that end, Mr. Clinton offered a variant of ideas that both he and members of Congress from both parties have been debating for months. He acceded to demands from Republicans that none of the excess Social Security taxes be available for spending, a shift from his previous proposal that would have allowed some of the Social Security money to go for other purposes in the next few years.
Instead, he proposed that all excess Social Security tax revenues -- more than $3 trillion over the next 15 years -- go to reducing the national debt, rather than being spent, as has been the practice of past Congresses and Administrations for decades. In addition, Mr. Clinton would funnel general tax revenues into Social Security starting in 2011, in an amount equal to the interest saved by reducing the debt over the previous decade -- $107 billion in 2011, rising to $166 billion in 2014.
The plan would insure Social Security's solvency through 2053. If nothing is done, Social Security will run short of money to pay benefits starting in 2034.
Republicans said the new approach appeared to be an improvement over Mr. Clinton's original Social Security proposal earlier this year. But the plan set out by Mr. Clinton today would funnel the new money into Social Security through a mechanism that Republicans have long rejected: having the Social Security trust fund buy stocks and corporate bonds.
While Mr. Clinton's approach would give Social Security tangible assets with which to finance retirement benefits rather than paper promises from the Treasury, it runs afoul of Republican opposition to the idea of Government as an investor in private industry.
"It's a proposal that's doomed to fail from the beginning," said Richard E. May, a former Republican staff director for the House Budget Committee who is now a lobbyist.
News of the improving fiscal picture called to mind 1997, when a budget windfall helped break a political logjam and allowed both parties to agree to a balanced budget plan. In that case, both parties got enough of what they wanted to be able to claim victory.
There is now clearly enough money on the table for the White House and the Republican leadership to work out a deal.
But that prospect makes rank-and-file members of both parties nervous. Conservative Republicans fear that Mr. Clinton will again win a substantial increase in spending, ending their hopes of reducing the size and influence of the Federal Government. Liberal Democrats fear that Mr. Clinton will sell out their concerns about the poor and children in order to chalk up a legacy-enhancing bipartisan budget deal near the end of a Presidency marred by the impeachment trial.
The political calculus is complicated by Mr. Clinton's desire to help the campaigns of Vice President Al Gore and Hillary Rodham Clinton. Eliminating Social Security, Medicare and tax cuts as issues for the next election could take time-tested campaign themes away from Mrs. Clinton, Mr. Gore and Democrats who hope to regain control of the House.
Having huge new sums of money available "makes it easier to get a deal, but it doesn't make it easy," said Rudolph G. Penner, a former director of the Congressional Budget Office who is now a senior fellow at the Urban Institute. "There will still be huge arguments within the parties and between the parties about the proper way to go."

SECTION: Section F; Page 7; Column 1; Dining In, Dining Out/Style Desk 

LENGTH: 1066 words

HEADLINE: WINE TALK;
In France, a Rumble Is Heard: 'Vive Gallo'

BYLINE: By FRANK J. PRIAL 

DATELINE: PARIS

 BODY:
FOR an American winemaker, France is the belly of the beast, a virtually impenetrable market. From Bordeaux to Burgundy, from Champagne to Beaujolais, American wines are about as popular as some new strain of vine-eating bug.
But you wouldn't learn that by talking with Michael Novy, 32, a breezy Chicagoan charged with nothing less than establishing the wines of Ernest and Julio Gallo in France. While his reception has often been about as warm as that accorded the Americans who stormed the Normandy beaches 55 years ago, Mr. Novy is confident that he, and the giant California winery he represents, will prevail. 
 Gallo has had considerable success in the rest of Europe. After little more than a year in Germany, its 1998 sales there were a million cases. Gallo wines also have a modest presence in Belgium, the Netherlands and Scandinavia. And its sales in Britain last year came to some two million cases.
The most popular Gallo labels overseas are Turning Leaf and the Ernest & Julio Gallo Wine Cellars. In European markets, Gallo advertising emphasizes the Gallo family heritage, tapping into the Old World respect for family ties and for elders. In France, striving for a youthful image, the company focuses its advertising on Gina and Matt Gallo, Julio Gallo's grandchildren, the third generation in the business.
Mr. Novy and his team set up shop in France just a year ago in Boulogne-Billancourt, just outside Paris, after studying the market for quite some time. "We were really surprised," he said. "We'd been lead to believe that the French were unalterably opposed to non-French wine. What we discovered was that about 10 percent of all French wine drinkers would like to try something else."
He said he was targeting the 25-to-40-year-old consumer with disposable income, a yen for travel and a taste for American movies in English. Turned away by some of the major French wine distributors, Gallo got a more encouraging response from supermarket chains like Auchan, Casino and Carrefour.
Not too many years ago, poor vintages and wines from marginal regions were dismissed by Frenchmen as "supermarket wines." Now, some of the most prestigious winemakers, including famous Bordeaux chateaus and small Burgundy estates, are not unhappy seeing their labels on supermarket shelves. Which has not always made things easier for Mr. Novy and Gallo. Some supermarkets have ordered him to remove his wines after only a few days on display because they weren't selling.
Mr. Novy said Gallo's immediate goals were modest. Fewer than 2,000 cases were sold in France last year, and he hopes to sell about 5,000 cases in 1999. Not much for a company that produces some 75 million cases of wine and spirits a year. But Mr. Novy is undeterred. "It will take us 5 to 10 years" to see a return on investment, he said.
Gallo may be the best-known American winemaker trying to open up the French market, but there are others. American wine exports last year totaled about $530 million, mostly to Canada. Exports to France totaled only about $8 million.
If France is a particularly tough market to crack, the reason has more to do with economics than chauvinism. Even though the French drink more wine than anyone else -- more than 16 gallons a person a year, compared with just under 2 gallons a person in the United States, according to Impact, a trade publication -- wine consumption in France has fallen steadily since the 1950's. Recent figures show that more than half of all French adults drink no wine at all. Each year, millions of gallons of surplus French wine are converted to alcohol and burned.
The rare Bordeaux wines that sell for astronomical prices and make headlines account for only 2 percent of Bordeaux's production. A powerful, resourceful competitor like Gallo is understandably viewed with alarm there and in other parts of France. Not by Chateau Lafite-Rothschild, perhaps, but by dozens of major producers of average table wines.
What's more, the French image of American wine could change rapidly. Since a now-historic blind tasting in Paris in 1976, when French experts preferred some relatively unknown California wines to some of the most famous Bordeaux, French winemakers have had considerable respect for American wines. Young French winemakers regularly apprentice at California wineries, and it's no secret that many prominent Bordeaux wine producers now make their wines in the less tannic, earlier-maturing California style. Many other winemakers, particularly in the south of France, have adopted the American practice of identifying wines by their grapes, rather than by the place from which they come.
In commenting on Gallo's initial moves into the French market, the newspaper Le Monde noted that Gallo, the largest winery in the world, has a longstanding relationship with Wal-Mart, the largest retailer in the world, and that the two were working together in Germany. Wal-Mart recently acquired Asda Group P.L.C., Britain's third-largest supermarket chain, which can only strengthen Gallo's position there. Wal-Mart has as yet no position in France, but the moves in Germany and Britain leave little doubt that Gallo intends to be a major participant in the wine business in Europe.
"We are here for the long term," Mr. Novy said, and it's evident that Gallo has the means to be patient. The company had an estimated $1.2 billion in sales in 1998, which, Le Monde noted, is a little more than was sold by the entire Bordeaux wine exporting business.
 
TASTINGS
Rich, but Without a Price to Match
 
Danzante Sangiovese 1997, Le Marche, Italy. About $10.
IT'S about time we all got a break, and here it is: a really delicious Italian wine, widely distributed and at a great price. This is yet another fruitful result of the combined talents of the Robert Mondavi Winery in California and the Frescobaldi family in Tuscany. This one, from the point of view of the average wine buyer, really makes sense.
The sangiovese grape is usually associated with Tuscany and Chianti wine, of which it is the chief component. But good sangiovese can be found all over north and central Italy. The Marches, the coast region east of Tuscany and Umbria, produces excellent wines from the grape, and this is one of them. It's a rich wine with lots of fruit, and it's ready to drink now. "Danzante," by the way, is Italian for "dancing."

HEADLINE: How Not to Spend the Surplus

BYLINE: By Leon E. Panetta; Leon E. Panetta served as President Clinton's chief of staff, director of the Office of Management and Budget and chairman of the House Budget Committee.

DATELINE: CARMEL VALLEY, Calif.

 BODY:
My father, an Italian immigrant, lived through the Great Depression and, like countless others, never forgot its lessons. His advice was born of that experience: "Never take the good times for granted; always save something for the future because you just never know what can happen."
Washington would do well to take that same advice today as it debates what to do with the surplus. 
 This week, it was announced that the projected budget surplus will exceed all expectations: $99 billion in 1999, $142 billion in 2000. In 10 years, it's estimated, there will be a surplus of $2.926 trillion; in 15 years, the figure will be a whopping $5.935 trillion.
Of course, this is a proud achievement. The combination of tough budget plans and a booming economy has produced results far beyond anyone's projections. And the temptation to spend this bounty is understandable. But it is also dangerous.
Remember that not so long ago the nation was looking at endless annual deficits totaling $300 billion per year. And remember, too, that a balanced budget was not achieved with a quick fix, but with political leadership and courage. President George Bush paid a political price for agreeing to the 1990 budget agreement that raised taxes. President Clinton lost a Democratic Congress in part because of the one-vote margin that passed his 1993 economic plan.
The current debate is over whether part of the surplus should be used for tax cuts, as Republicans want, or to shore up Social Security, Medicare and other domestic programs, as the Democrats urge. But if there is a lesson to be learned from the last 30 years of deficit spending, it is that this hard-won surplus should not be spent now; it should be saved for the future. The reasons are clear:
There is no surplus yet. While the Social Security portion of the budget enjoys a surplus of $123.6 billion this year, the rest of the Federal budget is still in the red, to the tune of $24.8 billion. It seems a little presumptuous to spend the surplus when the non-Social Security portion of the budget has not yet realized even $1 of surplus money.
What about the national debt? The years of annual deficits have ballooned the debt to more than $5 trillion, with $3.72 trillion of that debt held by the public. The net interest being paid by taxpayers in 1999 is $229.4 billion. In 1979, the ratio of total debt held by the public to gross domestic product was 25.7 percent. In 1993, it was 50.2 percent. Today, even with the projected surpluses, it is still 44.3 percent. President Clinton is right to argue that the debt should be significantly reduced before the surplus is spent on anything else.
The baby boom generation is getting older. In nine years, the baby boom generation, 76 million Americans, will start retiring. Both Social Security and Medicare will be under serious financial stress. With those uncertainties, it makes more sense to protect the surplus rather than spend it now on new or increased benefits.
The economy remains strong but unpredictable. There is no question that the nation is enjoying the strongest economy in its history. Growth in the last quarter of 1998 was 6 percent, and it is continuing at 4 percent in the first quarter of this year. The economy has outperformed every benchmark. But the Clinton Administration's economic update projects a reduced growth rate and an increased unemployment rate over the next two years. While this may not affect the surplus, it tells us that the economy will probably slow down. And history tells us that it doesn't take much to adversely affect our economy; a sudden increase in the price of oil, a surge of inflation, a major strike, an international financial crisis can suddenly send economic forecasts into a spin. None of this, of course, may happen, but why not plan for the possibility?
Regardless of the economic arguments, it is fair to ask whether the current political atmosphere in Washington is conducive to producing the kind of budget agreement that the nation deserves. The 2000 campaign is already well under way. The Republican campaign strategy is to push for a large tax cut, and the Democratic strategy is to push for spending $156 billion of the surplus on education, child care and health.
Indeed, even with the surplus, the President and the Congress have so far been unable to agree on a Federal budget this year. Congress may very well be headed toward another Government shutdown in October, because few, if any, appropriations bills will be passed.
If decisions about the surplus are made under the threat of a shutdown or while the parties are worried about their own political survival, the result is unlikely to be in the nation's interest. Neither side seems prepared to engage in the serious negotiation necessary to achieve a balanced agreement. This climate could change, but the bitter partisanship in this Congress is not something you want to bet the nation's surplus on.
For all these reasons, it would be better, for now, to leave the surplus alone and do what my father advised: "Save something for the future because you just never know what can happen."

HEADLINE: The Gasoline-Powered Raise

BYLINE: By Robert H. Frank; Robert H. Frank, a Cornell University economist, is the author of "Luxury Fever" and the co-author of "The Winner-Take-All Society."

DATELINE: ITHACA, N.Y.

 BODY:
What's a business to do when it can't hire and retain enough qualified workers? Economists, who have been described as often wrong but never in doubt, have a ready answer: simply offer higher salaries!
But at least some employers now appear to have discovered a far more effective tactic. For example, Arcnet, a wireless telecommunications company in Holmdel, N.J., figures to slash its recruiting and training costs by more than half with its recent offer of a "free" BMW sedan to every employee with at least one year of service. Several other companies have reported success with the same offer. 
 The cars are not really free, of course. Each one costs about $9,000 a year in leasing and insurance fees. Employees who get one must declare that amount as additional income to the Internal Revenue Service.
So we're left with a puzzle: if the company had given not the car but an additional $9,000 a year in salary, no one should have been worse off, and at least some should have been better off. After all, any worker who really wanted a BMW could have spent the extra cash to lease one. And although the BMW is a fantastic car, those who happen not to want one would have come out ahead by having $9,000 a year extra to spend on other things.
Why, then, do employers give cars instead of cash? Essentially the same question is raised by ordinary gift exchanges among family and friends. Why give your cousin a necktie he might never wear when you know you could trust him to spend the same money on something he really wants?
Some would answer that giving cash is just too easy, and is hence a less effective way of demonstrating affection than taking the time and trouble to shop for a gift. That explanation might work for small gifts, but it's surely a stretch for luxury cars.
A more promising tack has been suggested by Richard Thaler, an economist at the University of Chicago, who has observed that the best gifts are often things we're reluctant to buy for ourselves. Why, for example, is a man happy when his wife gives him a $1,000 set of titanium golf clubs paid for out of their joint checking account? Perhaps he really wanted those clubs but couldn't quite justify spending so much. Having someone else make the choice allows him to enjoy his new clubs guilt-free.
One attraction of this way of thinking about gift giving is the plausibility of the advice it suggests for gift givers. Consider this thought experiment: Among each of the following pairs of items costing the same amounts, which item would be the more suitable gift for a close friend?
* $20 worth of macadamia nuts (1 pound) or $20 worth of peanuts (10 pounds)?
* A $75 gift certificate for Lespinasse (one lunch) or a $75 gift certificate for McDonald's (15 lunches)?
* $30 worth of wild rice (4 pounds) or $30 worth of Uncle Ben's converted rice (50 pounds)?
* A $60 bottle of Mondavi Reserve Cabernet (750 ml) or $60 worth of Cribari red (10 gallons)?
Most people would say the first item in each pair is almost surely the safer choice.
The same logic may explain why Arcnet and other employers in the fast lane are giving away BMW's. Perhaps you'd find it awkward to tell your Depression-era parents that you'd bought a car costing twice as much as a Toyota Camry. Or you may worry that your neighbors would think you were putting on airs if you bought yourself a new BMW. Or perhaps you've wanted to make such a purchase but your spouse insists on remodeling the kitchen instead.
A gift car from your employer wipes away all these concerns and more. From the company's perspective, an added advantage is that offering a luxury car to all long-term employees would kindle fewer resentments than the alternative strategy -- also recently in the news -- of offering cash signing bonuses only to new recruits.
Is the American labor market headed for a full-fledged barter system? Not likely, because the Arcnet strategy would make little sense for many employers. Burger King owners, for example, probably won't dangle used Ford Escorts the next time they find themselves short of counter help. They and other employers of unskilled labor are more likely to stick with the time-honored strategy of paying higher wages.
But in-kind compensation is almost sure to spread further among employers of the most highly skilled workers. It is these employers who have faced consistent labor shortages, and it's the people they are trying to hire and retain who have been so responsive to the new luxury offerings.
As the trend unfolds, the gifts are likely to change. The new strategy depends on the gift's ability to generate excitement. And excitement, always and everywhere, depends on context. Most readers were astonished when the young lawyer in John Grisham's 1991 novel "The Firm" was given a new BMW as a signing bonus, and the same tactic attracts media attention even today. As more and more companies adopt this tactic, however, it will inevitably lose much of its punch.
To achieve the same impact, employers will have to raise the stakes. Can anyone doubt that talented consultants and software developers will eventually snub any employer who dares offer less than a Porsche 911?

HEADLINE: Consumer Confidence Is Highest Since '68

BYLINE: Bloomberg News 

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, June 29

 BODY:
Consumer confidence rose in June to the highest level in more than three decades, suggesting to some that the Federal Reserve might raise interest rates more than once to rein in spending.
The Conference Board's index of consumer confidence rose to 138.4 this month from 137.7 in May, the research group said today. June's reading was the highest since October 1968. "It looks like we are getting to the point where euphoria is creeping into consumers' outlook," said Vincent Boberski, senior economist at the brokerage firm Dain Rauscher in Minneapolis. 
 Still, a Commerce Department report showed that higher borrowing costs might be starting to cool demand in at least one part of the economy. Sales of new single-family homes fell 5.1 percent in May, to a seasonally adjusted annual rate of 888,000 units, reversing much of the 6.4 percent increase in April.
The message from the housing report is that rising mortgage rates may be keeping home buyers at bay, analysts said.
Consumer confidence is closely watched because spending accounts for about two-thirds of the gross domestic product. Consumer spending bounded ahead in the first quarter at the fastest pace in 11 years, bolstered by rising incomes and stock prices, which set highs in May.
The Federal Reserve chairman, Alan Greenspan, suggested to members of Congress two weeks ago that Fed policy makers might raise interest rates pre-emptively, even with few signs of an acceleration of inflation. While consumer prices rose at a 2.6 percent rate in the first five months of the year, up from a 1.6 percent increase last year, that was still lower than the 3 percent yearly average increase in the Consumer Price Index so far in the 1990's.
The Federal Open Market Committee, the Fed's policy panel, began a two-day rate meeting today, and many expect it to raise the overnight bank-lending rate by a quarter of a percentage point, to 5 percent. It would be the first increase in interest rates since March 1997.

HEADLINE: INTERNATIONAL BUSINESS;
Embattled Bankers Trust Chairman Resigns

BYLINE: By EDMUND L. ANDREWS 

DATELINE: FRANKFURT, June 29

 BODY:
Frank Newman resigned today as chairman of the Bankers Trust Corporation, apparently isolated and powerless after Deutsche Bank A.G. acquired his company for $10 billion early this month.
"I concluded that the role originally envisioned for me will not be necessary," Mr. Newman said today in a brief written statement. "I thought it made sense for me to step aside at this time." 
 The statement said Mr. Newman would pursue "other projects" after his departure on Wednesday, but did not elaborate. Deutsche Bank officials said he was not available.
Though Deutsche Bank and Mr. Newman's statement both portrayed the resignation as amicable, it came after Deutsche Bank retreated from a promise to give Mr. Newman a seat on its management board.
Mr. Newman, 57, had come under increasing criticism after Bankers Trust pleaded guilty to illegally diverting $19.1 million in funds from dormant accounts between 1994 and 1996.
Though Mr. Newman did not take over Bankers Trust until after the practice was stopped, the guilty plea was a surprise to Deutsche Bank.
Deutsche was deeply embarrassed by a small stock scandal three years ago at Morgan Grenfell, the British investment bank it had acquired in the early 1990's. To avoid a repeat, Deutsche Bank executives established much tighter control over Bankers Trust and indeed are doing away with the name itself.
Under the original takeover plan announced last November, Mr. Newman was to become co-chairman of Deutsche Bank's corporate banking and investment banking division, known as Global Corporates and Institutions. He was also supposed to become a member of Deutsche Bank's management board.
But after Bankers Trust's guilty plea, some of Deutsche's top executives rebelled against giving Mr. Newman a board seat. When the two banks formally completed the sale on June 4, they omitted any mention of the matter.
Mr. Newman will not leave penniless. His contract with Bankers Trust guaranteed him salary and bonuses of roughly $55 million, and Deutsche Bank may pay significantly more in order to avoid an embarrassing public fight.
Deutsche Bank, Germany's biggest bank, wants to use Bankers Trust as a springboard into the United States and to strengthen its position in investment banking.
Mr. Newman, a former Deputy Treasury Secretary, was hired in late 1995 to clean up Bankers Trust after a series of scandals at the bank involving high-risk derivative securities. He orchestrated several acquisitions, including that of Wolfensohn & Company, a small investment bank, and BT Alex. Brown & Sons, the Baltimore brokerage firm with a reputation in high-technology stocks.
But Bankers Trust suffered heavy losses as a result of Russia's economic collapse, and it was in desperate need of a strong new partner.
The German bank has thus far installed Bankers Trust executives in several senior jobs. Yves de Balmann and Mayo A. Shattuck 3d have been placed in charge of Deutsche Bank's global investment banking operations. Mary Cirillo will be in charge of the bank's large business in custodial services for securities.
Deutsche Bank budgeted $400 million to $500 million for incentive bonuses aimed at retaining key Bankers Trust executives.
But Deutsche Bank's chairman, Rolf-Ernst Breuer, made it clear in November that he was taking firm control. "This is an acquisition," he said, not a merger.
As plans moved forward during the last six months, Mr. Newman had become increasingly isolated. Deutsche Bank installed John Ross, an American who had formerly run its Asian operations, in charge of North American operations.
Though Mr. Newman was supposed to share power over the Global Corporates business with Josef Ackermann, a Deutsche Bank board member, he remained outside the lines of real operational authority.
Mr. Newman's isolation increased when Deutsche Bank refused to name him to its management board, known as the Vorstand. After Mr. Newman and Mr. Breuer met in Paris last week, the two men agreed to part ways by the end of the month.
Investors were apparently pleased about Mr. Newman's placid departure. Deutsche Bank shares rose 1.73 euros, or 3 percent, to 58.47 euros.

HEADLINE: Business Travel;
As U.S. air farescontinue to climb, more corporate travelers are turning to the Internet.

BYLINE: By Jane L. Levere 

 BODY:
AS air fares continue their seemingly never-ending climb, more business travelers are going to the Internet to find discounts, a practice that is creating problems for some employers.
According to a survey released yesterday by American Express, the average price paid in April by United States corporations for an airline ticket for business travel reached a seven-year high, partly a result of two across-the-board fare increases by major carriers in the first quarter. As calculated by American Express, the average round-trip fare paid by business travelers in April was $600, up 1 percent from March and up 5 percent from April 1998. 
 "After a year of minimal increases, average air fare tabs are rising faster," said Rusty Carpenter, vice president of American Express Consulting Services Group. "To minimize the damage to travel budgets, companies are turning to a new line of defense: Web-based booking systems. Because they display all kinds of fares, including discounted fares negotiated by corporations, users are more apt to book a lower air fare than when they make reservations over the phone."
Business travel agency giants like American Express and Rosenbluth Travel, as well as companies like Oracle, offer corporations customized Web-based booking systems, which allow employees to make their own reservations using the corporation's negotiated rates and following the parameters of the corporation's travel policy.
Employees of many companies are, however, increasingly working around these systems and going on line themselves to find Internet discounts; the air fares they find are often lower than those negotiated by their employers. In fact, in an informal survey taken last month of members of the National Business Travel Association, which represents 1,300 United States corporate travel managers, almost three-fourths of the managers said this practice was their No. 1 problem, because it interfered with their cost-control efforts.
"The real problem for travel managers is that they've negotiated rates with one or two carriers and they have to produce volume to maintain the fare agreements," said Norman Sherlock, the association's executive director. "At some companies, if an individual goes to the Internet for a fare outside the negotiated rate, the individual won't be reimbursed on his expense account." Mr. Sherlock declined to identify the companies.
Mr. Sherlock also said the association was petitioning United States airlines to put their Internet discounts on computerized reservation systems to provide direct access to them for corporate travel managers and their employees. Displaying these fares on computerized reservation systems would, however, significantly increase demand, which airlines would find unacceptable.
To get around this, Mr. Sherlock said the systems were developing special software that could restrict access to Internet fares to specific groups like corporate travel managers. He said Galileo, the system partly owned by UAL's United Airlines and other carriers, already had created such a program.
Sam Buttrick, aviation analyst for Paine Webber, suggested that softness in United States airlines' domestic revenues -- up only 1 percent in the last eight months, despite a robust economy -- could stem, in part, from greater use by travelers of the Internet. "The Internet has resulted in a shift in buying power and knowledge to consumers and corporations," he said. "Consumers and businesses have much better pricing information now, the best they've ever had."
He also said that although in some cases it might make sense for a company's employees to pay a negotiated rate, even if they find a cheaper fare on line, "in most cases people are better off taking the lowest fare." Customized booking systems that give users Internet access are a good compromise solution in situations like this, he added.
 
Special Fares
With the two busiest travel months starting tomorrow, many of the nation's airlines are offering special E-mail fares, Fourth of July holiday sale fares or double frequent-flier miles for full-fare tickets in coach, business class or first class.
Delta Air Lines started the bonus miles sweepstakes when it offered a 70 percent premium. U S Airways countered with double miles, and AMR's American Airlines, Continental Airlines, Trans World Airlines and United Airlines followed.
The holiday fares, which Delta, American and United are offering, generally require departure this Friday through Sunday and return by Tuesday. In each case, restrictions apply.
Delta's bonus offer was intended to call attention to its 70th anniversary as a passenger airline. On June 17, 1929, a five-passenger Delta monoplane took off from Love Field in Dallas, arriving in Jackson, Miss., five hours later after stops at the Louisiana cities of Shreveport and Monroe. Today Delta averages 2,600 flights a day.
 
On the Town
More than 50 New York City cultural institutions -- including museums, performing arts centers, botanical gardens and zoos -- are offering special one-hour tours that can be slotted into a business traveler's schedule.
Available through Sept. 30 and produced by the New York Convention and Visitors Bureau, the tours include a walk through the South Street Seaport district and a visit to WNET, the New York area public television station.
All tours are free, though there is an admission fee to most attractions.

HEADLINE: Microsoft Offer On Net Service

BYLINE: Bloomberg News 

DATELINE: REDMOND, Wash., June 29

 BODY:
The Microsoft Corporation, the world's largest software maker, is offering new subscribers who sign up for its MSN Internet access service a $400 credit to buy a personal computer.
The offer is available through Staples Inc. stores and Microcenter stores, an MSN customer service representative said. Microcenter is a small consumer-electronics chain based in Ohio. Consumers must sign up for MSN for three years as part of the promotion, according to a Staples salesman. MSN's ISP service costs $19.95 a month. 
 Microsoft's MSN Internet service has struggled to gain new subscribers and catch up to America Online Inc., the world's largest on-line service with more than 17 million members. MSN, which has an estimated 1.5 million to 1.8 million subscribers, is ranked No. 3 behind AT&T's WorldNet Internet access service and America Online, according to the market researcher Forrester Research Inc.
The MSN special "is a decent deal," said Bruce Kasrel, a Forrester analyst. But, he added, "It's not enough to put them over the top."
Mr. Kasrel said the three-year commitment to MSN service may make consumers wary because they do not like to be locked into contracts.

HEADLINE: Scudder to Cut Jobs in Shift to Internet

BYLINE: By Dow Jones 

 BODY:
The Scudder group of no-load mutual funds plans to close offices and lay off up to 120 employees in an effort to improve sales by shifting emphasis to the Internet.
Scudder Kemper Investments told employees in its Scudder no-load funds unit on Monday that the firm would close Scudder retail offices in Boston, New York, San Francisco, Chicago and Boca Raton, Fla., and two of its four customer service telephone centers, in Salem, N.H., and Boston. The layoffs represent about 2 percent of Scudder Kemper's 5,000 employees. 
 In recent years, Scudder Kemper, a unit of the Swiss insurer Zurich Group, has struggled to reconcile its two fund operations: the Scudder funds, which are sold directly to consumers, and the Kemper funds, which are distributed through stockbrokers and other intermediaries.
Many of Scudder's no-load funds have performed poorly, especially its value-oriented funds, which seek to buy undervalued shares. The Scudder and Kemper stock funds averaged a negative 0.28 percent return in the 12 months through March 31, compared with Fidelity Investments' 17.92 percent gain, according to the Kanon Bloch Carre consulting firm in Boston.

HEADLINE: INTERNATIONAL BUSINESS;
Latin Growth Weakens, With Joblessness Up

BYLINE: By CLIFFORD KRAUSS 

DATELINE: BUENOS AIRES, June 29

 BODY:
Despite brighter-than-expected economic performances in Brazil and Mexico, a spate of new economic data around Latin America in recent days suggests that the recession gripping the region will be deeper and more prolonged than most economists had expected.
Plunging regional trade and retail sales, persistently low commodity prices and growing jitters over the financial health of several Latin American governments -- all complicated by fears of an increase in United States interest rates -- have led forecasters to lower their projections of growth and recovery well into the next year. 
 Unemployment is rising in most of the region's countries and consumers are spending less.
Over the last month, the currencies of Brazil, Chile, Ecuador and Venezuela have come under pressure. Colombia devalued the peso on Monday by about 10 percent. Argentina, Colombia, Ecuador and Venezuela have reported lower-than-expected economic growth in recent days. Repeated cuts in interest rates have not turned around the Chilean economy, as many economists had expected.
The Argentine Government's cost of borrowing has sharply increased amid investor concern over an expanding budget deficit.
In Peru, there are growing indications that unpaid debts are jeopardizing the health of several major banks.
"We thought the effects of the Asian crisis, the Russian virus and even the Brazilian crisis would have been milder and shorter," said Jose de Gregorio, an economist at the University of Chile. "With the exceptions of Mexico and Brazil, all the countries are doing worse than expected."
In a report released on Monday, Bear, Stearns lowered its 1999 growth estimates for all countries in the region except Mexico and Brazil. It projected that the gross domestic product for the region this year, adjusted for inflation, would shrink by one-tenth of a percent. Earlier, many economists had forecast 2 or 3 percent expansion.
Bear, Stearns projected 3.5 percent growth for the region in 2000, which is considered a relatively modest rebound in light of the sharp contraction of trade and the flight of foreign investment this year as well as in 1998. "Latin America is under pressure as it prepares for a Fed rate increase in the face of falling prices in much of the world," the report said. "Exchange rate weakness is likely in 1999 for most countries, with sizable devaluations possible in Colombia and Venezuela."
The report predicted that despite a growing demand from the United States for South American goods, exports would fall 5 percent from already-weak 1998 levels.
Mexico has been an exception to the trends, with the Government last week forecasting that the economy would grow 3 percent this year, largely because of expanding trade with the United States. Mexico's ties to the strong American economy have also helped strengthen confidence in the peso -- which settled in New York today at 9.425 to the dollar, up from 9.45. There is confidence, as well, that Mexican borrowing rates will fall further. And stocks have had a stellar year.
Despite Mexico's growth, Bear Stearns estimated that the purchasing power of the region as a whole would contract by $173 billion this year.
Brazil is the anchor of the South American economy, and its performance in recent months has also been better than expected, considering the financial crisis that erupted with devaluation there in January. Interest rates have come down without pumping up inflation, while temporary cuts in consumption taxes have helped increase production and sales in the important auto industry.
In February, economists predicted a contraction in Brazil of up to 4 percent this year; now, Brazilian output is expected to contract by 1 percent. Still, the economists remain cautious.
The deterioration elsewhere has in recent weeks become especially noteworthy in the so-called Southern Cone -- where the Argentine, Chilean and Uruguayan economies are suffering their worst downturns in several years. Chile announced this week that May unemployment had risen to 9.8 percent from 8 percent in April, well above what Government and private economists had predicted.
Argentina recently recalculated the decline in first-quarter output to 3 percent from 2.8 percent, and this week officials acknowledged that unemployment -- now 14.2 percent, or two points above the already steep level last October -- could exceed 15 percent in coming months.
The human costs of this deepening recession in Argentina were suggested in dismal quarterly sales figures reported last week by the nation's supermarkets, with declines of 15 percent or more in such staples as powdered milk, beef and sausage compared with a year earlier. And Argentine car sales have fallen 31 percent in the first five months of the year from the corresponding period of 1998.

HEADLINE: INTERNATIONAL BUSINESS;
Latin-European Meeting Ends With Vague Commitment

BYLINE: By LARRY ROHTER 

DATELINE: RIO DE JANEIRO, June 29

 BODY:
The first summit meeting of Latin American and European nations ended here today with leaders of the world's second- and third-largest trading blocs unable to agree on a timetable or an agenda for negotiations for a free-trade agreement. They opted instead for a much vaguer "strategic partnership" and a commitment to "encouraging the increase and diversification of trade."
In a joint communique, the 15-member European Union and Mercosur, the South American customs union comprising Argentina, Brazil, Paraguay and Uruguay, set November 1999 as the start of meetings to consider proposals "regarding the definition of the structure, methodology and calendar" of future talks. But formal negotiations on tariff issues are not scheduled to begin until at least July 2001, and no deadline was set for their conclusion. 
 That modest commitment fell far short of what advocates of stronger commercial ties between the two blocs had sought. At a news conference here this morning, Antonio Guterres, the Prime Minister of Portugal, said he "had hoped the process would have moved further ahead."
The Mercosur group, with 200 million people and a combined economy of more than $1 trillion, has been pushing Europe to grant more access for its agricultural products like meat, wheat, sugar and oil seeds, over the objections of France and a few other countries. The Europeans, in turn, are insisting the South Americans open up their service sectors further and reduce barriers to imports of automobiles and car parts.
With Mercosur and the rest of Latin America already negotiating a free-trade pact with the United States, "it is necessary to work with urgency," said Mr. Guterres, whose Government will assume the presidency of the European Union in January 2000 and has been one of the strongest advocates of closer ties between Europe and Latin America. At a news conference following the formal closing of the summit meeting, Germany's Chancellor, Gerhard Schroder, speaking for the European bloc, described the two-day meeting here, attended by leaders of 48 countries, as a "historical landmark." He expressed confidence that both regions would act in concert on some issues during the next round of talks at the World Trade Organization this fall.
Mr. Schroder and the president of Brazil, Fernando Henrique Cardoso, along with his Mexican counterpart, Ernesto Zedillo, all made an effort to deny that one of their goals in meeting here was to outflank the United States or create a counterweight to it. Though not invited to the conference, American officials have praised the meeting as a positive step toward freer world trade.
Mr. Cardoso said Europe and Latin America together were seeking to create "a diversity of centers of decision."
But even on those nontrade issues, some negotiators went home frustrated. The president of Chile, Eduardo Frei, could not persuade Britain and Spain to halt judicial proceedings against Gen. Augusto Pinochet, the former dictator of Chile held in Britain pending an extradition request from Spain.
Fidel Castro failed to get the meeting's final declaration to include a condemnation of the Helms-Burton Act, the American law imposing sanctions on foreign investors in Cuba, Britain's Foreign Secretary, Robin Cook, who met with his Cuban counterpart in the highest-level meetings between those Governments in 40 years, lobbied unsuccessfully on behalf of four political prisoners held by Castro's Government.
Nevertheless, Latin American countries were quick to take advantage of the opportunities offered by the meeting to strengthen trade ties among themselves. In an advance toward economic integration within South America, for instance, the host country, Brazil, reached an agreement with the five members of the Andean Pact to reduce or eliminate tariffs on more than 3,000 products.
In addition, Brazil and Venezuela, which have been working quietly to strengthen their electricity and transport links, reached an agreement today to step up cooperation in the oil and natural gas industries.

Trade Warning to Europe
 WASHINGTON, June 29 (Bloomberg News) -- Stuart Eizenstat, nominated to the Treasury Department's No. 2 job, delivered a strong defense for free trade at his Senate confirmation hearing today and sharply criticized European nations that want to ban imports of genetically modified crops.
"The Europeans have an absolute fear, unfounded by any scientific basis," Mr. Eizenstat, Under Secretary of State for Economics and Trade, told the Senate Finance Committee. "They're talking about a moratorium. This would have a severe impact on our agricultural community. It is, in my mind, the single greatest trade threat we face."

HEADLINE: Foreign Medical School Has Hopes Of Hanging Its Shingle on U.S. Soil

BYLINE: By SHARON R. KING 

 BODY:
The idea seemed simple enough.
Ross University, a medical school in the Caribbean island nation of Dominica, would open a branch in the United States -- in Wyoming, to be specific, which lacks a medical school of its own.
Casper, the city chosen for the branch, would get 200 jobs and a projected $10 million a year in economic development. Ross would get a mainland campus for 600 to 1,000 students -- the first for-profit medical school to open in the United States in nearly a century. 
 The plan was embraced by local business leaders and many residents. Gov. Jim Geringer backed the idea, as did the state's Congressional delegation.
But the devil was in the details, and now the project has stalled amid fears that permitting a foreign medical school to operate in the United States would lead to a proliferation of unlicensed and unregulated schools.
The dispute provides a vivid example of the tight hold that American medical schools, their regulators and the American Medical Association maintain on the medical education system. These groups control not only the number of doctors practicing medicine but also which students get an education and where they study.
For 20 years, the Ross campus in Dominica has been educating prospective doctors, many of whom were turned down by medical schools in the United States. The students, primarily Americans, spend about two years in the classroom in Dominica, and complete their medical training with two years of clinical studies at United States hospitals. The total cost is about $84,000, compared with $100,000 for a typical medical school education in this country.
The school's founder and namesake is Robert Ross, an eccentric 80-year-old New Yorker who made his fortune trading commodities like grain and petroleum with the Soviet Union in the 1970's. He established the university in 1979 at the suggestion of an employee whose son had been unable to win admission to a United States medical school. The first class, of 11 students, attended lectures at a motel in Roseau, Dominica's capital.
From such humble beginnings, Ross has grown to serve about 1,700 students. Nine hundred of them study basic science in Dominica; the rest participate in clinical rotations at 40 teaching hospitals in the United States. A separate veterinary school, established in 1983, has about 350 students.
Like other foreign medical schools, Ross is not accredited in the United States. Its only accreditation comes from the medical board in Dominica; graduates must pass rigorous licensing exams to practice medicine in the United States.
The Federation of State Medical Boards of the United States is made up of 69 state and regional medical boards, which license doctors to practice medicine in each state. Most state boards refuse to grant licenses to doctors from unaccredited schools. The group believes that any medical school "existing on United States soil is a U.S. school," said Dale L. Austin, the group's deputy executive vice president.
"A school existing in the U.S. would need to come under accreditation in the U.S." Mr. Austin said.
The United States has 125 accredited medical schools. For more than a decade, the number of students accepted by these schools has remained stable at about 16,000 a year. About 20 percent of the doctors practicing medicine in the United States were educated abroad.
Thus far, no foreign medical school has been able to establish a campus in this country. An effort by Kigezi University in Uganda to establish a branch campus here was recently abandoned after the University of Washington Medical School refused to provide space.
The Liaison Committee on Medical Education, a regulatory agency sponsored by the A.M.A. and the Association of American Medical Colleges that accredits United States medical schools, does not accredit foreign schools.
For United States medical education officials, the solution to the Ross problem is simple. The university should move its charter from Dominica to the United States and seek accreditation as a domestic school.
"We support the existing accreditation system," said Wendy Curran, executive director of the Wyoming Medical Society. "How do we address such medical education that plans to operate outside of the existing quality standards?"
The American Medical Association concurs. "Any medical school -- whether in Casper, Wyo., or anywhere else in the country -- needs to provide assurances that the education of its students is comparable to the 125 established medical schools now accredited in the U.S.," the association said in a statement. Last week, at its annual meeting in Chicago, it voted to warn state medical societies that branch campuses of foreign medical schools could not be accredited.
But Mr. Ross refuses to move his school and seek domestic accreditation on the ground that the university provides a lift for the economy of Dominica. And despite the A.M.A.'s opposition, Ross's administrators and its supporters in Wyoming are moving ahead with their plans.
Mr. Ross says he is willing to put $35 million of his own money into the venture. The Casper Area Economic Development Alliance has bought 32 acres of land for the school; 60 additional acres have been donated. Plans call for 600 to 1,000 students to be enrolled for four semesters of basic medical education. A fifth semester, before clinical rotations, would be spent in Dominica.
"We figured it would be a pretty controversial process," said George B. Howley, president of the alliance. But, he added, "we're all excited about bringing a medical school to Casper."
But as Mr. Ross made clear in a recent interview, nothing is certain. "Wyoming students must have the same privileges as if they attended the whole five semesters in Dominica," he said. "If I cannot get those assurances, I am going to abort the deal."
"We've been fighting the establishment for 20 years," he went on. "We've come a long way, but I'm not going to fight city hall again."

HEADLINE: Immigrants Help Defeat L.I. Bill Banning Street Job Markets

BYLINE: By JOHN T. McQUISTON 

DATELINE: RIVERHEAD, N.Y., June 29

 BODY:
To the cheers and applause of nearly 100 Latino immigrant workers and their families, the Suffolk County Legislature rejected a bill today that would have banned day laborers from the roadside job markets they use to find work.
"It was a solid victory for us and a wonderful way for the workers to discover how democracy works," said the Rev. Allan B. Ramirez, of the Brookville Reformed Church, who helped the immigrants oppose the legislation. The vote was 7 to 4, with two abstentions. 
 Immigrant workers, chiefly from Central America and Mexico, have made a practice of gathering on street corners and parking lots between 6 and 8 A.M., where they are hired for the day by landscapers, painters and construction contractors needing day laborers.
The bill was proposed by Legislator Joseph Caracappa of Selden chiefly as a traffic safety measure. He and other supporters contended that the gatherings, sometimes numbering scores of workers, created traffic hazards and disrupted the flow of school buses and other traffic in commercial areas.
Mr. Ramirez, one of three members of the public who spoke in opposition, told the legislators: "This is a county that has always welcomed immigrants. This bill would basically prevent these new immigrants from getting work. They are already the poorest of the poor. It should not be against the law to work."
The workers and some of their children arrived about 8 A.M. aboard buses chartered by Mr. Ramirez and the Workplace Project, an advocacy center in Hempstead, in Nassau County, that fights immigrant issues on Long Island. They carried signs saying "Don't Outlaw Work" and "Traffic Is No Excuse for Unjust Laws" and picketed outside the county center before attending the discussion.
One worker who spoke before the Legislature was a Mexican day laborer who identified himself only as Antonio because he feared that he might be deported. Through a translator, he said he needed to work to support his six children and wife back in Mexico.
The session was also attended by about 20 supporters of the bill, some of whom spoke in favor of the bill, saying it would improve highway safety and protect their "quality of life." The bill had bipartisan support when it was proposed several months ago, but was defeated by bipartisan opposition today. The legislators had been scheduled to vote on the proposal last month, but public opposition had grown enough to force it to be pulled off the agenda and rewritten more narrowly.
After today's defeat, Mr. Caracappa angrily told the other legislators that if someone was killed in a traffic accident as a result of the bill's failure, "their blood will be on your hands." His supporters cheered.
The bill stated that commercial parking areas in the county were for "the benefit of the public, clients, customers, members and occupants of adjoining commercial premises." Using the areas and county roadways "for the solicitation of employment or business in unauthorized locations results in interference with the intended use of the parking area and roadways," the bill said.
The bill would have immediately made it unlawful to hire day laborers at roadside locations from 5 A.M. to 11 A.M., punishable by fines of up to $1,500 and by sentences of up to three months in jail.
Those who wrote the measure believed it would pass a constitutional test, said Paul Sabatino, chief counsel for the Legislature. Proponents were aware that the United States Supreme Court recently struck down a Chicago antiloitering law on the ground that it gave police too much discretion to sweep up innocent people, Mr. Sabatino said, but the Caracappa bill was specific about the activity it outlawed, he added.
Opponents, however, insisted that the law had nothing to do with traffic safety and was racist in intent.
Nadia Marin-Molina, a lawyer and executive director of the Workplace Project, said in an interview, "Such a law would tear apart families, destroy the local economy and undermine the Constitution of the United States."
 After the vote, Mr. Ramirez said in an interview he was pleased that the immigrants had overcome fears of speaking out in a public forum. "Imagine how amazing all this is to these newcomers to America," he said. "This is something they and their children will never forget."

HEADLINE: THE MARKETS: STOCKS & BONDS;
Share Prices Rise Yet Again As Fed Decision Is Awaited

BYLINE: By KENNETH N. GILPIN 

 BODY:
Stocks staged another broad advance yesterday as the Nasdaq composite index and the Standard & Poor's 500-stock index moved close to their record highs.
Despite the solid gains, traders said the increase in prices appeared to have more to do with technical adjustments to portfolios than a shift in investor sentiment. 
 The main market gauges have been under pressure for weeks as high valuations and the threat of a series of interest rate increases by the Federal Reserve have combined to sow seeds of uncertainty.
The Fed governors began two days of meetings yesterday and will announce the outcome this afternoon. The central bank is widely expected to raise rates by one-quarter of a percentage point.
One school of thought holds that with few signs of inflation, the Fed will need to raise interest rates only once this year. But there is a real possibility that this could be the first in a series of moves.
At the end of last week, many had expected trading activity to remain light and convictionless until the Fed disclosed its intentions.
That did not happen.
"It's counterintuitive," Bob Gasser, head of United States equity trading at J. P. Morgan Securities, said of the recent rise in prices. "With valuations where they are and on the eve of what could be more than one increase in interest rates by the Fed, I don't understand."
The Dow Jones industrial average rose for the third consecutive session yesterday, gaining 160.20 points, or 1.5 percent, to 10,815.35.
Chevron, which was raised to a "buy" by analysts at Donaldson, Lufkin & Jenrette, led the blue-chip index higher along with United Technologies and General Electric.
Broader market averages also did well. The S.& P. 500 surged 20.10 points, or 1.5 percent, to 1,351.45, just 16.11 points below its high, reached on May 13. Nasdaq gained 39.67, or 1.5 percent, to 2,642.11; the index is now just 9.94 points below its high on April 26.
Smaller-company stocks continued what has been a strong quarter-long rally. The Russell 2000 index gained 5.47 points, or 1.2 percent, to 454.08.
Trading volume was much higher than in recent sessions.
On the New York Stock Exchange, 820 million shares changed hands. Volume on the Nasdaq market was 952 million shares.
Still, Mr. Gasser and other traders said much of the activity centered on the reconstruction of various market indexes, including the S.& P. 500 and the Russell 2000.
Airtouch Communications, which has been acquired by Vodaphone and will be leaving the S.& P. 500 index today, and Best Buy, which is replacing it, were heavily traded stocks. Airtouch fell 3 11/32, to 107 21/32. Best Buy rose 2 55/128, to 65 15/128.
And some 350 issues, including many Internet stocks, have seen their market capitalizations expand to the point that they no longer qualify to be included in the Russell 2000 index. They will be replaced by others whose market capitalizations fall within a $1.4 billion ceiling and a $180 million floor.
"We spent a lot of time with Airtouch and Best Buy," Mr. Gasser said. "All told, I spent about 90 percent of my time on these index reconfigurations."
Michael Clark, head of United States stock trading at Credit Suisse First Boston, said, "At the end of the day you could see the rebalancing taking place."
"But you don't want to be a hero," at this juncture, he added. "What you really want to see is what the Fed does and how the market reacts to early second-quarter earnings reports. The next couple of weeks will be very important."
Still, there were some fundamental reasons for optimism.
A number of positive announcements from Asian countries, including Japan and South Korea, helped to bolster stocks of companies that do a lot of business in the region, like United Technologies, General Electric, Applied Materials and Intel.
United Technologies, which derived about 17 percent of its sales from Asia last year, gained 3 3/4, to 68 15/16. General Electric rose 3 1/2, to 109 11/16.
Applied Materials, the world's leading maker of semiconductor equipment, said its sales in Southeast Asia might double this year. The stock rose 2 3/4, to 69 1/8. And Intel gained 2 1/4, to 59 1/4.
Drug stocks, which have been the worst-performing group this quarter, regained some ground yesterday.
Pfizer rose 3 13/16, to 104 7/8. Warner-Lambert shot up 4 3/8, to 67 1/8. And Merck gained 1 7/16, to 71 1/2.

Treasury Prices Rise
 By Bloomberg News
 Bond prices rose yesterday as yields neared their highest levels in 19 months and a housing report that was weaker than expected lured investors, a day before the Federal Reserve is expected to raise interest rates to guard against inflation.
"We're at pretty decent yield levels," said Paul Corbin, who invests $600 million of bonds for Brown Investment Advisory and Trust Company in Baltimore. "We'll probably be buying." Mr. Corbin said he was favoring bonds issued by companies and agencies like Fannie Mae, with maturities from 7 to 10 years.
The price of the 30-year Treasury bond rose 12/32, to 88 29/32. The yield, which moves in the opposite direction from the price, fell to 6.06 percent from 6.09 percent on Monday.
Bonds climbed as the Government reported a 5.1 percent drop in sales of new homes in May from April, an indication that higher interest rates are curbing economic growth, said Mitchell Stapley, who oversees $3 billion of bonds at the Kent Funds in Grand Rapids, Mich.
Still, growth is fast enough to justify at least one Fed rate increase, analysts say. The vigor of the economy was highlighted in a separate release today that showed consumer confidence rose to its highest level in more than three decades.

HEADLINE: Turkish Court Sentences Kurdish Rebel Leader to Death

BYLINE: By STEPHEN KINZER 

DATELINE: ISTANBUL, Turkey, June 29

 BODY:
A Turkish security court today sentenced the Kurdish rebel leader, Abdullah Ocalan, to death for his 15-year campaign against the Turkish Army.
Because of the violence that followed Mr. Ocalan's arrest in February, the sentence set off concerns for security around the country and beyond. But there were no reports of violent reaction either in Turkey, where the police were on high alert, or elsewhere. Kurds staged peaceful protests in Bonn, Moscow, The Hague, Paris and other European cities. Others scuffled with the police in Rome and Nicosia, Cyprus. 
 Foreign governments, including the United States, appealed for calm and urged Turkey not to carry out the sentence. Mr. Ocalan's rebel group said it had no plans to order retaliation for the verdict because the Turkish state "still has the power to amend this."
The Kurds in southeastern Turkey began their armed struggle for an independent state 15 years ago, rebelling against Turkey's attempts to suppress their language and culture. Both sides have been accused of atrocities during the war.
The chief of the three-judge panel that pronounced the sentence, Turgut Okyay, said Mr. Ocalan's fighters had "murdered thousands of innocent people, among them babies, children, women and the elderly."
"Ocalan and the organization he ruled carried out terrorist actions aimed at dividing the state," Mr. Okyay said. "His activities constitute a serious, immediate and great danger to the country."
During his trial on the prison island of Imrali, Mr. Ocalan confessed his responsibility for killings but said he had changed his views and is ready to make peace. He said that if his life is spared and the Government grants greater cultural freedom to Kurds, he would ask his followers to give up their guerrilla campaign. But he also warned that if he is hanged the conflict will grow. He said 5,000 of his followers were ready to stage suicide attacks.
"I do not accept this charge of treason," he said in a brief statement before the sentencing. "I believe I have struggled for the unity of this land and for a free life."
Mr. Ocalan, 50, showed no reaction as he was sentenced. Relatives of Turkish war victims who were in the courtroom, together with their lawyers, whooped for joy and began singing the national anthem. Mr. Ocalan rose to his feet, smiled wanly, and left the courtroom.
Conviction of treason carries a mandatory death sentence in Turkey, so the verdict was widely expected. A long appeals process may lie ahead. The case will automatically go to a higher court for review, and if the sentence is upheld there, both Parliament and President Suleyman Demirel must approve it. Military commanders may be able to influence the final decision.
Mr. Ocalan is also expected to appeal to the European Court of Human Rights, whose jurisdiction Turkey has accepted in the past. Such appeals can take a year or longer.
The political wing of Mr. Ocalan's party, the Kurdistan Workers Party, issued a statement in Vienna after the verdict saying that party leaders would "look at this new development from all sides, discuss it, and then implement the strategy and actions as soon as possible."
The group "has regarded this process as illegitimate from the beginning," the statement said. "With this ruling, the Turkish side has ignored the call by the Kurdish side for a peaceful settlement of the war."
Turkish leaders say that the group has served as a tool for foreign powers seeking to divide Turkey. They have repeatedly refused to consider its cease-fire proposals.
In southeastern Turkey, however, many Kurds openly express admiration for Mr. Ocalan and his movement. They complain about their region's low level of economic development and what they perceive as the Government's refusal to recognize their ethnic rights. Those sentiments helped Mr. Ocalan to build his movement into one of the world's most potent guerrilla forces.
They have achieved some victories. When the Kurds took up arms against the Turks, their language was banned on the streets, their music was not allowed to be played and Kurdish-language education was forbidden. Today, Kurdish conversation and music can be heard but the language is still not taught.
During his trial Mr. Ocalan acknowledged that his fighters have carried out all the attacks they were accused of and described some of their activities as "the mistakes of people with a peasant mentality."
Foreign governments and human rights advocates say Turkish security forces burned several thousand villages, tortured Kurds, and supported death squads that killed many suspected Kurdish nationalists.
At the end of last year, Mr. Demirel said that since the armed conflict began in 1984, it had killed 5,302 civilians, 5,555 soldiers and security officers, and 23,638 rebels.
In the days after Mr. Ocalan's capture last February in Kenya, where he had been living under the protection of Greek diplomats, Kurds in Turkey and in many European cities erupted in violent anger. After the death sentence was pronounced today, the German Interior Minister, Otto Schily, urged the 2 million Kurds living there to remain calm.
Comments by American officials reflected a combination of sympathy for the Turks and concern about their treatment of Mr. Ocalan. A White House spokesman, Joe Lockhart, called Mr. Ocalan "an international terrorist who should be brought to justice."
European governments strongly urged that the death sentence not be carried out. Prime Minister Massimo D'Alema of Italy, which denied Mr. Ocalan asylum, warned Turkey against "distancing itself from Europe" and said, "One cannot even think of carrying out this sentence." President Vaclav Havel of the Czech Republic also said he opposed executing Mr. Ocalan.
The Council of Europe, which is dedicated to promoting democracy and human rights on the continent, said none of its 41 member states had executed a prisoner in the last two years, and called upon Turkey, which is a member, to extend the unofficial moratorium on executions that has been in force here since 1984.
"Now there seem to be real opportunities to bring an end to the terrorist actions in the southeast part of Turkey and to establish peace," the council said in a statement.
The council said Mr. Ocalan had been given a fair trial, but the private human rights group Amnesty International disagreed, saying the trial "violated both national law and international standards." The chief human rights officer of the United Nations, Mary Robinson, said "lack of due process" made the verdict "particularly disquieting."
Public opinion in Turkey still seems heavily favorable to carrying out the death sentence. It is shaped in part by relatives of Turkish war victims.
Prime Minister Bulent Ecevit opposes the death penalty on principle, but his main coalition partner, Devlet Bahceli, whose party includes many militant nationalists, has said he will do all he can to assure Mr. Ocalan's execution.
In recent days various intellectuals, including some close to the Government, have been urging that Mr. Ocalan's life be spared. A former Turkish Ambassador to the United States, Sukru Elekdag, said Turkey should use this "historic opportunity" to end the war by commuting the death sentence and recognizing "Kurdish identity."
One prominent newspaper columnist wrote, "Let us not hang him because he will then be a symbol; because Turkey will then be isolated; because a democratic country does not act with vengeance; because terror will then never end; and because Turkey, to remain part of the democratic world, must realize that Kurds are living on their soil and respect their demand to protect their identity."
But Yilidrim Akbulut, the Speaker of the Parliament, reflected what may be the view of many Government leaders.
"This was definitely the necessary punishment," Mr. Akbulut said soon after the verdict was announced. "Those who practice terror must pay the price."

HEADLINE: Harlem Neighbors Split Over Fate of Prison Amid Brownstones

BYLINE: By AMY WALDMAN 

 BODY:
For years, the homeowners of Mount Morris Park, a brownstone-filled historic area in central Harlem, have agitated for the closing of a women's prison in their midst. So they were elated when the budget proposal put forth by Gov. George E. Pataki in January said that the prison would be closed and its residents transferred.
The Mount Morris Park Community Improvement Association, which led the lobbying effort, says that closing the prison is the last piece in the renaissance of the area, where the city is selling off countless abandoned brownstones to spur their rehabilitation. 
 But as budget negotiations in Albany continue, the prison's fate is once again unclear. A coalition of groups that includes some Harlem residents, corrections officers and advocates of the incarcerated have been lobbying the Assembly to keep the prison open. Apparently, they have had some success: in its budget proposal, the Assembly has included operating funds for the prison.
At war are Harlem's competing images of itself. Is it a nascent middle- and even upper-middle-class community that has the right to protect its real estate values and that for too long has been overburdened with social service buildings? Or is it a compassionate community that should not cast out its wayward daughters, and that, like many upstate towns, should welcome the economic benefits of a prison?
The prison, the Parkside Correctional Facility, occupies a narrow brick building at No. 10 Mount Morris Park West. It has 184 beds but is the only totally female work-release prison in the state. Many of the women do not stay there full time, or at all, but report there regularly.
Mike Houston, a spokesman for the state's Department of Correctional Services, said that the prison was being proposed for closing as part of the Governor's effort to trim the number of work-release inmates statewide. He said the women would be transferred to Bayview, another women's center, in Chelsea.
The closing, he said, will save the state $1.4 million. And a spokeswoman for the Governor said he remained committed to shutting the Parkside prison.
The prison abuts a row of late 1800's brownstones that have fallen into such disrepair that they have been called "the Ruins." They are literally shells of the beautiful neo-classical and French Renaissance row houses they once were.
The state and community groups are moving forward with a plan to restore the facades of the buildings and turn them into two- and three-bedroom condominiums, with prices starting at $155,000.
Shelly Cleary, the project director of Community Preservation Corporation Resources, one of the developers, said that ground would probably be broken in the fall.
For Conrad Neblett, an entertainer and producer who has lived in Mount Morris Park for nine years, keeping Parkside on the block defies logic. "Who would buy a condominium next to a prison?" he asked, adding that he also did not like the neighborhood's children looking out their windows to see a prison.
But the prison has its proponents, including an association of black corrections officers worried about the loss of jobs in the region and some residents who feel safer with a prison in their midst.
Mishi Faruqee, the director of the Women in Prison Project for the Correctional Association of New York, which has been lobbying the Assembly, said that Bayview, where the women would be transferred, was already at capacity.
