HEADLINE: Vexing Party, Clinton Backs Year 2000 Bill

BYLINE: By DAVID E. ROSENBAUM

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, June 29

 BODY:
Infuriating many Democrats, the White House today reached a basic agreement with Republicans in Congress on legislation to limit lawsuits arising from Year 2000 computer breakdowns.
Republican leaders said all the main points had been settled and that they hoped Congress would clear the measure for the President's signature this week. The White House agreed that only technical language remained to be worked out. 
 The agreement, aggressively promoted by the computer industry and other business interests and opposed by trial lawyers and consumer groups, generally follows the lines of the bill approved by the Senate two weeks ago. Most Democrats voted against that bill, and President Clinton promised at the time to veto it.
A White House spokesman said relatively minor changes enabled the President to approve the agreement.
As described by lawmakers from both parties, it would set a ceiling on punitive damages against small companies, give all companies a grace period to fix breakdowns before they could be sued, sharply limit class-action suits involving many plaintiffs and protect wealthy companies against being held responsible for computer failures caused by poorer ones.
Many angry Democrats in the Senate and House said the White House had encouraged them to cast a politically risky vote against the bill and then sold them out. "This is a closed-door deal that will hurt consumers," said Senator Patrick J. Leahy, Democrat of Vermont. "Maybe we can dare hope that this will be the last case of triangulation of the 20th century."
"Triangulation" is a term coined by Dick Morris, President Clinton's former political adviser, to mean that the President should not stand with Democrats or Republicans but at points above or between them. Democrats use it nowadays to express their irritation when Mr. Clinton does not follow the party line.
What is called the Y2K problem results from the longtime reliance of computer programs on only the last two digits of a year. When confronted by 00, some computers, software and electronically controlled machinery that have not been updated might misinterpret it as 1900 rather than 2000, substitute another year or simply freeze. A company could suffer financial loss, for example, if its computers broke down and it lost its payroll and billing records.
These are the main aspects of the legislation:
*Companies with fewer than 50 employees could not be assessed more than $250,000 in punitive damages.
*All companies would be given 90 days to fix a problem from the time they were notified before they could be sued.
*No more than 100 plaintiffs could be involved in a class-action suit, and no more than $10 million in damages could be sought.
*Class-action suits would be diverted from state courts to Federal courts, making them more expensive and burdensome for plaintiffs.
*All companies responsible for a computer failure would share the legal liability, and large, wealthy companies could not be forced to pay more than a portion of the damages caused by less affluent or bankrupt businesses.
The central purpose of the measure is to prevent frivolous lawsuits. But critics said there were insufficient protections for consumers who suffered financial loss because of computer failures.
A White House spokesman said President Clinton insisted that the measure be confined to Year 2000 computer problems and did not involve general tort reform.
In addition, the spokesman said small changes involving class-action suits and the proportional liability provisions convinced the President that the bill was worthwhile.
Senator Christopher J. Dodd, Democrat of Connecticut, was instrumental in working out the deal. Insurance companies in Connecticut are especially interested in limiting lawsuits.
Business interests were gleeful. Thomas J. Donohue, president of the United States Chamber of Commerce, which made the legislation its top lobbying priority of the year, called it "a watershed achievement for business and the American economy."
But groups representing consumers were critical. When computer problems develop, "the public is going to be very angry, and small businesses are going to be the most affected," said Joan Claybrook, president of Public Citizen.
Complicating the work on the bill all year has been the desire of both parties to win the political and financial support of wealthy individuals and companies in the computer industry.
A Republican strategist in Congress said his party was not willing to give much in the negotiations because the President was over a barrel: If he vetoed the bill, it would have hurt Vice President Al Gore specifically and Democrats generally in their quest for support in Silicon Valley. By accepting the agreement, in this view, the President gave Republicans what they wanted.
A Democratic strategist added another element. He said Vice President Gore, as he begins his campaign for the Presidency, was eager to rid himself of the taint of financial support from trial lawyers.
The conflict between trial lawyers, who are among Democrats' biggest campaign contributors, and business interests, which are the Republicans' biggest donors, has been a thread running through legislative battles for the last several years. Among other issues, it has arisen on product liability legislation, antismoking measures and bills to regulate managed-care health plans. The Democratic strategist said that for Mr. Gore, the money from the trial lawyers was probably not worth the criticism he faced for taking funds from an unpopular source. But that may not be the case, the strategist said, for Democrats in Congress.
In a careful statement tonight, Mark S. Mandell, president of the Association of Trial Lawyers of America, said, "It is positive that this unique response to a unique situation will be law for only three years and that the legal rights of anyone who suffers a physical injury are preserved."

HEADLINE: $72 BILLION DEAL OF PHONE GIANTS CLEARS BIG HURDLE

BYLINE: By STEPHEN LABATON 

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, June 29

 BODY:
Federal officials said today that they had tentatively agreed to approve the $72 billion acquisition of the Ameritech Corporation by SBC Communications Inc. provided that the new company not only open its market to competition but also offer local telephone service in 30 cities outside its existing region.
Officials said the unusual conditions were intended to drive down phone bills by encouraging competition in markets that have been dominated by one local phone company. SBC and Ameritech executives said today that regulators would impose stiff financial penalties if the new company, which will be the nation's largest local telephone company, failed to offer residential and business telephone service in cities like Miami, Seattle and Boston within 12 months and New York, Washington and Phoenix within 18 months. 
 Although SBC said soon after the merger was announced that it intended to enter those cities, where Federal officials hope it will become a giant competitor, the regulatory agreement speeds up its time frame. It also sets penalties as high as $2.2 billion for the company's failure to offer local telephone service outside its existing territory or sufficiently open its markets to competing phone companies.
The agreement reflects a fundamental shift in the approach regulators have taken in reviewing large mergers such as the Ameritech-SBC deal, which once completed will create a company with about 55 million local phone lines in 13 states, cutting a swath from Michigan to Texas and including Connecticut and California. Regulators have traditionally responded to such proposed corporate behemoths by blocking the deals or requiring divestitures, not expansions, of business units.
Officials acknowledged that they agreed to the 28 conditions in the deal in part because of the heavy criticism of their approval of the merger of Bell Atlantic and Nynex two years ago, a deal that did not have such conditions and that consumer groups say has not significantly increased competition or lowered prices.
The officials also said that the proposed agreement was an effort to fix the unexpected shortcomings of a three-year-old law that was intended to deregulate the telecommunications industry and foster competition but has been unable to force the regional Bells to open their markets. Instead, the law has largely encouraged a wave of corporate mergers and a heavy consolidation of the industry that has raised concerns among some consumer groups.
The tentative accord announced today is the product of three months of intensive negotiations between lawyers for the companies and the staff of the Federal Communications Commission, which were ordered by William E. Kennard, the agency's chairman, to find ways to encourage competition in the local telephone markets. The agency will now give competitors and the public about a month to comment on the proposal before a final vote is taken by the commissioners. State regulators in Indiana and Illinois will also be passing judgment on the acquisition before it can be closed.
Mr. Kennard applauded the work of the staff and indicated that he would most probably approve the deal. It would be highly unusual for a majority of the five commissioners, who were kept apprised of the negotiations, to reject the agreement.
"I am encouraged by SBC and Ameritech's commitment to open their markets to competition and their agreement to suffer stiff penalities if they do not," Mr. Kennard said. "Our paramount goal must be to hasten the day when consumers enjoy the benefits of more competition in local and long-distance phone service."
Executives at the two companies said that they were pleased to have resolved the issues and hoped to be able to complete the merger soon.
"It's fair to say that the merger is now in the home stretch," said James D. Ellis, senior executive vice president and general counsel of SBC. "The conditions are perhaps some of the most wide ranging that any company has ever submitted to the F.C.C. in order to win approval of this type of transaction. They answer fully the concerns that Chairman Kennard first raised last April. They are tough, they are fair and they are certainly enforceable."
But some consumer groups that had urged the F.C.C. to block the proposed acquisition criticized the accord reached today and said it held little hope of actually increasing competition, particularly given the industry's history.
"Rather than block consolidation among monopolies and forcing the companies in the marketplace, we have a massive expansion of monopoly with an enormously complicated set of conditions that will take an army to police," said Gene Kimmelman, the co-director of the Washington office of Consumers Union. "We've never had local telephone competition, and to allow these companies to consolidate is an enormous public policy risk, and I believe a mistaken one."
Mr. Kimmelman also said that to the extent that SBC actually served new cities, it made economic sense for the company to offer service only to the high end of the market, mostly businesses and residents now paying top rates.
"It defies the economic realities of the market that SBC would sign onto this deal at any significant cost to it," he said.
While other regional Bell and local exchange companies did not criticize the proposed agreement today, saying they needed time to study it, analysts said they expected it would not face serious obstacles.
"This is a win-win for almost everybody involved," said Scott Cleland, an analyst at the Legg Mason Precursor Group. "You will not see a lot of people trashing this because it has a little bit for everybody. The F.C.C. gets a host of pro-competitive commitments and a way to enforce them with stiff penalties. The competitors get a lot of their favorite pet rocks that they have long sought. And SBC gets approval of its merger and it gets to control its own destiny."
Bob Atkinson, the lead Government lawyer who negotiated the conditions, described them today as "substantial and unprecedented" and said they would "create irreversible market competition."
Mr. Atkinson said that the agreement would be enforced by an independent auditor approved by the F.C.C. and paid for by SBC, formerly Southwestern Bell. Ameritech, based in Chicago, was originally formed by the joining of Bell companies in Illinois, Indiana, Ohio, Michigan and Wisconsin.
The proposed agreement requires SBC, based in San Antonio, to offer competitors in its territory steep promotional discounts of up to 32 percent for reselling their services and 25 percent for leasing the use of wires that run from SBC's switching centers to customers' homes. It also requires that SBC establish a separate corporate affiliate for offering high-speed Internet service on telephone lines. The affiliate would be largely independent of SBC and would have to be treated like any competitor of SBC.

HEADLINE: BUSINESS DIGEST

 BODY:

Phone Merger Approved, Subject to Unusual Rules
Federal officials tentatively approved the $72 billion acquisition of Ameritech by SBC Communications provided that the new company not only open its market to competition but also offer local telephone service in 30 cities outside its existing region.
Officials said the unusual regulatory approach for approving the acquisition was intended to drive down phone bills by encouraging competition in markets that have been dominated by one phone company. SBC and Ameritech executives said that regulators would impose stiff financial penalties if the new company failed to offer residential and business telephone service. Page A1. 

Agreement Near on Year 2000 Suits
The White House and Republicans in Congress have agreed on legislation to limit lawsuits arising from Year 2000 computer breakdowns. The President is expected to sign the measure this week. A1.
 
Energy East Seen in Deal for CTG
In a move signaling further consolidation in a deregulated utility industry, Energy East is expected to announce that it is buying CTG Resources of Hartford, the holding company for Connecticut Natural Gas, for about $355 million in stock and cash, people close to the talks said. Shares of CTG Resources rose $1.625, to $35.625. C2.
 
China.com Links Hot Concepts
China.com combines two of the decade's hottest investment concepts -- China and the Internet -- in a single stock offering. While both have lost some luster among investors in recent weeks, China.com seems convinced that putting them together will prove irresistible. The company, based in Hong Kong, plans to raise $56.3 million through an initial public offering on Nasdaq. Market Place. C11.
 
Bankers Trust Chairman Resigns
Frank Newman resigned as chairman of Bankers Trust, apparently isolated and powerless after Deutsche Bank acquired his company for $10 billion. C4.
 
Nasdaq and S.& P. 500 Near Highs
Stocks staged a broad advance as the Nasdaq composite index and the Standard & Poor's 500-stock index moved close to their record highs. The S.& P. 500 surged 20.10 points, to 1,351.45, and Nasdaq gained 39.67 points, to 2,642.11. The Dow Jones industrial average rose 160.20 points, to 10,815.35. C12.
 
Summit Meeting Ends Without Deal
The first summit meeting of Latin American and European nations ended with leaders of the world's second- and third-largest trading blocs unable to agree on a timetable or an agenda for talks for a free-trade pact. C4.
 
CMGI to Buy a Stake in Alta Vista
CMGI, the Internet venture capital company, will buy a majority stake in Compaq Computer's Alta Vista Internet search engine business for $2.03 billion in stock. C7.
 
Marketing Executive for Thompson
J. Walter Thompson is naming a high-profile executive to a new post with duties that include marketing the agency itself. Mary Baglivo, 41, who unexpectedly announced last month that she would resign as chief executive at Euro RSCG Tatham in Chicago for personal reasons and return with her family to the East Coast, will join Thompson, effective Sept. 1, as executive vice president and chief marketing officer-U.S.A. Stuart Elliott: Advertising C8.
 
Consumer Confidence Index Rises
Consumer confidence rose in June to the highest level in more than three decades. The Conference Board's index of consumer confidence rose to 138.4 from 137.7 in May. C2.

HEADLINE: Group Finds a Way to Offer A Hepatitis C Drug for Less

BYLINE: By DENISE GRADY 

 BODY:
Advocates for people with hepatitis C have found a way for patients to bypass a major drug company and buy an expensive medication, Ribavirin, at far below the usual price.
Starting July 9, when the patent on Ribavirin for use against hepatitis C expires, patients will be able to order the drug from a pharmacy in Pittsburgh, according to the Hepatitis C Action and Advocacy Coalition, a patients' group based in San Francisco. 
 The strategy, which seems to have taken both the Food and Drug Administration and the drug industry by surprise, is carefully constructed to navigate the nation's drug approval and patent laws. It involves an overseas manufacturer, an importer in the United States and the drugstore in Pittsburgh known as a compounding pharmacy, where pharmacists will hand-pack imported Ribavirin powder into capsules to fill individual prescriptions.
F.D.A. officials said yesterday that they were looking into the plan, but would probably not try to stop it unless it was determined to be unsafe.
Like old-fashioned drugstores, the compounding pharmacies prepare drug mixtures and doses that differ from what is commercially available. They are not permitted to copy commercially available products, but may make versions of them that vary one or more ingredients according to a doctor's prescription.
The drug now at issue, Ribavirin, is approved to treat hepatitis C only in combination with another drug, Intron A. Alone, Ribavirin has no effect on the disease. Although the combination appears to eliminate the virus in only 40 percent to 45 percent of patients, it is the best treatment developed so far for the disease, which kills 10,000 Americans year and is the leading cause of liver transplants. About four million people in the United States are thought to be infected. The virus is spread mainly by contaminated needles and other exposure to infected blood.
The only way that patients can buy Ribavirin is as part of a combination product, Rebetron, sold only by the Schering-Plough Corporation of Madison, N.J. Rebetron includes Ribavirin and Intron A, a brand of interferon made exclusively by Schering-Plough. Patients take it for six months or a year, with a year of treatment costing $15,600 to $17,300, or $1,300 to $1,440 a month.
When Rebetron was approved in June 1998, it was hailed as a major advance, because it worked far better than the previous treatment, interferon alone. But some patients' enthusiasm withered when they found out that Schering-Plough had "bundled" the two drugs into a kit and would not sell them separately.
In the year since its approval, 293,000 prescriptions for Rebetron have been filled, bringing in $214 million for Schering-Plough, according to IMS Health, a health care information company in Plymouth Meeting, Pa.
The bundling of Rebetron provoked bitterness because three other companies also make interferon, and some patients who had trouble tolerating the side effects of Intron A wanted to try their luck combining Ribavirin with another brand.
Now, starting on July 9, patients will be able to buy Ribavirin alone for $225 a month, from Fisher's Specialty Pharmacy Services, in Pittsburgh. That price is about 80 percent less than what Schering-Plough's version costs, based on subtracting the price of Intron A from the price of Rebetron, and could represent a saving of as much as $900 a month.
"We're a corner drugstore, basically," said Betty Stein, a pharmacist and owner of the business along with two other pharmacists, Jeff Fisher and Donald Kerrish. "We have checked into this. We are not doing it on a whim. Every avenue is on the up and up."
Ms. Stein said that she and her colleagues would not manufacture the Ribavirin, but would buy it from another company and pack it into capsules by hand, according to each patient's prescription. She declined to name the company, but said it was an importer that bought the drug from an overseas manufacturer.
Even though the Ribavirin is imported, Ms. Stein said, it is pure and carries the U.S.P. label, which stands for U.S. Pharmacopeia and means that the product has been approved for dispensing in this country.
Ms. Stein said it would be legal to sell Ribavirin in this manner on July 9 because Schering-Plough's patent on using it for hepatitis C would expire the day before. Although the company still has an agreement that bars competitors in the United States from marketing generic forms of the drug, that agreement does not apply to pharmacies like Fisher's, which merely puts the drug into capsules.
A spokesman for Schering-Plough said that the company was investigating the matter, but that so far, it appeared to be legal.

HEADLINE: Political Memo;
Albany Democrats Savor Request for County Bailout

BYLINE: By CLIFFORD J. LEVY 

 BODY:
When enduring economic hardship, New York City has usually turned to the state for assistance, and the reception from the many suburban Republican lawmakers in Albany has rarely been gracious. Why can't the city live within its means, they disdainfully ask, as if lecturing a ne'er-do-well neighbor who has sought a handout once too often.
So the current budget shortfall in the cast-iron Republican fortress of Nassau County has put Democrats in the State Capitol, most of whom represent the city, in an unaccustomed position. Now Nassau wants help from the state -- and fast. Hmm. Wait a minute. Why can't Nassau live within its means? 
 Even the news yesterday that Standard & Poor's, the Wall Street firm, had drastically lowered the county's bond rating while issuing a finger-wagging critique of its management was not enough to jolt the Democrats into action. It may not exactly be payback time, but the Nassau bailout has certainly become entangled in the regional and partisan rivalries that define Albany.
The Republicans who control the county have asked the state to approve a new local tax on real estate sales that could generate at least $50 million annually, to be used to patch a budget gap of more than $200 million. The Republicans are desperate to avoid a measure they believe could be far more politically noxious: raising property taxes.
Standing in the county's way in Albany is the Democratic majority in the Assembly, which has accumulated a lengthy list of grievances against suburban Republicans.
The Assembly Democrats say they will authorize the tax only if the state creates an independent board to oversee the county's finances, a proposal that the county has greeted like castor oil. Ripping a page from the Republicans' litany about fiscal responsibility, the Democrats have declared that they need to break the county of its bad budget habits.
And they seem to savor the prospect of Republicans' trekking to Albany when the economy is robust to ask Democrats for a tax increase.
"It is an interesting role reversal," said Assemblyman Thomas P. DiNapoli, a Nassau Democrat who is one of only a handful of Assembly Democrats from Long Island.
For their part, Gov. George E. Pataki, a Republican, and the Republican majority in the Senate say they will approve the new tax without conditions, contending that they generally back such local requests. The Governor and the Senate Republicans, who typically cast themselves as fierce opponents of new taxes, have declined to find fault with their Republican brethren in the county.
"I don't think that is for me to comment on," said Senator Dean G. Skelos, a Nassau Republican.
The Assembly Speaker, Sheldon Silver, has named Mr. DiNapoli to lead the Assembly side in the talks with the County Executive, Thomas S. Gulotta, and it can probably be assumed that the choice of Mr. Di Napoli galls Mr. Gulotta and other Nassau Republicans.
Mr. DiNapoli is the new county Democratic chairman, and he is trying to revive the party's fortunes. (His predecessor was convicted on Federal fraud charges this year.) Mr. DiNapoli does not deny that he would like an agreement in Albany to give the Democrats more power over the county budget, from negotiating contracts with unions to directing spending on health care.
"If they want to come to us for support, they are going to have to do some fundamental reordering of the way they do business," he said. "For a change, we have a lot more leverage than we normally have."
Mr. Gulotta declined to address Mr. DiNapoli's comments, saying only that he was reviewing the Democrats' demands. Mr. Gulotta has defended the county's performance by asserting that he has reduced the size of government and kept property taxes level during his tenure.
Likewise, other Republicans were careful in how they discussed Mr. DiNapoli, showing surprising restraint in a county not known for its political pleasantries. "I am hoping that partisan politics will not enter into the negotiations," said Bruce Blakeman, presiding officer of the Nassau County Legislature.
Even if Mr. DiNapoli reaches a tentative deal with Mr. Gulotta this week, the gamesmanship will not end there. In Albany, everything is trade bait, and the Assembly Democrats have long complained that Senate Republicans hold up measures that New York City wants to extract concessions. This time, Mr. Silver will undoubtedly delay approval of the tax until he gets something in return from the Governor and Senate.
"Everything that the city has gotten has been by begging, scratching, fighting," said Assemblyman Scott Stringer, a Manhattan Democrat. "Nothing happens without us having to swallow something."
Senator Skelos called such attacks misguided. "The Republicans were always there to help out the city, even when under Democratic administrations," he said.
Ultimately, the tax could be at the mercy of the negotiations over the state budget, which is now three months late. And while the Assembly Democrats insist they do not want to leave the county twisting, they are not in any hurry to jump to its aid.
"I don't want to say that this is a purely political thing because the fiscal health of Nassau County is very important to me," said Assemblyman David Sidikman, a Nassau Democrat. "The mismanagement there is obvious. But will the Republicans take a political hit? I hope so."

HEADLINE: CRISIS IN THE BALKANS: THE OPPOSITION;
Thousands Protest Milosevic In City Devastated by Bombs

BYLINE: By BLAINE HARDEN

DATELINE: CACAK, Serbia, June 29

 BODY:
In the first major anti-Government demonstration since NATO stopped bombing Yugoslavia, 10,000 people packed into the central square of this war-damaged manufacturing city today to demand the resignation of President Slobodan Milosevic.
A parade of speakers said Mr. Milosevic, by committing robbery and murder in Kosovo in the name of the Serbian people, had shamed Serbia in the eyes of the world, ruined the economy and tried to cover up his failures by a campaign of lies in the state-owned media. 
 Remarkably, in this city where several large factories were destroyed by American bombs that killed five civilians, not a single speaker blamed the United States for devastation that will likely take decades to overcome. Instead, they accused Mr. Milosevic of "evil" that provoked attacks from Serbia's traditional allies in the West.
"This Government has shamed us in front of ourselves, in front of God and in front of the whole world," said Milan Protic, a history professor from Belgrade and a leader of the Alliance for Change, an umbrella group of opposition parties that organized the rally.
Vladan Babic, coordinator of what the Alliance for Change says will be a series of rallies across Serbia in coming weeks, told the crowd that the point of the demonstration was to send a simple message to Mr. Milosevic: "Go away! Go to Cuba and study Stalinism there."
Cacak is one of the few cities in Serbia where the opposition is in complete control of the city government. The rally organizers said they had not yet planned demonstrations in Belgrade.
Three months of daily rallies in Belgrade in the winter of 1996-97 brought huge crowds into the streets who demanded that Mr. Milosevic resign. But owing in part to infighting among opposition leaders, the demonstrations fizzled out.
Just as demonstration today began, a loud explosion went off in a far corner of the square. The crowd was stunned into silence, but the blast hurt no one. A device used in war games to imitate the sound of artillery reportedly caused the explosion. It was not clear who planted it.
After demonstrators saw that nobody was hurt, the rally resumed, and they joined in shouting, "Red bandits, red bandits!" a familiar reference to Mr. Milosevic and his Government.
The state of war imposed here during the 11 weeks of bombing ended last weekend. But the Serbian Government has not lifted its ban on public gatherings. The city of Cacak decided to go ahead with the rally despite having been told by the Serbian police that it was illegal, said Vice Mayor Dusan Dzinkovic.
On roads leading into the city of 80,000, the Serbian police stopped a number of buses and cars, some from Belgrade carrying opposition party leaders, as well as foreign and local journalists. A few foreign reporters said they had been turned back.
At the rally, an opposition leader, Vuk Obradovic, thanked the Yugoslav Army for the local soldiers who, he said, picked up demonstrators and took them to the rally after the police had stopped busloads of opposition figures outside the city.
Army reservists have blocked roads and bridges in several towns in central Serbia in the last week, protesting that they have not been paid for having served in the Kosovo conflict. It was not clear whether the reservists were the ones who had helped take demonstrators to the city.
"Thanks to our army for enabling people to break through the police barricades on some roads and come to join us," said Mr. Obradovic, a former Army general who resigned in 1992 to protest the conduct of Serbia's war against Croatia. "They are with us, and we are with them, and they know it."
The Yugoslav Army has had a rocky relationship with Mr. Milosevic over the years, a relationship punctuated by frequent purges and grumbling about declining living standards, as the Serbian leader has shifted money to the police.
A Serbian police officer outside the town said after the rally that he and his fellow officers had stopped no one from going to Cacak and accused the speakers at the rally of having spread false information.
The demonstration organizers said it was the first of what they hoped would be 20 to 25 protests across Serbia in the five weeks. Only then, they said, will they go to Belgrade.
"With the police harassing us, it is easier to organize locally," Mr. Protic said. "Belgrade should come as the culmination."
The opposition crushed the governing Socialist Party in elections in Cacak in 1996, taking control of all but eight seats in the 70-member City Council. The already high feelings against Mr. Milosevic soared in the conflict with NATO. Mayor Velimir Ilic complained publicly that the Yugoslav Army had caused the death of four civilians in the town by parking its tanks near houses. NATO bombs aimed at the tanks hit the houses.
After Mr. Ilic's complaints found their way onto Radio Free Europe, the military police tried to arrest him on May 19, and he went into hiding. Mr. Ilic used the rally as a "coming out" party, and his appearance on stage afternoon ignited the crowd.
"Today, I am the happiest person in the world, because I am with you again," he said. "But I could cry because of what this Government has done to this country."
The Mayor challenged the Serbian Government to arrest him now, saying that he would no longer hide from authorities and that they knew where to find him.
The rally was as notable for those who did not speak as for those who did. The charismatic leader of the largest opposition party in Serbia, Vuk Draskovic, has refused to join the opposition alliance. He says the time is not ripe to take to the streets to demand that Mr. Milosevic leave power.
At a news conference in Belgrade, Mr. Draskovic said today that he hoped to add pressure for change without disruptive demonstrations and that in any case there was no viable opposition movement in Serbia without his Serbian Renewal Movement.
"If it wasn't for journalists," Mr. Draskovic said, "no one would have any knowledge either of the alliance or of their rallies. The alliance groups small parties with no influence, and everything they organize will substantiate that fact."
Mr. Draskovic's dismissive remarks were the only mention of the demonstration on state television tonight.
On a wiltingly hot afternoon in the square, expectations were low that demonstrations alone could force Mr. Milosevic to step down.
"I have no hope that this rally will change anything," said Vladimir Lukic, 25, who is soon to graduate with an engineering decree from a college here whose windows were blown out by NATO bombs. "These demonstrations have happened before, and they didn't change anything."
Asked whether he blamed the United States or Western Europe for the bombing, Mr. Lukic said: "No. This is not the will of the American people. It is the consequence of the crimes that have been going on in this country for 10 years."

SECTION: Section A; Page 1; Column 1; National Desk 

LENGTH: 1700 words

HEADLINE: CLINTON LAYS OUT PLAN TO OVERHAUL MEDICARE SYSTEM

BYLINE: By ROBERT PEAR 

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, June 29

 BODY:
Following up on a promise to preserve Medicare for the aging baby-boom generation, President Clinton today proposed fundamental changes in the program's structure to make it more like private health insurance and to add coverage of prescription drugs, a benefit long sought by the elderly.
The White House said his proposal, one of the biggest domestic policy initiatives of the last three decades, would help keep Medicare solvent until 2027 -- 12 years longer than the last official projections under current law. 
 The President who rode into office with a promise to guarantee health insurance for all said he was hoping to close his Administration by enhancing the giant health program for the elderly and the disabled. The number of Americans 65 and older is expected to double in 30 years.
Administration officials acknowledged a potential contradiction in their efforts to shore up Medicare's finances while at the same time creating a costly new drug benefit. But they said they would save money by making structural changes in the program, by encouraging managed care competition, by trying to negotiate high-volume discounts for care as private insurers do and by increasing certain costs to beneficiaries. The $100 annual deductible for doctors' services, for example, would rise with consumer prices, and patients would have to pay 20 percent of the cost of laboratory services.
Still, those savings would be dwarfed by a huge infusion of cash from the Treasury: $374 billion in the next 10 years, and $794 billion over 15.
Some lawmakers said the fast-growing budget surplus improved the prospects for passage of a major Medicare overhaul. But beneath the inklings of bipartisanship today, the old suspicions lingered.
Republicans in Congress reacted cautiously. They did not reject Mr. Clinton's proposals out of hand. And they said they too wanted to restructure Medicare. But they also said they feared that the costs of the proposed drug benefit would soar.
The economic context for Mr. Clinton's proposal could hardly be better, with a vibrant economy generating a windfall of Federal revenue. The White House said on Monday that it foresaw surpluses totaling nearly $3 trillion over the next decade. One in eight of those dollars would go to shore up Medicare under the President's plan.
"In a nation bursting with prosperity, no senior should have to choose between buying food and buying medicine," Mr. Clinton told a bipartisan audience of lawmakers at the White House today.
Under his plan, drug coverage would be voluntary, but the Administration said it would be such a good bargain that almost everybody would choose it. In 2002, the first year of Mr. Clinton's proposal, each beneficiary would pay a drug premium of $24 a month, or $288 a year. Medicare would cover half the beneficiary's drug costs, up to an initial annual limit of $1,000 on the first $2,000 of drug expenses. The premium would rise gradually, to $44 a month in 2008, and so would the benefit: to an annual maximum of $2,500, or half of drug expenses up to $5,000.
People with low incomes (under $17,000 a year for a couple) would not have to pay the drug premium or the beneficiary's share of the drug costs.
"This is a drug benefit our seniors can afford, at a price America can afford," Mr. Clinton said.
Lobbyists for the elderly said enactment of a Medicare drug benefit before next year's elections was a long shot. But they rejoiced that Mr. Clinton had put the issue high on the nation's agenda.
While Mr. Clinton would offer drug coverage to all 39 million Medicare beneficiaries, Republicans said Congress should start by helping the 15.5 million who do not have such coverage through their Medigap policies, retiree health plans or health maintenance organizations.
In a joint statement, the Speaker of the House, J. Dennis Hastert, and the Senate Republican leader, Trent Lott, said, "We should be careful not to force those who already have prescription drug coverage to accept a Government-run plan that costs more and provides fewer choices."
Under the President's proposal, Medicare beneficiaries would pay a total of $110 billion in premiums for drug coverage over the next 10 years, and the Government would put up $118 billion.
Gail E. Shearer, a health policy expert at Consumers Union, said, "The proposed drug benefit offers consumers good value -- far better than they can now get in the Medigap market," which offers private insurance to supplement Medicare.
The White House estimates that the new drug benefit would cost the Treasury $21.8 billion a year in 2009, and it anticipates that the costs at that point would be growing about 11 percent a year -- much less than the increases in drug spending reported recently by private health plans. This disparity will inevitably raise questions about whether the Administration has underestimated the long-term cost of its proposal, as the Government has done with other Medicare benefits in the past.
Some elements of Mr. Clinton's plan had been disclosed in recent weeks, but there were a few surprises today. For example, he proposed to eliminate all co-payments, deductibles and other charges that beneficiaries pay for preventive services covered by Medicare, which include screening for osteoporosis and for breast, colon and prostate cancer. Mr. Clinton also called for a study to see if Medicare should cover services to help people quit smoking.
But the President also proposed to make beneficiaries pay 20 percent of the cost of laboratory services, for which they now pay little or nothing.
In addition, he asked Congress to increase the deductible amount that Medicare beneficiaries pay for doctors' services and outpatient hospital visits. The deductible, which has been fixed by law at $100 a year since 1991, would be automatically increased each year to reflect increases in prices, as measured by the Consumer Price Index.
Other highlights of Mr. Clinton's proposal included these:
*For the first time, H.M.O.'s would be permitted to compete on the prices they charge Medicare beneficiaries. A beneficiary who chose a low-cost H.M.O. could keep 75 cents of every dollar saved. H.M.O.'s would have to provide all the benefits covered by the traditional Medicare program and could offer more.
*The Government would offer subsidies to employers as an incentive for them to continue providing health benefits to retired workers. To be eligible for a subsidy, an employer would have to offer a prescription drug benefit at least equal to the proposed Medicare benefit.
*The Government would continue many of the Medicare cuts that were made by Congress in 1997 and are scheduled to expire in 2003. But the Administration said it would provide some financial relief for nursing homes and home health care agencies and for hospitals that serve large numbers of poor people.
Howard J. Bedlin, vice president of the National Council on the Aging, a research and advocacy group, welcomed the President's proposal for drug coverage.
"It would be wonderful if a bipartisan consensus emerged, but it's a long shot," Mr. Bedlin said. "Prescription drug benefits are so complex in substance, so controversial politically and so expensive, I'd be surprised if any major reform was enacted before 2001."
Chris Jennings, the coordinator of health policy at the White House, said people on Medicare would not abuse drug benefits because they would have to pay half the cost of any medicines. The Government would hire private companies to manage drug benefits for the elderly.
In recent years, employers and health plans have reported sharp increases in spending for prescription drugs. The Segal Company, an employee benefits consulting concern, told its clients last week that they "should expect to see 1999 prescription drug costs rise by an average of 16 percent."
PCS Health Systems of Scottsdale, Ariz., a company that manages drug benefits, said private health plans saw drug spending increases in the range of 14 to 18 percent last year.
Total retail drug spending in the United States has nearly doubled in the last six years and now exceeds $90 billion a year. New, more effective medicines often cost three or four times as much as older drugs for the same condition.
The drug industry reacted warily to the President's announcement.
"We agree on the need to expand drug coverage for seniors, but we want to make sure it's done in the right way," said Alan F. Holmer, president of the Pharmaceutical Research and Manufacturers of America. "We plan to review the President's plan with great care."
The association is planning a major public campaign on the issue of Medicare coverage of prescription drugs, revolving around the industry's aversion to anything resembling price controls.
Democrats had high praise for the President's plan. Representative Richard A. Gephardt of Missouri, the House minority leader, said Mr. Clinton's drug proposal "will be the single greatest improvement to the Medicare program since its inception" in 1965.
Senator Edward M. Kennedy, Democrat of Massachusetts, said Mr. Clinton had found "the right prescription for Medicare."
Mr. Clinton's Medicare proposal is in many ways a careful piece of politics, reflecting some of the hard-learned lessons of previous struggles over health care, including his campaign for universal coverage in 1993-94 and the last big Medicare expansion attempted, in 1988-89.
Administration officials presented their plan today as completely voluntary, aimed at helping the middle class as well as the poor and structured to run with only the lightest regulatory hand. In other words, they said, it is far different from the vast quasi-governmental apparatus proposed by the President six years ago.
Still, experts said, it will be difficult to pass any major health care proposal in a Congress getting ready for the political warfare of an election season. Robert D. Reischauer, former director of the Congressional Budget Office, said, "A prescription drug benefit is very attractive to Republicans and Democrats in the abstract, but when the details are laid out they will be controversial."

Chart: "A CLOSER LOOK: The President's Goals"
President Clinton's proposals for Medicare's 39 million beneficiaries would use $794 billion in expected Government surpluses over the next 15 years to shore up the program and add a benefit.
 
New voluntary prescription drug coverage for Medicare beneficiaries.
ESTIMATED COST -- $118 billion over 10 years.
 
Premiums would start at $24 a month once the benefit becomes available in 2002, rising to $44 by 2008.
 
Coverage would start with the first dollar of prescription costs, with no deductible and a 50 percent co-payment. The Government would pay half the first $2,000 in 2002, with the limit rising to half the first $5,000 by 2008.
 
Single people with incomes under $11,000 and couples with incomes under $17,000 would not pay premiums or co-payments.
 
Financial incentives would be given to employers who provided retiree health coverage if it included a prescription drug benefit at least equivalent to the new Medicare benefit.
 
Existing co-payments and the deductible would be eliminated for preventive services covered by Medicare, including screening for prostate and colon cancer, bone mass measurements, pelvic exams and mammograms. An education campaign aimed at Americans over 50 would be adopted, including a three-year demonstration project to help beneficiaries stop smoking.
ESTIMATED COST -- $3 billion over 10 years.
 
A 20 percent co-payment for laboratory tests, for which beneficiaries currently pay nothing, would be added. The deductible for doctor's office visits and other outpatient treatments would be adjusted for inflation; the current deductible of $100 a year would probably rise by $2 to $3 a year. ESTIMATED SAVINGS -- $11 billion over 10 years.
 
Competition would be added among H.M.O.'s in the prices they charge Medicare beneficiaries. Beneficiaries who chose a low-cost H.M.O. could keep 75 cents of every dollar saved. H.M.O.'s would have to provide all the benefits covered by the traditional Medicare program and could offer more.
ESTIMATED SAVINGS -- $8 billion over 10 years.
 
Most curbs on payments to health care providers that were part of the 1997 balanced-budget agreement would be extended, but a $7.5 billion fund would be established so that adjustments could be made if some cuts were hurting patient care.
ESTIMATED SAVINGS -- $31.5 billion over 10 years.
 
Medicare administrators would be given new authority to negotiate discounts rather than using Government fee schedules to buy goods and services, including medical equipment.
ESTIMATED SAVINGS -- $25 billion over 10 years.
(pg. A18)

HEADLINE: Canada's Close Ties to Cuba Fraying Over Human Rights

BYLINE: By ANTHONY DePALMA 

DATELINE: TORONTO, June 29

 BODY:
The cozy relationship between Canada and Cuba that has so infuriated American Presidents and Congressional leaders has sunk to its lowest point in years, principally because of Cuba's continuing record of violating basic human rights.
Angered by the recent conviction of several dissidents on whose behalf Prime Minister Jean Chretien had personally intervened, Canada is blocking all new assistance programs to Cuba that do not clearly further the protection of human rights. 
 Canadian officials have also decided not to encourage other countries in the hemisphere to support Cuba's admission into the Organization of American States.
Officials in the Canadian foreign ministry say the new approach is not a break with Cuba -- as some Washington politicians had hoped it would be -- but rather the refinement of a longstanding policy that continues to have as its goal Cuba's transition to democracy.
"We're still engaged with them, but we have to put some northern ice in the middle of it," Mr. Chretien told reporters in Ottawa today.
But some political analysts see the cooling of Canada-Cuba relations as the inevitable result of dealing with Fidel Castro.
"Chretien is learning what Khrushchev knew before," said Larry Birns, director of the Council for Hemispheric Affairs in Washington. "You can supply billions in foreign aid, as the Russians did, and still have minimal leverage. Ultimately Castro is a nationalist, and he decides what he thinks is best for Cuba."
When Mr. Chretien visited Cuba in 1998, he presented a list of four political prisoners that he wanted Mr. Castro to release. But in March the four were convicted of "counterrevolutionary activities" and sentenced to up to five years in jail. Canada-Cuba relations then sank to their lowest point since foreign aid was suspended in the mid-1980's because of Cuba's military involvement in Angola.
Foreign Affairs Minister Lloyd Axworthy ordered a review of contacts with Cuba and the policy of constructive engagement that has been the basis of Canada's approach throughout the period that Cuba has been ruled by Fidel Castro's Communist Government.
While the United States imposed an economic embargo on Cuba, Canada moved in the opposite direction, believing that the best way to bring about real democratic reform in the country was to maintain strong ties. Canada also believed that it could use its influence to prod Cuba into doing more to protect human rights of its citizens, particularly political prisoners.
After the policy review was completed in April, Canada decided to continue trading with and doing business in Cuba. That commerce came to $555 million in 1998.
But because of the serious flaws in Cuba's human rights record, Canada's foreign ministry believed that a tougher approach was needed, at least until Cuba responded positively to Canadian concerns.
Mr. Axworthy had demanded that all Cabinet ministers with programs in Cuba clear any official visits through him, and he has decided that planning for all visits should be suspended for now. That meant the indefinite postponement of trips by the International Trade Minister, Sergio Marchi, and the Minister of International Cooperation, Diane Marleau.
Canada has given Cuba $24 million in foreign aid during the last five years. Officials say the funds have not been frozen completely but are subject to a case-by-case review. Several projects have already been suspended, including a plan to provide financial assistance to help Haiti cooperate in a medical development effort with Cuba. The Canadians fear that Cuba may use the medical research more for propaganda than humanitarian purposes.
Canada has also backed away from a commitment to lobby for Cuba's integration into the hemisphere, particularly through participation in the Organization of American States, from which it was expelled in 1962. Canada will be the host of the organization's general assembly next June.

HEADLINE: NOTEBOOK;
Campus in Cyberspace

BYLINE: By MICHAEL POLLAK 

 BODY:
Unext.com, an Internet company that recently joined with Columbia Business School to sell business-education material supplied by Columbia, has added Stanford, the University of Chicago and the London School of Economics to its list of academic providers for profit.
Unext.com signed them up last week. Initially available only to employees of corporate customers starting this fall, the noncredit courses, which are being developed, should be available to individuals next year, said Edwin Eisendrath, Unext's vice president for communications. Mr. Eisendrath said Unext hoped to receive academic accreditation and eventually to offer a master's degree. The universities are not granting degrees or credit themselves through Unext, which pays them royalties. 
 The on-line service, based in Deerfield, Ill., gives the universities veto power over all course material and use of their names, he said.
John M. Preis, associate dean for finance and administration at Columbia Business School, said the arrangement would provide access to 35 million desktops worldwide.

HEADLINE: Nassau's Bonds Deemed Riskier By Credit Rater

BYLINE: By DAVID M. HALBFINGER 

DATELINE: GARDEN CITY, N.Y., June 29

 BODY:
Nassau County's financial problems took a significant turn for the worse today when Standard & Poor's downgraded the county's bonds to the lowest investment-grade category, and delivered a scathing critique of the county's fiscal management.
The new rating of BBB, two notches above that designated for junk bonds, could make it much more expensive for Nassau to raise money through bond sales, and gives it one of the worst credit ratings among wealthy suburban counties nationwide. 
 Only Orange County, Calif., which declared bankruptcy in 1994 after making disastrous investments in risky securities, has a lower rating among such counties, officials at the rating agency said. In New York, only Erie, the county in which Buffalo lies, has a rating as low as BBB, but Erie's battered industrial economy stands in sharp contrast to the high per capita wealth and low unemployment of Nassau.
In lowering the rating on Nassau's $2.6 billion in bonds to BBB from A-minus, officials at Standard & Poor's also dismissed the county's protracted effort to eliminate its budget deficit, which now stands at more than $200 million, as too little, too late, and too full of holes.
"The current fiscal problem is symptomatic of the historical difficulty the county has had in matching ongoing revenues to ongoing expenditures over several fiscal years," the rating agency said in announcing its action. "When faced with budget imbalances, the county has been unable to take decisive corrective action to close the budget gaps."
The announcement is a bitter blow to Nassau's Republican machine, which has maintained its grip on power in recent years largely by avoiding property tax increases even while doling out increasingly generous wage increases to the county's civil servants, police and patronage workers. The political cost of that fiscal imbalance could be paid as early as this fall, when all 19 county legislative seats, of which Republicans hold 14, are up for election.
Increasingly, and imprudently, the rating agency said, county officials have relied on one-time-only revenue measures like the sale of assets, including the county medical center, which is now being spun off to a quasi-public corporation.
One of the many problems with those one-shot sales, Standard & Poor's said, is that many of them take years longer to complete than county officials forecast. The revenues from the hospital sale, for example, were first placed in the budget two fiscal years ago.
As a result, the agency said, the county has had to increase its short-term borrowing substantially to cover current expenses like salaries. This year, the county expects to incur $430 million in that costly short-term debt, or about 20 percent of its $2.2 billion budget, said Diane P. Brosen, a Standard & Poor's analyst. Since 1994, she said, Nassau's short-term debt has jumped 70 percent, even though the total amount of the budget is largely unchanged.
Perhaps the most damaging news in Standard & Poor's review of Nassau's predicament was its opinion that a deficit-closing plan the county is promoting in Albany will not be enough to do the job.
Desperate to avoid raising property taxes, County Executive Thomas S. Gulotta has been seeking legislative approval for a new 1 percent tax on real estate sales, which would produce an estimated $50 million to $70 million a year in revenue.
"Even if the county were to realize the major components of its fiscal recovery plan," the rating agency said today, "it is unlikely that the plan would fully correct the current year's fiscal imbalance."
Ms. Brosen, of Standard & Poor's, asserted that Mr. Gulotta's plan unwisely includes $170 million in borrowing against the county's share of the nationwide settlement of tobacco litigation. To expect that Nassau would receive the tobacco payments beginning in December, she said, was to ignore complex negotiations involving several states that could delay that money for years.
Nassau Republicans spoke as if from a single script today, saying that the downgrade was expected and promising to work together to solve the county's financial problems. They also played down the significance of the rating agency's move. While a lower bond rating suggests a higher risk to investors, who over time demand higher interest rates in return, those added costs are difficult to estimate because of fluctuations in the bond market.
"It certainly isn't good," said the Nassau Comptroller, Frederick E. Parola. "But at this point it won't cost the county a great deal of money. It's really more embarrassment than substantive. It's a slap of cold water in the face. It's an unfriendly reprimand."
But Democrats wasted no time, or niceties, in placing blame. "It's Gulotta's fault," said Judith A. Jacobs, the minority leader in the Legislature. "It's his budget. It's his one-shot deals. You can't go through one year after another with these deficits and one-shots and not know something was going to happen like this. It's like knowing the train is about to roll by, and you keep going anyway."
Leaders of several civic and business groups have complained loudly in recent weeks about Mr. Gulotta's handling of Nassau's finances. One of them, Phoebe Goodman, executive director of the nonpartisan NassauCitizens Budget Committee, lamented today what she called the County Executive's stubborn denial of reality.
"He seems to feel that the worst thing you could do is raise property taxes," Ms. Goodman said. "That's O.K. if you're cutting services and staff. But if you're increasing staff, which he did even though he said he was cutting it, you're going to have a deficit. Once the decision was made seven years ago to lower taxes and not fill the gap, the die was cast. He's been resorting to fiscal gimmicks ever since."
Mr. Gulotta declined to respond today to much of the criticism of his budget practices, saying that placing blame "is neither productive nor helpful." But he said he regretted that Standard & Poor's had not waited to see the outcome of his efforts in Albany. "I had asked if they could wait five to seven days, so they could review a fuller financial picture, depending on what actions the State Legislature might or might not take," he said.
Richard Marino, a Standard & Poor's analyst, noted in response that the rating agency warned Nassau as early as March 1998 that it was contemplating a downgrade. In December, he added, Standard & Poor's set a deadline of the end of June for Nassau to resolve its most basic budget problems.
Moreover, Ms. Brosen noted that Mr. Gulotta's administration had been through a similar scenario just seven years ago. The county's bond rating was lowered twice in 1992 by Moody's, a rival agency, at a time of a similarly ballooning deficit. The following year, after floating bonds to cover the deficit, Nassau asked Standard & Poor's to begin rating the county's debt. Ms. Brosen said officials promised that from then on, they would exercise fiscal discipline, using only taxes and other recurring revenues to pay current expenses. "But they didn't," she said.
Mr. Gulotta also suggested that the rating agency was mistaken in its review of his fiscal stewardship, noting that Nassau had enjoyed surpluses before the current deficit materialized. However, those surpluses are far outweighed by the recent deficits.

What the Standard & Poor's ratings mean
 
AAA AA A BBB
Best ratings. Muncipalities or companies with these ratings have an "extremely strong" to "adequate" ability to meet financial commitments.
 
BB B CCC CC C D
Considered "junk bond" or "speculative" ratings. Municipalities or companies with these ratings present greater risk and range from "vulnerable" to"in default."

HEADLINE: CRISIS IN THE BALKANS: THE OPPOSITION;
Thousands Protest Milosevic In City Devastated by Bombs

BYLINE: By BLAINE HARDEN

DATELINE: CACAK, Serbia, June 29

 BODY:
In the first major anti-Government demonstration since NATO stopped bombing Yugoslavia, 10,000 people packed into the central square of this war-damaged manufacturing city today to demand the resignation of President Slobodan Milosevic.
A parade of speakers said Mr. Milosevic, by committing robbery and murder in Kosovo in the name of the Serbian people, had shamed Serbia in the eyes of the world, ruined the economy and tried to cover up his failures by a campaign of lies in the state-owned media. 
 Remarkably, in this city where several large factories were destroyed by American bombs that killed five civilians, not a single speaker blamed the United States for devastation that will likely take decades to overcome. Instead, they accused Mr. Milosevic of "evil" that provoked attacks from Serbia's traditional allies in the West.
"This Government has shamed us in front of ourselves, in front of God and in front of the whole world," said Milan Protic, a history professor from Belgrade and a leader of the Alliance for Change, an umbrella group of opposition parties that organized the rally.
Vladan Babic, coordinator of what the Alliance for Change says will be a series of rallies across Serbia in coming weeks, told the crowd that the point of the demonstration was to send a simple message to Mr. Milosevic: "Go away! Go to Cuba and study Stalinism there."
Cacak is one of the few cities in Serbia where the opposition is in complete control of the city government. The rally organizers said they had not yet planned demonstrations in Belgrade.
Three months of daily rallies in Belgrade in the winter of 1996-97 brought huge crowds into the streets who demanded that Mr. Milosevic resign. But owing in part to infighting among opposition leaders, the demonstrations fizzled out.
Just as demonstration today began, a loud explosion went off in a far corner of the square. The crowd was stunned into silence, but the blast hurt no one. A device used in war games to imitate the sound of artillery reportedly caused the explosion. It was not clear who planted it.
After demonstrators saw that nobody was hurt, the rally resumed, and they joined in shouting, "Red bandits, red bandits!" a familiar reference to Mr. Milosevic and his Government.
The state of war imposed here during the 11 weeks of bombing ended last weekend. But the Serbian Government has not lifted its ban on public gatherings. The city of Cacak decided to go ahead with the rally despite having been told by the Serbian police that it was illegal, said Vice Mayor Dusan Dzinkovic.
On roads leading into the city of 80,000, the Serbian police stopped a number of buses and cars, some from Belgrade carrying opposition party leaders, as well as foreign and local journalists. A few foreign reporters said they had been turned back.
At the rally, an opposition leader, Vuk Obradovic, thanked the Yugoslav Army for the local soldiers who, he said, picked up demonstrators and took them to the rally after the police had stopped busloads of opposition figures outside the city.
Army reservists have blocked roads and bridges in several towns in central Serbia in the last week, protesting that they have not been paid for having served in the Kosovo conflict. It was not clear whether the reservists were the ones who had helped take demonstrators to the city.
"Thanks to our army for enabling people to break through the police barricades on some roads and come to join us," said Mr. Obradovic, a former Army general who resigned in 1992 to protest the conduct of Serbia's war against Croatia. "They are with us, and we are with them, and they know it."
The Yugoslav Army has had a rocky relationship with Mr. Milosevic over the years, a relationship punctuated by frequent purges and grumbling about declining living standards, as the Serbian leader has shifted money to the police.
A Serbian police officer outside the town said after the rally that he and his fellow officers had stopped no one from going to Cacak and accused the speakers at the rally of having spread false information.
The demonstration organizers said it was the first of what they hoped would be 20 to 25 protests across Serbia in the five weeks. Only then, they said, will they go to Belgrade.
"With the police harassing us, it is easier to organize locally," Mr. Protic said. "Belgrade should come as the culmination."
The opposition crushed the governing Socialist Party in elections in Cacak in 1996, taking control of all but eight seats in the 70-member City Council. The already high feelings against Mr. Milosevic soared in the conflict with NATO. Mayor Velimir Ilic complained publicly that the Yugoslav Army had caused the death of four civilians in the town by parking its tanks near houses. NATO bombs aimed at the tanks hit the houses.
After Mr. Ilic's complaints found their way onto Radio Free Europe, the military police tried to arrest him on May 19, and he went into hiding. Mr. Ilic used the rally as a "coming out" party, and his appearance on stage afternoon ignited the crowd.
"Today, I am the happiest person in the world, because I am with you again," he said. "But I could cry because of what this Government has done to this country."
The Mayor challenged the Serbian Government to arrest him now, saying that he would no longer hide from authorities and that they knew where to find him.
The rally was as notable for those who did not speak as for those who did. The charismatic leader of the largest opposition party in Serbia, Vuk Draskovic, has refused to join the opposition alliance. He says the time is not ripe to take to the streets to demand that Mr. Milosevic leave power.
At a news conference in Belgrade, Mr. Draskovic said today that he hoped to add pressure for change without disruptive demonstrations and that in any case there was no viable opposition movement in Serbia without his Serbian Renewal Movement.
"If it wasn't for journalists," Mr. Draskovic said, "no one would have any knowledge either of the alliance or of their rallies. The alliance groups small parties with no influence, and everything they organize will substantiate that fact."
Mr. Draskovic's dismissive remarks were the only mention of the demonstration on state television tonight.
On a wiltingly hot afternoon in the square, expectations were low that demonstrations alone could force Mr. Milosevic to step down.
"I have no hope that this rally will change anything," said Vladimir Lukic, 25, who is soon to graduate with an engineering decree from a college here whose windows were blown out by NATO bombs. "These demonstrations have happened before, and they didn't change anything."
Asked whether he blamed the United States or Western Europe for the bombing, Mr. Lukic said: "No. This is not the will of the American people. It is the consequence of the crimes that have been going on in this country for 10 years."

HEADLINE: Vexing Party, Clinton Backs Year 2000 Bill

BYLINE: By DAVID E. ROSENBAUM

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, June 29

 BODY:
Infuriating many Democrats, the White House today reached a basic agreement with Republicans in Congress on legislation to limit lawsuits arising from Year 2000 computer breakdowns.
Republican leaders said all the main points had been settled and that they hoped Congress would clear the measure for the President's signature this week. The White House agreed that only technical language remained to be worked out. 
 The agreement, aggressively promoted by the computer industry and other business interests and opposed by trial lawyers and consumer groups, generally follows the lines of the bill approved by the Senate two weeks ago. Most Democrats voted against that bill, and President Clinton promised at the time to veto it.
A White House spokesman said relatively minor changes enabled the President to approve the agreement.
As described by lawmakers from both parties, it would set a ceiling on punitive damages against small companies, give all companies a grace period to fix breakdowns before they could be sued, sharply limit class-action suits involving many plaintiffs and protect wealthy companies against being held responsible for computer failures caused by poorer ones.
Many angry Democrats in the Senate and House said the White House had encouraged them to cast a politically risky vote against the bill and then sold them out. "This is a closed-door deal that will hurt consumers," said Senator Patrick J. Leahy, Democrat of Vermont. "Maybe we can dare hope that this will be the last case of triangulation of the 20th century."
"Triangulation" is a term coined by Dick Morris, President Clinton's former political adviser, to mean that the President should not stand with Democrats or Republicans but at points above or between them. Democrats use it nowadays to express their irritation when Mr. Clinton does not follow the party line.
What is called the Y2K problem results from the longtime reliance of computer programs on only the last two digits of a year. When confronted by 00, some computers, software and electronically controlled machinery that have not been updated might misinterpret it as 1900 rather than 2000, substitute another year or simply freeze. A company could suffer financial loss, for example, if its computers broke down and it lost its payroll and billing records.
These are the main aspects of the legislation:
*Companies with fewer than 50 employees could not be assessed more than $250,000 in punitive damages.
*All companies would be given 90 days to fix a problem from the time they were notified before they could be sued.
*No more than 100 plaintiffs could be involved in a class-action suit, and no more than $10 million in damages could be sought.
*Class-action suits would be diverted from state courts to Federal courts, making them more expensive and burdensome for plaintiffs.
*All companies responsible for a computer failure would share the legal liability, and large, wealthy companies could not be forced to pay more than a portion of the damages caused by less affluent or bankrupt businesses.
The central purpose of the measure is to prevent frivolous lawsuits. But critics said there were insufficient protections for consumers who suffered financial loss because of computer failures.
A White House spokesman said President Clinton insisted that the measure be confined to Year 2000 computer problems and did not involve general tort reform.
In addition, the spokesman said small changes involving class-action suits and the proportional liability provisions convinced the President that the bill was worthwhile.
Senator Christopher J. Dodd, Democrat of Connecticut, was instrumental in working out the deal. Insurance companies in Connecticut are especially interested in limiting lawsuits.
Business interests were gleeful. Thomas J. Donohue, president of the United States Chamber of Commerce, which made the legislation its top lobbying priority of the year, called it "a watershed achievement for business and the American economy."
But groups representing consumers were critical. When computer problems develop, "the public is going to be very angry, and small businesses are going to be the most affected," said Joan Claybrook, president of Public Citizen.
Complicating the work on the bill all year has been the desire of both parties to win the political and financial support of wealthy individuals and companies in the computer industry.
A Republican strategist in Congress said his party was not willing to give much in the negotiations because the President was over a barrel: If he vetoed the bill, it would have hurt Vice President Al Gore specifically and Democrats generally in their quest for support in Silicon Valley. By accepting the agreement, in this view, the President gave Republicans what they wanted.
A Democratic strategist added another element. He said Vice President Gore, as he begins his campaign for the Presidency, was eager to rid himself of the taint of financial support from trial lawyers.
The conflict between trial lawyers, who are among Democrats' biggest campaign contributors, and business interests, which are the Republicans' biggest donors, has been a thread running through legislative battles for the last several years. Among other issues, it has arisen on product liability legislation, antismoking measures and bills to regulate managed-care health plans. The Democratic strategist said that for Mr. Gore, the money from the trial lawyers was probably not worth the criticism he faced for taking funds from an unpopular source. But that may not be the case, the strategist said, for Democrats in Congress.
In a careful statement tonight, Mark S. Mandell, president of the Association of Trial Lawyers of America, said, "It is positive that this unique response to a unique situation will be law for only three years and that the legal rights of anyone who suffers a physical injury are preserved."

CORRECTION-DATE: July 2, 1999, Friday

 CORRECTION:
A front-page article on Wednesday about legislation to limit lawsuits arising from Year 2000 computer failures misstated a provision dealing with class-action suits. The agreement between the White House and Republicans in Congress would require that suits involving more than 100 plaintiffs or seeking more than $10 million in damages be filed in Federal court rather than state courts. The agreement does not prohibit such suits altogether.
